The RED PAGE
MAURICE HEWLETT
ROM mediaeval romance—from the
1 Madonna of the Peach Tree, the
Fools Errant and the Fond Adven-
turers of Italy and of the Forest, from his-
torical interpretations of Mary, Queen of
Scots, and Richard Yea-and-Nay, Maurice
Hewlett has turned to give us the story of
the aristocratic lady of London who loved a
butcher a mere hundred years ago. A real
butcher, tradesmen’s entrance, horse, and
apron, and cleaver. The idea is audacious ;
but Hewlett—our later Boccaccio—is always
audacious ; and his love affair of the hero
who hutches is done in the best Hewlett
manner—in a deft and perfect style, with an
iridescent humor, a sense of irresponsible
youth, and, over all, a glow of romance.
For Hewlett shows no signs of becoming
less Hewlettish : he can always be depended
upon for delectable entertainment. His
stories speed the reader on almost raptu-
rously through a tangle of humorous romance,
and his characters are no literary puppets
with thin ink in their veins. The lushness of
the sentiment is always kept in check by a
philosophic wit.
The Stooping Lady (Macmillan) begins
with more than a promise of George Mere-
dith. There is the same alleged and almost
convincing reliance upon historical docu-
ment, the same air of the mere scrupulous
recorder, as in the greatest of all Dianas;
but Hewlett, unlike Meredith, does not
parade his cleverness in chapters that are
designed—from the aloofness and disdain of
a mighty intellect—to be deliberately dull.
Hewlett is left little time for moralising;
his maids and lovers are too impatient, too
impetuous, too much alive. And here,
with the same abandon as in his mediteval
romances, he plunges you into an assem-
blage of lords and princes, of politics and
oaths and aristocracy and drunkenness, with
the Prince of Wales the inert centre of the
seething factions. Here, incidentally, is a
pretty picture of that personage :
It was eleven clock when the Prince came —in
semi-state. Six horses drew him instead of
twelve, and it is possible that there was rather
less hooting because there were rather fewer
sightseers ; but there was enough to render very
necessary the services of a brass band, which, for
drowning purposes, had been ambushed in the
court. When he entered the hall he was flushed
and noisy, but not tipsy...
So seriously she looked at her advancing prince,
you might have thought her his judge ; so scorn-
fully curved her lip you must have known it.
What to her was the advance and boisterous cor-
diality of this overflowing personage, this young
Silenus of creased eye-sockets, and of the color of
pink paint? Was she to curtsey, kiss the hand of
this be-ribboned, fuddled, bloated'man of forty—
and her heart, her will, and soul out there in the
cedared garden, quivering under the calm eyes of
a god ?
Which brings us to the butcher ! He is
quite a remarkable and quite a respectable
butcher ; though, to be sure, in his lady’s
eyes,
David Vernour shone through his blue frock
like Apollo through his shepherd-skins—what
time he was keeping herds for Admetus, serving
his term in proud humility with that Thessalian
king.
But his most useful quality—for a hero—
is his reserve. He knows enough to keep
out of the way—and let his lady love him the
more. He has not the greatness of
Meredith’s magnificent tailor—the tailor
who was also a gentleman ; but he has a
proper pride in his cleaver and apron.
“ My lord.” Vernour said, “ you must fight me
for that.”
“I fight with gentlemen, sir. Not with
butchers.”
‘‘Don’t let my trade stand in your way, my
lord. It should not, for it was your own.”
His lordship started. ‘‘You are impudent, my
man, as well as a rogue, I see.”
“ Sir,” said Vernour, ‘‘you were a soldier, I’ve
been told. What difference there may be between
us seems to be to my credit. I made sheep bleed,
you made men bleed. I carved joints, you carved
limbs. You butchered your own kind ; you slew
men. I made men live. Now, my lord ? ”
“Damn him! he’s right there,” said Lord
Rodono to himself. “"Vernour, I’ll fight you, if
you please.”
But with the butcher so tied to his busi-
ness, it might have been expected that the
centre of the stage would be filled by the
Stooping Lady: But her rightful place is
usurped by a lady who does not stoop—Lady
Morfa, her grandmother, the ancient head
of the family, a grimly picturesque figure
whose pride and will furnish the motifs of
the plot.
She divided mankind, for all purposes, into two
classes. Either you were family, or you were a
person. Collectively, you were still Family,
unless you were the mob.
Lady Morfa was a Whig who “took kings
into favor, not because they were estimable,
but because without them the families could
not govern,” and of prayer she remarked
that no doubt it was—“ ‘Very right and
proper ’ —but she didn’t like it. Any sugges-
tion that applications should be addressed
elsewhere than to herself offended her a
good deal.”
So, through this turmoil of politics and
butchering, Hewlett carries you unresist-
ingly along upon the rush of his preposterous
plot, till, just when you are beginning feebly
to be ashamed of your surrender, he lightly
rescues you from the flood, and dismisses
the tale almost with a philosophic laugh. In
the character of Captain Ranald the romancer
steps forward from the dropped curtain, and,
as chorus, with a light and disconcerting
frankness, sums up :
Ranald shook his head. “I’m not romantic,
but I can see the vestal in her. God bless you,
why do you suppose she tumbled into that young
man’s arms ? ”
“Fine fellow, you tell me; fine figure of a
young man. Had parts—spoke well—could think
—is that what you mean ? ”
Ranald snapped his fingers. “Pooh, sir;
nothing of the sort! His greatest chance with
her was that she knew nothing about him. It was
all gossamer-web of her own spinning. The busi-
ness was done on that visit of hers to Brook-street,
to apologise. Well, she was in a great fright, and
no wonder. He got the benefit of that—don’t you
see ? She was there to sing small, and she became
small; the smaller she, the greater he. As she
stooped, he towered up, higher and higher. She
projected him as a god, and a god he remained to
the end of the chapter. All a generous figment of
her brain. . . .
“Mind you, I knew Vernour well, and
admired him.... No; he struck her imagination,
and she chose—as queens used to choose. As for
him, damn it, he was a male.” ...
“ Do you mean that the eternal male ?”
“ I do. He was no more than that essentially—•
splendid brawn.”
A delightful story, which we may place in
the lowest division of the class reserved for
the best—Fl. For it is merely romance—
and the pride of place is reserved for greater
things than romance—for Tess and Lord Jim
and Diana and Vanity Fair ....
BERNARD O'DOWD.
Ego and Universe entwine;
Thoughts that a wide flight urge
With course centripetal incline
On Ego to converge.
9
Since Blue to me to all is Blue,
Save to the Color-Blind,
Pervadingly then must be True
What thinks my Unit-mind.
It thinks of Protozoic Slime,
Divergency of Race,
The Continuity of Time,
The Trimetry of Space.
The Brain's associative tracts
Link tricklets on the pane
With Kosciusco-cataracts,
Mud-puddles ivith the Main.
Atoms and Suns cohesive spin,
And High with Low is met ;
There lurks some Shakespeare-germ within
The Penny Novelette.
Age with its obsolescent Sense
Will be metempsychosed
To Infancy of influence
Benign as Buttered Toast.
Prenatal Consciousness will teach
Vision to infant Eyes,
Psychotic Mimicry bring Speech
Where Fact may crystallise.
Thought grasps, conversely, That Which Is
Behind its wordy Shroud ;
But can it through Periphrasis
Prehend Bernard O’Dowd ?
N.S.W.
CHARLES MACLEAN.
SHE TO HIM
Is it the persistent feminine instinct for imita-
tion, or woman’s inability to express her own
inmost feelings, that makes most women writers
of love-verse take the mere man’s point of view?
Women write charmingly of their love for
children ; they seem unable or afraid to write of
their love for men. In writing love-verses nine
out of ten women put themselves in the man’s
place, and tell us of her charms, not his. And
surely he has charms. With a brutal desire to
learn why a woman ever loves a man, the Red
Page offers a guinea for the best poem, written by
a woman, to or of a man, ideal or actual, that she
loves. Verses must be received by this office by
January 9th, 1908, and the envelope should be
marked “ Him ” and addressed to this page.
In the recent successful show of the Sydney
Society of Artists at Melbourne, Norman Lindsay
sold black-and-white work of the value of £l5O.
The extent of the (Melbourne) appreciation of the
best black-and-white artist in Australia can be
gauged by the fact that before his work the largest
price paid by the Melbourne Gallery for black-
and-white—a drawing by Yierge—was £4O. None
of Lindsay’s drawings were sold for less than £lO,
and many for £l5. v
The Nyngan Observer prints Clancy of the Over-
flow, without any signature, and remarks, “ The
following vigorous and atmospheric poem appeared
originally, unsigned, in an Australian newspaper.”
Fame !
.4 BOOK OF THE MAORI
MUCH praise must be given to
Johannes C. Anderson for his heroic
efforts to make the Maori of myth
and history live again in the pages of
a book, and much sympathy must be
extended to that author for his heroic,
but inevitable, failure. In Maori Life in
Ao-tea (printed by Whitcombe and Tombs,
M.L., with the unrivalled expertness in
photographic reproductions common to
Maoriland publishers) Anderson has at-
tempted an impossible thing; he has tried
to combine a thesaurus of the Maori
with a narration of Maori epics and
legends; and in his care for accurate
detail he has missed the poetry that dis-
tinguishes these myths. In a foreword, A.
T. Ngata, an educated Maori barrister and
LL.B., hits the nail with his remark,
“ Indeed the reader may complain that the
conscientous attention to detail has here
and there impaired the vividness of the pour-
trayal.” It is true that Anderson has no
illusions about the success of his work, and
in his preface he points out some of the
difficulties of his task. His aim was to place
us in communication with the Maori people
in their every-day life; enabling us to see
their customs, to hear their sayings, to love
their land, to experience the terrors of their
omens, to be influenced by their mana,
wonder-tilled by their legends and folk-lore.
The rough material for this attempt having been
first gathered, it was necessary that it should be
set out in a manner not altogether European, yet
in a manner for which European taste would not
be averse. The coloring and atmosphere must be
different: for lions, roses, gold, gems and other
old-world poetic accessories being unknown to our
dusky people of Ao-tea, all reference to these
must be avoided : no man could be lion-hearted;
he must fight bravely like a shark: no maiden
might have rosy lips, for that would involve a
double error; roses were unknown, and lips
darkened with tattoo were preferred : golden sun-
sets and silver tides might not be seen in this
wonderland : though the Maori orator—and every
man was an orator —had his similes, his meta-
phors, many beautiful, many involved as those of
the Scandinavian skalds.
It was a brave ideal, but Anderson has
choked his path to his goal with bristling
weeds of detail and meticulous literalism.
The book is dull. And even in his rendition
of the great legends of the Maori,- which so
strikingly parallel the legends of the Greeks,
he has not improved upon Hori Grey,
Domett, or the greatest of these, John
White. Anderson specifically states his
intention :
The new world of Maori myth and tradition
and story has, as far as art and story are con-
cerned, been practically untouched. A time is
looked for, and this work is an attempt to hasten
its coming, when the world’s art will be enriched
with scenes from Maori legend ; and he who
would break away from old-world fetters will find
in this land a mine of wealth almost, if not quite,
equal to that afforded by Greek or Scandinavian
mythology. The dusky skin has been urged as
an objection to artistic treatment; but if marble
is unsuitable, is there not bronze? Many of the
scenes and tales scattered through this book will
be found specially written with a view to furnish-
ing subjects for painters and sculptors; poets,
too, who revel in good things. The first intention
was to make an epic of the whole subject; it was
found that there was material for twenty epics,
and the poem was therefore laid aside until all
had been gathered. This quarrying will supply
the artists of Maoriland with ample rough
material ; the references will indicate where still
more will be found ; let them but turn their
dazzled eyes from the w’hiteness of the Arcadian
nymphs and goddesses ; here are other sources of
inspiration as powerful ; anon we shall have a
new blossoming forth ; a pourtrayal, not of the
sunny voluptuousness of the Mediterranean, but
of the weird, if at times lurid, strangeness of the
Antipodean.
Anderson has lamentably failed in his effort.
A comparison of, for instance, the great
legend pf Puhi-huia, as rendered in Maori
Life in Ao-tea, and the same legend as get
forth in White’s Ancient History of the Maori
—several volumes of which monumental
work are now unfortunately out of print-
will disclose the fact that White has brought
out all the poetic significance of that love
story, while Anderson has drained it of much
of its suggestive beauty. Anderson has but
worked over, with infinite care and erudition,
the old quarry left by White. But White
had an instinct for the precious metal ; he
found all the nuggets. Anderson has but
picked out of the tailings a few inconsider-
able specks, and he has tarnished the
nuggets. So, despite the prolixity of both
works, the poets and artists of Maoriland
must still go to White for their material.
£52?
Yet in his narrative of the daily life of
the Maori of old time, Anderson has done
good and careful work. His book will
remain a comprehensive repository, put in
popular form, of the details of that life,
their folk-lore, their karakia, their religion.
Here, for reference, the worker at this new
mine of poetry and art may happily look ;
the book is a dictionary of the Maori, and
therefore useful. The work was well worth
doing. As an example of the matter —the
style is sometimes slovenly and dull—we
may quote this striking incident of the
Maori belief in omens :
“ It was here that Tara-iti saw, lying across his
path, what appeared to be the body of a man
newly dead. It was seen by none of us, nor as we
approached could we discern that which in his
eyes lay directly before us. We therefore knew
that it was a kehua of Tara-iti. He left that path
and turned into another. There, too, the body
lay extended before him, its face turned to the
ground. Again he tried another path, and again
the kehua appeared, its face now uppermost, when
Tara-iti saw that the features were his own. His
very moko, he declared, and the scar over his cheek-
bone, were reproduced in the face of the appari-
tion. It was an omen of death should he continue
on his way, and* Tara-iti at once determined to
return, obeying the warning. But now, to his
consternation and terror, the kehua lay on his
homeward path ; and turn which way he would,
it lay before him after the first few strides. Fear
and perplexity were also in our hearts ; but as
Tara-iti only saw it, for him alone was the omen
designed. Then Maru-wehi interpreted this
thing; we must proceed, and Tara-iti remain
where he then was until the kehua no longer
appeared, when he must hasten back to the pa.
There, then, going on our way, we left him
trembling ; not without misgivings, and many
backward thoughts. To you, though I see him
not, he has no doubt lfmg since returned, and told
you what I have now related.”
A wail interrupted the speaker, for all now
knew that Tara-iti must still be in that place,
surrounded by the one kehua ; and since so long a
time had passed, he must already have accom-
panied that kehucb to the land of shadows.
“Did he not return ? ” cried Pua-ruroa, inter-
preting the wail and the silence.
_ “ Alas, no,” sighed the others; and the cries of
his wife and sisters filled them all with sorrow.
The parallelism between the myths of the
Maori and those of the ancient world are
obvious in the Maori legend of Hinemoa and
the Greek story of Hero and Leander, with
the curious transformation that in Maori
legend it is the woman that swims the
Hellespont, the man who waits—and plays
the flute. In Maori myth, indeed, it is
nearly always the woman who chooses her
lover, the woman who acts. Here, for
instance, is the splendid pleading of Puhi-
huia in defence of love : a thought indicated
in Shakespeare’s
If all were minded so, the times should cease,
And threescore years would make the world away.
Thus Puhi-huia voices the Greek plaint of
mankind against Pate :
“ But was the evil mine ? Did I determine that
Tiki should be a man, or that Arohi-rohi should
be a woman ? No; it was the act of the gods.
Those gods are your progenitors ; and if 1 now
follow in the footsteps of Arohi-rohi, do I commit
wrong ? She took the man of her choice, and
from her yon are sprung, from her you receive
your sacredness. If I have done wrong, she did
wrong also; yet had she remained unwed, you
were none of you here; not one of you would
have seen the light of this world. Or you, having
been born, had none of you taken wives or hus-
bands, then I had never been born. But having
been born, am I guilty of the evil ? Is it wrong
that I follow in your steps, taking as husband the
man that I love ? ”
In a treatise upon what, in the not too distant
future, will possibly be the language of a great
portion of our continent, Walter Hillier remarks
that, in the British Consular Service in China,
every man must spend two years of constant study
of the Chinese language in Peking, and yet, so
difficult is the language that, “ at the end of this
two years’ course, there is not one of them who
could personally conduct a correspondence in
Chinese, translate an official document without
the aid of a dictionary, or speak with sufficient
fluency to act as an interpreter where important
negotiations were concerned. Before they can
reach this standard of proficiency, they have
at least five or six years before them.”
The reason is that there are forty thousand
words in the Chinese language, and each charac-
ter is pronounced as a syllable, and there are only
four hundred syllables. Thus each syllable must
represent a vast array of meanings. Consequently
variations of the same sounds are effected by their
pronunciation in different tones or inflexions of
the voice. The word chi, pronounced in the
even tone, means “a chicken,” in the rising
tone “impatient,” in the falling tone “to
shove,” in the abrupt tone, “to take note of.”
So if you order a chicken you may get a shove.
Once a deputation of native gentlemen waited on
the Governor of Hong Kong, and in their address
spoke of England as kwei kwo, or “ your honorable
country.” The interpreter, who was a well-known
scholar, thought the deputation had pronounced
the kwei in the third tone, when the phrase would
mean “your devil country.” Being naturally
angry at the application of this term to his native
land, he indignantly drove the deputation from
the Governor’s presence ; and it was not until the
correctness of the tone had been explained that
the deputation was readmitted. All this seems
an additional argument for a White Australia.

A
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Villanelle of Thursday Island.
“Sayonara, ” she sighed, and she flirted her fan,
As down by her lattice I sped to the shore.
The fair little, frail little Jade of Japan.
For the clank of the incoming cable began,
And the flaunting blue peter flew free atdbe fore;
“Sayonara,” she sighed, and she flirtecbher fan.
But time fled too q.uickly a campaign to.plan,
And chance there was none to abjure or adore
The fair little, frail little Jade of Japan.
So I blew just a Parthian kiss, as I ran,
To the gay little figure that bobbed in the door.
“ Sayonara,” she sighed, and she flirted her fan.
Though it shock you to say so, dear prim Puritan,
Once again I would see—as I saw her of yore—
The fair little, frail little Jade of Japan.
And hear her cry, faint as the fluting of Pan,
(But a note that could thrill to the innermost
core)—
“ Sayonara,” she sighed, and she flirted her fan—
The fair little, frail little Jade of Japan.
QUEENSLAND.
MACHETI.
Sing Me a Song.
Sing me a- song with the beat of the surf in it,
Roar of great combers borne down the wet breeze ;
Let there be thunder of hoofs on green turf in it,
Spurning the jniles in their rhythmical ease.
Sing me a song with a fierce note of strife in it,
Gleam of bright barrels, and stern steadfast eyes ;
Sing me a song with the sunshine of life in it,
Sing of the blue of God’s canopied skies.
Sing me a song of some great open space I love,
Whispering myalls on far stretching plains ;
There with my cheek pressed against the dear face
I love,
Bridles made music? with loose-hanging reins.
Sing the old garden >vith dim-scented fastnesses.
Gleam of white raiment that came o’er the lawn ;
Sing of the stars that grew pale in their vastnesses
As I rode happily home in the dawn.
Sing me a song with the fjlad mote of youth in it,
Days of past conquests by' field or by flood ;
Sing me a song with both honor and truth in it,
Sing of the courage that fires the young blood.
Sing of the true friends that .share of our sorrow
too,
Though bitter anguish their own hearts-may wring;
Friends of to-day that are friends of to-morrow
too — \
These are the songs I am longing tossing.
N.S.W.
HORACE HALLORAN.
My Typiste.
I’m an awfully sensitive man.
And I move in exclusive society,
Moulding all my affairs on a plan
Based on goodness and perfect propriety..
I have not got a blot on my fame
(Or I had not until very recently);
Yet ray once stainless name—
I confess it with shame —
Has been dragged through the mire quite! ‘ in-
decently.
I’ve a typiste. A beautiful girl. |
With such cheeks! Ah! How peachy ?and
pink they are!
Her teeth are like mother-o’-pearl,
And like nectar her lips (or I think they; are).
She’s the kind of girl all men admire,.
For she’s perfect in figure and feature ;
At my special desire
She sings in the choir,
And in my Sabbath-school she’s a teacher.
If there’s aught should be free from the liar,.
And from slander be quite independent,
’Tis a maiden who sings.in the choir,
And a Sabbath-school Superintendent.
Alas ! Mud to the whitest robe clings,
Leaving stains on its natural purity.
And foul slander grief brings
To the sweetest of things,
Where—Oh ! where—is our moral security ?
I admit if I work back at night
That I can’t let her go without missing her;
But I strongly deny he was right
When the caretaker charged me with kissing her.
He’s a fraud ! When I gave him a crown,
He admitted his eyes had deceived him,
Yet he went round the town,
As he knocked the crown down,
Telling stories. And people believed him.
So remorseless is Rumor’s long tongue,
And so prone is mankind to malignity,
That my friends, both the old and the young,
Seem to treat me with studied indignity.
Why, the office boy grins when I speak,
Quite ignoring our social disparity.
While my clerk, once so meek,
Sticks his tongue in his cheek,
Scarcely trying to hide his hilarity.
Though again and again I’ve declared
That the stories are wicked inventions,
And the sweetest of things is prepared
To corroborate all my contentions,
Yet the people all credit the liar
And, his lie being in the ascendant,
The effect is most dire
To a girl in the choir
And a Sabbath-school Superintendent.
N.S.W.
THOS. E. SPENCER.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin posted
regularly to you every t week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager, 'l'o send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special "Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor ivill carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all coiitri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
twc columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin ivill return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. ivill be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To A mntotirs in. Made and White •
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or ivash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
ivill be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 5, 1907.
THE CHELMSFORD GOVERNMENT IN
QUEENSLAND.
Every fresh point of consideration gives a more
sinister complexion to the revolutionary situation
in Queensland. The usurpation by Lord Chelms-
ford of despotic power over Parliament, in order
to maintain the Tory party in office, the more it
is examined, the more clearly it takes on the
aspect of a deliberate conspiracy against the self-
governing rights of the State. (Of course Lord
Chelmsford is the victim rather than the
leader in the matter.) The Tory party for
very many years held office in Queensland,
for so many years that the Government which
represented its interests came to be known as the
“Continuous Government.” Its personnel changed
frequently, owing to its system of absorbing in its
own corruption all Opposition talent that came to
the front. Its policy was always that of retro-
gression ; its administration always muddled and
ineffective. The country groaned, but had to
suffer in patience. There was no alternative to
the Continuous Government except a Labor
Government; and for some time the Labor party
in Queensland was associated with the wildest
extravagance of policy. But sweet reasonable-
ness began to modify the policy of that
party, and finally relief came when Mr.
Morgan decided to step down from the
Speaker’s chair and lead the Progressives
ni the Assembly, and with Mr. Kidston, of the
Labor party, a man of great ability and con-
spicuous moderation, formed a Government. It
was thought then, by observers of Australian
politics, that with the end of the Continuous
Government the Tory party had gone out of
office for ever. The correctness of this forecast was
being proved with each succeeding month until
the crisis of November brought the Tories
back to office, not by Parliamentary vote,
but by despotic exercise of royal power.
Now, what makes this development so
sinister is that the present affords the only
shadow of an opportunity for the Tories to snatch
a dishonest victory at the polls. A singularly
faulty Electoral Act is in operation. It gives great
opportunities for manipulation at the ballot-box
the “ postal vote” especially. It is now or never
with the Tories. They have seized the best of
opportunities to put their fortune to the test, and
Lord Chelmsford has been the instrument of
their designs.
* * * *
Still, it is not in the least degree likely that
a Tory electoral victory will follow the daring
revolution by which the Crown in Queensland
has become the partisan of a party. Even
were Queensland indifferent to Mr. Kidston
and the Labor party, yet democratic principles
are so firmly established in Australia that
anything would be preferred to a Chelms-
ford Administration. For that is practically the
position. It is Lord Chelmsford who goes to
the country asking warrant for a lawless usurpa-
tion of Parliamentary authority, seeking from the
people approval of a system by which their
Government would be chosen in future, not by
their representatives in the Legislature, but
by the Governor. It is unthinkable that any
Australian electorate, however Tory in spirit,
would deliberately endorse that appeal and thus go
back on the path towards a King-tyranny which
our forefathers freed themselves from with much
sacrifice. But there is always a danger that in
some quarters the issue will be confused. It is
satisfactory to note, therefore, that the Labor
party has decided to come to some electoral
arrangement with the Kidston party, so that
there will be no vote-splitting through two demo-
crats running against one despotist. The issue is
a great one, the greatest that has arisen in Aus-
tralian politics for many years, and all Australians
wish to see Queensland vindicate as decisively as
possible the self-governing rights of the people.
* * * *
What the electors should keep in mind is that
the attitude of Lord Chelmsford lias not been a
mere stretching of the royal prerogative, but an
absolute usurpation of power. When Mr.
Kidston advised him to appoint new members to
the Upper House, Lord Chelmsford might have
insisted on an appeal to the voters as a preliminary
step. That would have been wrong, according
to the precedents laid down by the Colonial
office, but it would have had the color of
legality. In sending for Mr. Philp on the resigna-
tion of Mr. Kidston, Lord Chelmsford was
undoubtedly right. But when an adverse vote
against Mr. Philp was carried as soon as he met
the House, Lord Chelmsford should then have
dismissed him, and he might then, sending for
Mr. Kidston, have insisted on a dissolution, and
have had some shadow of a claim to be within his
prerogative. But in keeping Mr. Philp in office
in spite of several hostile votes of the House, and
in determining to carry on the business of the
country without Supply, Lord Chelmsford
came clean out of the arena of constitutional discus-
sion. He took up the attitude of a Tudor or
a Stuart dismissing the Commons and carrying
on by illegal money levies. A robust Democratic
party will be needed in the new House to take up
a firm stand in the matter of those levies. Every
penny now taken from the Queensland Treasury
not covered by past Supply is appropriated
illegally. Constitutionally it is stolen. Unless
Parliament subsequently passes an Act of Indemni-
fication, every official responsible for paying; out a
shilling without the warrant of Parliament is per-
sonally and criminally liable. The Bulletin
would like to see now a steadily-formed and
sturdily-announced purpose on the part of the
Democrats that no such Bill of Indemnification
will be proposed. The Tories have usurped
power by a revolutionary move. Let it be made
clear to them now that they will have to pay for
their usurpation.
THE NEW PROTECTION.
It was not to be expected that so radical a de-
parture in the field of practical economics as that
which has been called the “New Protection”
could be made without evoking some protests,
without causing many alarms. Every reform has
to combat the forces of inertia, which withstand
change simply because it is change. The
Bulletin, which was originally responsible for
the idea of the “New Protection,” has not
the least doubt but that it will be ulti-
mately accepted and acclaimed by all reason-
able employers as well as employees. In
the meanwhile it must face and answer criticisms.
The root idea of the New Protection is that the
tariff is a national, not merely an industrial affair;
that it represents the chief arm by which the
State can secure the happiness of the people. The
New Protection goes further than attempting to
make industries economically possible ; it seeks to
enforce conditions on those industries which will
make them of the greatest possible value to the
nation, recognising all the while that the best
asset of a nation is its manhood. To the
New Protection, the foundation of a sweated-labor
industry is useless, except, perhaps, as a step
towards its quick development into a high-wage
industry ; and the chief product aimed at is not
goods but citizens. Once that is thoroughly
understood, a good deal of the criticism now
aimed at the principle will be recognised as either
mistaken or useless. The fair manufacturer, who
aims at having prosperous workers in a
prosperous factory, will see that there
is no hardship in asking him to pj/ good
wages whilst he is amply protected against
the competition of all cheap-labor employers. The
grasping capitalist, who sees in Protection a
chance to garner in illegitimate profits by employ-
ing cheap labor, and selling at dear-labor prices,
will recognise that his ideal is hopeless in Aus-
tralia.
* * * *
The deputation from the Associated Chambers
of Manufacturers, which waited on Minister for
Customs Chapman to present resolutions pro-
testing against the New Protection, last
week, was encouragingly half-hearted. Mr.
Beale disavowed personal sympathy with
the protest. It represented not his views, but
those of a majority of the Chambers. Mr. Joshua
was careful to say that the New Protection wages
fixed in his industry were fair, and had been loyally
paid. The deputation indeed seemed to have
come not so much to protest as to be reassured.
One solid grievance Mr. Joshua brought forward.
In the agricultural instruments industry, two
different wages awards have been made under
the New Protection system—one for South Aus-
tralia, the other for Victoria. The result is
that Victorian manufacturers are called upon to
pay higher wages than the South Australians.
That should be stopped. The essence of the
New Protection system is that a fair minimum
wage should be enforced equally all over Aus-
tralia (with possibly special increments for places
where the conditions of life are unduly hard).
Then the fair-wage manufacturer of one State-
cannot be under-sold by the low-wage manu-
facturer of another State; the low-wage foreign
manufacturer can be kept the other side
of the tariff wall. In the agricultural imple-
ments industry a just wage should be finally deter-
mined and then enforced in all the States without
distinction.
* * * *
Until the Federation can get full control of the
subject of industrial regulation, there will be some
difficulties in enforcing the New Protection by
the Excise expedient which has been adopted.
Those difficulties represent the price statesman-
ship has to pay for good intentions. I hey are
unavoidable, and must be surmounteQ. J.ney
constitute no reason at all for hesitancy in carrying
out the new policy. The Australian democracy
is resolved that the essential basis of every indus-
try is the citizen. The increase of well-paid
citizens able to marry and keep comfortable homes
is not a side issue, but the actual aim of the New
Protection. The manufacturers who are most
prompt to recognise that will be the first to get
their undertakings on a solid and settled basis.
The manufacturers who cannot see things from
that point of view may as well decide now to make
way for other and more reasonable men. Aus-
tralia has no room for the high-tariff and low-wage
capitalist.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The High Commissioner.
It is evident that this session of the Australian
Parliament will close without any definite action
being taken for the appointment of a High Com-
missioner for the Commonwealth. If at the very
inauguration of the Federation the first Govern-
ment had taken the view that the necessity of having
representation of Australian interests in London
was so pressing as to warrant it in anticipating
Parliamentary authority for the expense, and
appointing a High Commissioner by executive
warrant, there would have been no possibility of
reasonable objection. A nation does not really
take form and being until its authority is repre-
sented at those chancelleries abroad where its
interests are concerned. The Australian nation,
being under a suzerain power which has full con-
trol over its foreign affairs, has necessity for only
one diplomatic agent outside its boundaries, but
the necessity for that one is most exigent. It
would not be possible to calculate how much
Australia has already lost from the lack of
adequate representation in London. In the
matter of domestic policy alone the evil work of
the “Stinking Fish” Party would probably have
been checked at the outset by a capable
High Commissioner, who could have pointed out
that, so far from Australia’s Immigration Restric-
tion policy showing a petulant disregard for
Imperial interests, it was, as a matter of fact,
suggested, almost dictated, by a masterful British
Minister, Joseph Chamberlain ; and who could
have exposed the lie that British workers of good
character were not wanted here, and thus have
diverted a stream of immigration to these shores.
In foreign policy it is perhaps too much to hope
that the weight of Australian warning would
have been sufficient to prevent Great Britain
from entering into thedangerous Japanese alliance.
But an Australian representative might at the
outset have suggested doubts and difficulties
which since have been justified with experience,
and have thus at least won for his country a repu-
tation for diplomatic prescience which would
have been useful in future negotiations. In
smaller matters of foreign policy the Australian
High Commissioner would have had scope for
many triumphs. He might have stopped the
bitterly cruel and unjust sacrifice of Aus-
tralian interests in the matter of the Bush-
veldt Carbineers (S’Africa). He might have
secured some attention to Australian interests in
the various questions regarding Oceania which have
been arising lately. All this is of the obvious
work that one can see might have been done. In
addition, there would have been, it is fair to pre-
sume, many hundreds of opportunities which a
keen and intelligent representative of Australia
might have seized to advance the interests of the
Commonwealth. But all have been sacrificed to
the sordid political intrigues which made it “ bad
policy” to appoint a suitable man because of
the offence that would have given to unsuitable
aspirants. One or two persons have attempted
to do something to fill the pressing need
with what poor advocacy cf Australia was possible
without official prestige or warrant. B. It. Wise
for some years now has been a sort of private
High Commissioner in London, never allowing a
slander to go unchallenged, or an opportunity to
put things in the right light to slip by. But his
was, after all, only a private voice, and it cried in
a wilderness. N.S. W. Agent-General Cogiilan, in
spite of the fact that he represented a State Govern-
ment which is conspicuous in slandering Australia,
has managed to put in a good and wise word
for the Commonwealth. Ex-Senator Matheson
has also been known in advocacy of Australia.
But nothing could compensate for the want of
official representation. In every case affecting
Australian interests in London, judgment has
been allowed to go by default. And that policy,
seemingly, is to continue indefinitely, until the
politicians can settle on what appointment will
best suit party interests.
The Miserable Kum of Sydney!
The famous Lamentation "> of Jeremiah are cheer-
ful reading compared to tee daily foreign-tra,de
papers’ account of how the Ma State and City
have gone to the dogs under the combined in-
fluence of Federation and the tariff. Get on
board a tram-car, ride through the streets of
Sydney town, and test the statement. From the
Central Railway Station one is whirled into
Castlereagh-street, which, at the dawn of Federa-
tion, and for some while afterwards, was a
collection of filthy slums, an Alsatia given over
to the scum of the city, where the pavement
woman and the night thief mingled with the
Chinese, and the black and tan of all climates.
Everywhere now one sees (demolished hovels being
carted away, and preparations made for
the building of fine new buildings. The
firm of S. T. Leigh and Cos. is erecting a
factory which will accommodate hundreds of
hands. Mark For is rearing a huge shop which
will be a landmark, and occupy the best part of a
block. All along the way fresh new buildings
of brick and stone are replacing dens of vice and
cheap doss houses. Or let the man who left
Sydney just before Federation take a run down
George-street, and consider some of the
palatial places that have been erected during
his absence. He will see two extra stories
on David Jones’s emporium, he will note with a
backward glance the fine Bank of Australasia, he
will see the beautiful Challis House erected for
the University in Martin Place approaching com-
pletion. Round the corner of a street, as he
whizzes past, looms the huge red brick pile of
Henry Bull and Cos., eight stories high ; and the
brown, gigantic outline of John Sands’ new
factory vgill make him exclaim in admiration.
Further on he will see that the firm of Hordern
Bros., which stayed in Pitt-street all the
years before Federation, has broken right through
to George-street; and further on Farmer and Cos.
have done likewise. Away down Brickfield-hill
he will see the vastest pile of them all, Anthony
Hordern and Sons’ Palace Emporium; and
beyond it the steeple-like building of Marcus
Clark and Cos.; while beyond that the, sun
shines on the great dome of Grace Brothers’ new
store. These are only a few of the larger and
more elaborate of the new buildings—there are
scores of others too numerous to mention,
such as that pile half-a-block long in Hunter-
street ; Dalgety’s new building in Bent-street;
the new palace, where the Colonial Sugar Cos.
now carries on its business. And people do
not sink hundreds of thousands of pounds in
buildings in a city that is on the decline. Roughly,
according to the Master Builders’ Association, 11
new buildings are started in Sydney every day,
and for the year 1906 the number was 4088, which
when completed were assessed by the Water and
Sewerage Board at an annual rent value of £180,646.
The Water and Sewerage Board reckons that the
rental value is 6 per cent, on the value of land and
buildings, so the value of the buildings erected in
1906, and the land on which they were built, is
£3,010,766 ; and allowing that the value of build-
ings is only half the total (two-thirds would be
fairer estimate), it means that during the year
just ended over one-and-a-half-million pounds of
new buildings were erected in the ruined city.
It’s a curious sort of ruin.
The Advantage of Going Often to the
Pawnshop.
The Owe’Sullivan is incorrigible. He asked an
audience last week to consider what a magnificent
stroke of business it is to borrow money at per
cent, and spend it on railways and tramways which
give a return of 6 per cent. Well, for a start,
where are those 6 per cent, railways and tram-
ways? N. S. Wales railways never had such a
year as 1906-7; yet the return was under 5 per
cent. In Tasmania the return was under 2 per
cent.; in Maoriland, under 3g per cent. As for
trams, the Manly line, which was one of the
Owe’Sullivan works, has never paid one farthing
of interest—outside the city lines, very few of his
trams have. In 1902-3, which was one of the Owe’-
Sullivan years, all that the public undertakings
of N. S. Wales had left over after paying working
expenses was £1,509,752. N. S. Wales then owed
Cohen £71,592,485. The actual return was, there-
there, £2 2s. 2d. per cent.—which is only 6 per
cent, in an Owe’Sullivan way of speaking.
Instead of borrowing at 3J per cent, and using the
money to earn 6 per cent., N. S. Wales borrowed
money at nearer 3f per cent., and, even with the
Owe’Sullivan to manage things, only earned a
shade over 2 per cent. The interest actually
earned was £1,509,752, while the Owe’Sullivan’s
6 per cent, would mean £4,295,549 ; and between
£1,509,752 and £4,295,549 there is a pound or two
of difference. Why will the Owe’Sullivan spoil
the materials out of which a good and much-
needed type of politician could be made, by hum-
bugging himself on this pawnshop subject ?

some definite inquiries. The capitalist on this

to work the natives, but was to support
and pay them. Bell, then Minister for
Lands, smelt something ; so, as a feeler, he
asked for an interview with the capitalist s prin-
cipal. Then the fun began. The capitalist fumed
and raged about delay, waste of valuable time, the
shock to capital’s nerves, and other irrelevant
In Darwin’s Page.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They doubt, do some, if Darwin told
The truth in yonder pages,
Wherein he traced our pedigree
Through countless, countless ages ;
They say no mortal but a fool
Would take for an Evangel
The book of him who found an ape
Where once we found an angel!
And yet the keen, observant eye
May clearly see the traces
Of some such lowly origin
In daily forms and faces;
The claw that clutched in greed, and clung,
I fancy sometimes lingers
And leaves a mark—whene’er I see
Old Cent.-per-centum’s fingers.
The fiendish men who deal in blood
Of slaves on Nile and Niger,
How can their kinship doubted be
To striped and hungry tiger ?
And if we scorn the tale that tells
We’re kin to grim hyaena,
What beasts were they who gazed upon
Or fought in Rome’s arena ?
In ev’ry mart ye daily see
The teeth of slimy saurian,
Ye mark how man his fellow-men
Can run to earth and foray on ;
And surely ne’er a sailing hawk
Could ever match the greed of him
Who grinds his mate with usury
And fattens on the need of him.
The jackdaw’s mean and petty pride,
The peacock’s flaming vanity,
Do we not know them rooted well
In this our poor Humanity ?
And men who swill them like the swine—
Is not their number pitiful ?
And men with no more thought than kiue,
Ye find them by the cityful.
And as ye mark, and read, and think,
And see how Man the Drover
Is near akin to them he drives,
And them he feeds in clover,
Ye scarce can wonder Darwin dreamed
’Twas through an earthly portal
The Race of Man emerged below,
And not through one immortal.

The Encouragement of the Infant
Immigrant.
Maoriland Government recently ukased that
married men in the Public Service should receive
larger salaries than single ones for similar work.
The idea is to encourage matrimony and conse-
quent jncrease of the population. It is a good
idea, and need not stop short with the members of
the Public Service. Also it is not altogether anew
scheme. Over a century ago, the Parliament of
England, recognising that children were a most
valuable asset, passed laws by which the parents
of large families could obtain bounties every
month for their maintenance from the county
funds, and the system remained in force till the
Government discovered that brutal employers
used to deduct a corresponding amount from the
wages of men who received the allowances. (Curi-
ously enough, this was cited by Herbert Spencer
as an instance of the uselessness of legislating
to protect the workers: whereas it was merely
an instance of the urgent necessity of doing some-
thing sudden to the brutal employer.) A child
allowance scheme is the natural corollary of an
Old Age Pension scheme. Looking at the matter
from the individualistic standpoint, man is
tempted to ask why he should burden himself with
the heavy task of rearing and educating children
from whom he will receive little or no material
benefit. One reason why the birthrate, has re-
mained high in certain parts of England is that a
child is an asset. But in Australia, so far, we
have not allowed child slavery, in the cities, at
least. Therefore, the married are tempted to
restrict their families for the sake of their own
material comfort. It would be good business on
the part of the State to use every means available
to hold out encouragements in the other direction.
Fruitful parents should find rewards in preference
of employment, in remission of taxation, in the
provision of free midwifery homes, and in direct
bonuses, if necessary.
The Yes-No of the “Argus.”
In its issue of November 27 the Argus announces
that its weekly, the Australasian, is about to
publish a special edition, advertising Victoria and
its resources. This edition, says the Argus, will
give information for immigrants :
It is pointed out in one of theopening paragraphs that
in a country with only 14 people to the square mile, as
against 558 in England, 137 in Ireland, and 150 in Scot-
land .. . there must be room for more people, and a
promising field for the employment of additional capital
and labor. . . . Population is wanted to make the most of
our resources.
Then, on the very next page of the same issue,
there is printed
Land Hunger.
3000 Applicants for 260 Blocks.
Shepparton : A Small Holdings Board sat here on Mon-
day and Tuesday. .. . There are only 260 allotments
available for 2000 or 3000 applicants. . . . The Board was
impressed with the type of the applicants, all of whom
were men in the prime of life, in full work, at low wages.
Bent made a well-meaning attempt to provide a
big slice of capital land on the Western Plains a
few weeks ago, and the Argus then informed him
severely that the supply of land from private
subdivisions was fully equal to the demand.
Also, any hint of a iand-tax, with a view to
hastening on the cutting up of the big estates,
sends the Argus into hysterics. In view, there-
fore, of the great rush of farmers for the Shepparton
land—and this is typical of what happens whenever
there are a few decent blocks available —isn’t it
time the Argus explained definitely where it is
going to place its immigrants when it gets them ?

Japan’s Industrial System.
Writes a correspondent from Nagasaki, Japan :
There is probably no country in the world where in-
dustrial disquietude is more general and menacing than
in Japan to-day. In the past it has been either sup-
pressed or ignored by the authorities, and treated with
callous indifference by the masters of industry, but the
attitude of the working classes is now such that they can
be no longer neglected.
Japanese industrialism is a soulless machine, in which
the worker is ground to a degree that would not be
tolerated in any country where those that hear the
burden of industry were intelligently and sufficiently
organised to protect themselves. And woman, being the
cheaper vessel, suffers most. Strikes and other mani-
festations of discontent have broken out in all lines of
industry and among all classes of workers lately.
In some instances, as in the case of miners, the outbreaks
were so furious as to result in murderous rioting and wan-
ton destruction of extensive property. In cotton fac-
tories, where one would expect conditions of labor to
have reached the most modern development, the strikes
were no less frequent than among the operatives in other
spheres ; and even the Government naval yards were not
exempt.
In Japan of late there has been an abnormal increase of
the cost of living, without any corresponding increase in
the matter of wages. The war, with its attendant heavy
expenditure of ready money, made good times ; but after
the excitement was over, and the g-lory of battles began
to dissolve into a burden of unbearable taxation, demand-
ing no less than £6 from every family in the empire, con-
ditions were abruptly reversed. In the last five years the
cost of living has gone up over 20 per cent., while wages
and salaries are not higher than they were 10 years ago.
All strikes during the past year have been for an increase
of wages. Imagine the employees of the Liverpool or
Boston post office all going put on strike for a living
wage, and you have what occurred in Osaka a little
time ago.
Examine briefly the labor conditions in Tokyo, where
the highest wages in the empire are paid. There a
cotton ginner at best can make only a shilling a day ; and
over most of the country they have to be content with
less. Thousands of female factory operatives get not
much more than 6d. a day. This was hardly a living
wage a year ago ; but it is less to-day. The highest
wages a Tokyo carpenter can command are 2s. a day,
with less outside the capital. Taking Tokyo as repre-
senting the maximum wage, we have, to mention a very
few examples, tilers getting Is. 8d ; plasterers, Is. lid. ;
bootmakers, Is. ; laborers, lOd. And in the last year,
rice, which is one of the most essential articles of food to
a Japanese, went up in price to double the rate of the 12
months previous.
There are no laws regulating the hours of labor. In
some cases, as on railways, 24 consecutive hours are the
rule. This excessive strain upon the workman is doubt-
less responsible for the increasing frequency of accidents
on Japanese railways. Often as one passes a station at
night the whole staff appears to be asleep, and probably
is. Factory operatives usually work from 7 a.m. to
6 p.m., with a few minutes at noon for a bit of rice to eat.
But the Japanese is accustomed to long hours, and condi-
tions would have to be very extraordinary in this respect
before he would eomplain.
Most of the factory workers are women, for the greater
part young girls ; and these suffer much from the con-
stant strain of time and activity. They are drawn chiefly
from the poorer classes, and are usually ignorant and
wholly at the mercy of the foreman or employer. It is
said that the lot of the Japanese factory girl is the
hardest known to woman ; she is engaged in a toil that is
physically exhausting; her mere pittance keeps her
underfed, and she is without either protection or sympa-
thy. The old personal feeling of interest and responsi-
bility that existed between employer and employee
formerly has disappeared. Perhaps this accounts to some
deg Tee, even if it does not extenuate, the freedom with
whieh the poorer classes sell their daughters to lives of
shame, rather than let them become the industrial slaves
of the rich.
Owing to the increasing amount of hostile criticism
which the Japanese factory system is receiving from
missionaries and other foreigners, especially in regard to
the matter of female and child labor, some of the
managers are advertising the inauguration of opportuni-
ties for education and recreation among their employees ;
but those who have kept a close eye on the course of
events declare that this is a mere subterfuge to deceive
the outside world. The system is seldom put into opera-
tion except for the interest of visitors, the regular time
that the girls are permitted for self-improvement or
recreation being practically nil. I know of but one
factory where any general altruistic consideration may
be said to govern the attitude of the capitalists to the
operatives—the Sakura Gumi leather factory at Senju,
near Tokyo.
To a foreigner the most shocking aspect of the Japanese
industrial system is the absolute indifference of managers
to the unnecessary risk and exposure to which the
operatives are constantly subjected. The alarming loss
of life, that results from accidents in the industrial
circles of Japan from week to week, could no doubt be
much reduced by a little precaution. In the Mitsu Bishi
naval yards, at Nagasaki, the writer has often wondered
how the thousands there employed could all crowd into
the few lighters to be ferried across the harbor every even-
ing. The other day, however, he was not surprised to
see one upset, and plunge hundreds of struggling men
and women into the sea, resulting in considerable
loss of life. In an Osaka arsenal hundreds of
women were employed taking the powder from old
oartridges left from thp late war; a match was
dropped, causing an explosion that killed 49 women at
once, and the subsequent fire and explosions killed a
great many more. There was no supervision adequate to
warp of the danger of using matches ip such a place.
Such accidents are too common in Japan to be justly con-
sidered unpreventible.
But there are indications that the people now are being
gradually aroused to seek their rights. The old Japanese
idea was that it is the chief duty of the people to be quiet,
and let the superior wisdom of their rulers make them
happy and content. In i-eturn, they were to pay their
taxes, and, in the spirit of Bushido, fight for their country
in time of war. But there is now a growing discontent.
That discontent may be the forerunner of social and
industrial improvement.

Bloat.
Whenever the Protectionist points out openings
for new industries, some cocksure ass always
brays back, “ Well, why don’t you go and do it ? ”
The overhauling of the affairs of William Sand-
ford, Limited, the pioneers of the Australian
iron and steel industry, ought at least to stop
that bray for a while. For the Sandfords,
though they have long contracts from the
State Government on very favorable terms, find
themselves so completely driven into a corner
that they have had to go to the State for help;
and independent experts are so convinced that the
new industry must collapse without the backing
asked for, that the Government has agreed to
advance £70,000, subject to Parliamentary
sanction. Wm. Sandfoed has spent almost a
lifetime struggling with the business, and
all the occasional profits have been sunk
in the undertaking; yet because most of the
time he has had to struggle against the
foreign competitor whom Freetrade has let in,
he has really made so little progress that he and
his company now find themselves with an over-
draft of more than £130,000, and a banker who
says that his limit has been reached. The
Freetrade papers and politicians have made the
N. S. Wales public familiar with the idea of
the Bloated Manufacturer. And here we have
him in the flesh—driven into a corner, after
nearly a lifetime spent in establishing the in-
dustry which is indispensable to every prognessive
country; weighted with an overdraft of £130,000 ;
faced by a banker who will go no farther, and
probably believes that he has gone too far already ;
met with a deaf ear when he appeals to the pri-
vate investor to come in and share his bloatedness ;
and told by the chief organ of the Bloated Manu-
facturer perjury that none of its friends, the
importers, supposing they borrowed money from
a bank, would dream of putting up with such con-
ditions as those to which the Sandfords are pre-
pared to submit in connection with this transaction
with the State. Whatever may be said about the
manner in which the Government proposes to help
the Sandfords, let us hope that we shall not hear
any more about the Bloated Manufacturer for a
while. It is time he went to the bulging grave-
yard where the other myths of the anti-Australian
party have been set to rest.
Patents and Manufactures.
“ The Gunyah ” writes re the power of the foreign
patentee to block industry by neither manufac-
turing himself in Australia nor allowing anybody
else to manufacture his patented article (“The
Wheel and the Tariff,” B. 14/11/’O7): —
Section 87 of the Fe,deral Patents Act, which deals
with this question, provides that, after the expira-
tion of two years from the date of the lodgment
of the patent, any person may petition the Com-
missioner or the Court for the grant of a compul-
sory license to use the offending patent, or, in the
alternative, for the revocation of the patent; and, pro-
\ iding it be proved to the Court’s satisfaction that the
“ reasonable requirements ” of the public are not satis-
fied, power is given for the granting of licenses. By the
term “reasonable requirement” is meant (1) if the
patentee fails to work or import his patented article
within the Commonwealth to an adequate extent to
satisfy public demands, or (2) if he refuses to grant a
license to use or manufacture the article except under
terms that are unreasonable and oppressive to any existing
industry.
This latter part was included at the instance of a
Labor member, who in careering after votes in his con-
stituency, which is essentially the headquarters of the
Melbourne bootmaking industry, gained an insight
into the. existing hardships inflicted upon boot manu-
facturers by a certain American boot machinery co. If
a boot manufacturer desires to instal one of this com-
pany’s machines in his factory, before he is even per-
mitted to gaze on the same he has to sign a document as
long as George Reid’s promises, as tricky as a hatful of
monkeys, and as binding as the Commonwealtlj Constitu-
tion. This document sets forth (1) that the machine is
only lent out on hire, (2) that the hirer has to employ cer-
tain men supplied by the eo. to watch the running of the
machine, and (3) should a part get broken he must
purchase a replica from the co. and no one
else. W. Lyne, the other day, drew attention to
this section, and stated it was optional for any person to
tackle the co. under this clause. No doubt ; but who is
going to tackle such a wealthy co. in the courts? Sup-
pose the private person gained the day, the co. would
immediately appeal and appeal. The only person
directly interested is the boot manufacturer (although
the public has to pay in the end), and under the terms of
the hire of the machine he is bound hand and foot. Even
were he to break his agreement and tackle the co., the
latter would first of all withdraw the hired machines
from his factory, which he would then have to close
down, and as the law is no Poseidon he would probably
be either bankrupt or an angel before the case came on
for hearing. If licenses were granted under terms that
were not oppressive and unreasonable, boots manu-
factured in Australia would come down fully 30 per cent.,
for the machine to build and instal costs just about as
much as the manufacturer pays for the hire for 12 months.
The only way out of the difficulty is for the Common-
wealth Government to leg-rope this and similar com-
panies under the Patents Act.

Australia’s Stomach.
There are agitations from time to time in this
country to bring about most things, from Secesh
and the total abolition of Australian manufac-
tures, to the filling of the country with China-
men and niggers. There is one urgent circum-
stance, however, that no one seems to agitate
about with much enthusiasm —namely, that of
Australia’s stomach. Excited representatives of
the importing interests group themselves in the
streets around a flag once a year, and blather ear-
nestly about the health of the Empire; but they
don’t show much concern for the health of the
people that make up the Empire. Australia is a
favored no one will starve in fairly
good times unless he decides he would rather do
that than rise up and get a hustle on him. The
conditions of the Freetrade United Kingdom,
where, according to the present British Premier,
one-fourth of the total population is always ailing
for lack of food, will never obtain in the Common-
wealth so long as a rational fiscal and land
system is adhered to. But there are other
things nearly as fatal to mankind as not
assimilating sufficient food; and one of them
is assimilating too much food, and another is
assimilating the wrong sort of food. Australia’s
tendency is in the two latter directions. The
amount Australians don’t know, and don’t seem
to want to know, about their internal arrange-
ments is extensive. The quantity they don’t
know about the things they eat, but shouldn’t, is
simply vast and immeasurable. Daily lecturers in
State schools on indigestion, dyspepsia, and liver
troubles, with details as to how such complaints
are best obviated, would do a lot of good for the
coming generation of mostly meat-eating Aus-
tralians. And after the Tory person had said his
little piece in opposition, and pointed out
that nothing of the sort was done in Eng-
land, and that, anyhow, it was indelicate
and disgusting, and, in fact, immoral,
to openly discuss people’s innards, the new
addition to the State school curriculum would
probably become rather popular. Anyhow, in
the welter of local agitations there should be
roeni for one advocating reform along these lines.
The formation of a society for the express pur-
pose of making Australia better acquainted with
its stomach would be novel, and full of valuable
possibilities.
Landlords and Rabbits.
“T.A.W.” writes :
Asa person who has spent a good many years of a
moderately sober life in and around the Wagga (N.S.W.)
district—a district having its full share of the twin curses
of rabbits and big landlords —I give the palm to the
second a§ the champion destructive pest in all things
except that they are not so prolific.
When the Pastures Protection Board.B were instituted
they were armed with power to enforce active and
effective measures for rabbit destruction, and in the dis-
trict of which I speak—as in other districts—rabbit
inspectors were appointed. The result was that the
small farmer was compelled, under pain of heavy fines, to
keep his poison-cart orfumigatorgoingpretty constantly,
the effect, as far as the small holdings were concerned,
being decidedly had for the rabbit. The big landlord,
however, was easily able—no doubt by judicious palm-
greasing—to persuade the inspector that a couple of
poison-carts standing in the homestead yard were quite
sufficient guarantee against the existence of rabbits on
his property, and the inspector never took the trouble to
go and look, and see for himself—or, if he did, he conve-
niently failed to see anything. Thus, on the big holdings
the rabbits increased and multiplied, and as soon as the
small man relaxed his efforts in the way of destruction,
to get in his crop or for any other purpose, they spread
thence over his land, and the weary fight had to com-
mence all over again, It was very little use the small
man complaining to the Pastures Protection Board.
Much of the Pastures Protection Board was big landlord
or his manager, and it naturally preferred to listen to
the tale of its own inspector as against that of the small
farmer. It is two or three years since I left the Wagga
district, but although one or two of the big estates there
have now been subdivided, I believe the weary old
struggle is still going on.
In regard to wire-netting as a protection against rab-
bits, all experience goes to show that it is only a very
slight check to the pest, and its cost is such that very
few farmers consider the gain worth the outlay. Unless
the ground is as hard as granite, and the netting
buried deep, bunny will burrow under it. Also the
rotten imported netting quickly develops holes, which
bunny is much more adept at finding than is the
farmer, and if there is no way of getting under or
through it, he has been known—definitely known, mind
—to climb over it. So that, whilst the big estate remains
as a breeding-ground for the rabbit, dozens of him
will always be getting past the farmer’s netting' fence and
on to his land, and, as the rabbit is perhaps the most
prolific beast on the face of the earth, if the farmer
leaves those few dozens alone for four or five months he
will find, at the end of that time, that he has as many
rabbits on his securely-netted land as ever before—and
thus the poison-cart must be kept going just the same,
netting or no netting.
The foreign potentate who rules the Lake View
Consois, the mining show that once caused a
giddy boom, announces in London that he is
going to organise a movement to compel the
miners to take less pay for swallowing Kalgoorlie
dust; or else shut down the mines. Govett, the
potentate in question, says that Lake View Con-
sols is only earning 4 per cent, on its capital, and
the co. isn’t going to wear out machinery and
root out gold just for the sake of the Australian
miners. Govett doesn’t mention that in 1898 the
co. made a profit of £286,325, and £614,633 in
1899, and £387,718 in 1900, or £1,288,676 alto-
gether, so that in three years he and the
other foreign sponges mopped up four times
the amount of their capital. He didn’t
offer the miners any bonus then ; he just paid
them the lowest wages he could get them to work
for. If anybody had said in those days that
Westralia shouldn’t allow its gold to be torn out
just to fatten a foreign crowd that didn’t care two
straws about Australia, there would have been a
horrible squeal about driving capital out of the
country. Now, however, that the co. has rooted
out most of the gold and pocketed the pioceeds,
it asks the miners whether they aren’t ashamed
of themselves for taking wages from a poor co.
that can’t pay 100 per cent, dividends once a
quarter any more. That’s the sort of absentee
Australia is asked to speak gently to, and feed
well, lest it should go away and not come back
any more. Govett’s threat about shutting up
the mines ought to be met on the threshold, and
kicked back with another remark pasted to its
t&il—;that the one condition on which Australia
permits the foreigner to hold a mine is that he
works it, and that, if he doesn’t work it, then his
right stops short. Australia has set up Arbitra-
tion Courts, and is prepared to see that even the
foreign employer gets a fair-wage award ; but it is
not going to permit any absentee to stop the
wheels of progress because it won’t accept what-
ever conditions he pleases to lay down.
Cheerful remark, in the annual report of Super-
intendent Webb, of Sydney Fire Brigades
A Bill for better regulating theatres and places of
public concourse generally was drafted, but owing to the
pressure of other business, was not dealt with. .. . One
building used for athletic entertainments is a disgrace to
Sydney, in want of cleanliness and the means of exit. A
license for the building to be used for theatrical enter-
tainments was refused some years ago, and rightly so ;
but on occasions the building is packed with audiences
more numerous than when it was licensed.
There was, for sometime, that scandal also of the
Lyceum Theatre, which, having been refused a
license as a theatre, was turned into a hall where
dangerous cinematograph shows were given before
packed houses; and, though things are being
altered there, the law which permitted such an
anomaly is an obvious ass. Presumably when a
Governor, a Judge, and a thousand or so Sydney
people are grilled in a dangerous building, after
the manner of the Iroquois Theatre disaster in
America, the IN’. S. Wales Parliament will find
time to handle this subject.
“One of the Perspiring ” :
Though a LYNE-man myself, I take exception to Tariff
William’s recent statement that the officials in the
Sydney G.P.O. are the “best paid, and blessed with the
snuggest billets in the world.” Take my own case. I
have been in the employ of the Electric Telegraph De-
partment of this State 17 long and more or less
weary years. I am 33, married, and, during the
last five years, have never been guilty of an empty
cradle. The man who acts as the Recording Angel
of the misdeeds of the men in my Depart-
ment cannot show a black cross against my name.
I work like Sheol seven hours and a half a day, Saturday
included, a,nd no provision is made for meal hours.
Through wolfing my bit of food whilst working at the
same time, I, in common with many of my contempo-
raries, have destroyed my digestive machinery, and the
wraith of chronic dyspepsia looms darkly ahead. I deal
daily with thousands of important telegrams (more or
less). The delay or bungling- of any one of them invari-
ably brings a stinging complaint through the Deputy
P.M.G., or an infuriated letter in the daily
press. Then I catch the very father of a wigging.
And, for it all, I draw the princely Salary
of £2 2s. weekly, from which the sum of 2s. 6d. is
deducted yearly to make good the occasional postal note
which some understrapper in the same position as myself
has been tempted to steal. We have, times without
number, deputationed the Public Service inspector, and
whispered our tale of woe into his ear, but he has only
assumed a sorrowful countenance, agreed that our case
“ \yas indeed a bard one,” promised he would use his in-
fluence, then sunk back with a sigh of relief as we filed
out, and hastened to forget all about us. Years and
years back—the Lord only knows how many—provision
was made for the section I am quoting to attain £126 per
annum by small yearly increments ; and the flame of
hope blazed in our breasts. But it burned out quite a
long time ago. No ; “ the best paid and easiest billet in the
world ” isn’t to be found in the Sydney G.P.O.

It seems impossible in N. S. Wales to frame a
Land Act without incorporating some absurdity.
Ashton’s 1905 Act is like the rest in that respect.
In the original mapping out of the country, those
spots where there was a settlement, or likely to be
a settlement, were enclosed with a line, inside of
which were “ town lands.” Outside another line
was drawn, the area between the two being
marked “suburban lands.” With an unkind dis-
regard for departmental measurements, towns have
not always grown within their measured limits.
The main street of a place (Wyalong, for example)
is often right outside the suburban land area.
Now, Ashton’s Act (section 15) states :
No person shall be competent to apply for a conditional
purchase lease who is the holder of any land, except
(1) Town or suburban land, declared so by proclamation
in the Gazeite.
(2) Land rented from a private holder.
It is a fairly common thing for a hard-working
man, who has been thrifty and bought a small
piece of land to make a home, to be an applicant
under Ashton’s Act. He finds then, because of
the section referred to, his application is thrown
out. To own even an eighth or a sixteenth of an
acre of land outside the imaginary line which
marks suburban lands is an absolute disqualifica-
tion in all cases where land is thrown open for
conditional purchase lease. The original Closer
Settlement Act of ’O4 held a similar provision, but
the amending Act allows a man who holds
up to 40 acres of land anywhere to make a settle-
ment purchase. Land legislators, however,
apparently learnt their work in a sewing-school,
where “crazywork” was fashionable, and they
patched one Act and left the other.

From “Port Arthur,” by E. Ashmead-Bartlett :
We have prided ourselves for years on the fact that we
are a phlegmatic race, ready to take every turn of the
wheel of fortune with equal stoicism. In reality we are
an hysterical nation. John Bull still appears in popular
caricature as a solid, red-faced, substantial person, in-
capable of being aroused by even an explosion under his
house. What a farce is such a portrait! He should be
represented thin, neurotic, idle, conceited, and too lazy
to prepare against attack, alternating between excessive
optimism and excessive despair. He calls out the same
battle-cries of 50 years ago in an unconvincing voice, and
relieves his overwrought conscience at periodical inter-
vals by the appointment of a Royal Commission.
There is more fuss made in England over the departure
and return of a single citizen soldier than over the de-
parture and return of all Oyama’s million heroes. The
one nation deals'with war in a businesslike manner,
claims her soldiers as her due, and sends them home
when she has finished with them. The other, by extrava-
gant display, by music-hall effusions, by bounties, buns
and beer, stirs up an entirely false and temporary feeling
of patriotism in the minds of those peculiarly susceptible
to the influence of brass bands. She sends them to the
front, and removes any general who sacrifices one of these
national darlings unnecessarily. Then, when the war is
over, they are received back in an extravagant manner,
utterly out of accordance with their martial feats.
We in England live in a fools’ paradise. We detest the
idea of devoting one short year of our lives to the defence
of the Empire, a subject which supplies a peroration for
every orator, and which is invariably greeted with loud
applause. We forget that if universal military service
were introduced, the idea of compulsion would be at once
eliminated where all are treated alike, and it becomes a
recognised factor of our national life. We forget that our
little army is but a drop in the ocean compared with our
necessities, and costs more than the mighty machine
which adds such potency to a certain emperor’s tele-
grams. We rely on one barrier, and that barrier alone
stands between England and her foes—our fleet. But
what a poor defence is a fleet, however strong, in this age
of advanced science ! At any hour an invention may come
to light which will neutralise decades of ship-building,
and then the issue will be fought out on- land in the
primitive method by flesh and blood.
