Atuen/eu.u Hall Living Scotland Bio.
In “Lady Madcap,” Alma Barber implores
the “ man in America ”to send her a song. The
audience at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday
night would have backed up her request even if
it had been for half-a-dozen, so that she might
have shared them round. Poorer musical-comedy
hasn’t been played in Sydney under so big a
heading for many a night. The dearth of song in
it gets pretty frank admission from the manage-
ment, which hangs up proceedings between the
acts to allow Miss Maher to show off a satisfac-
tory contralto voice; while Miss Celia Gliiloni,
who has a merely picturesque part, inter-
polates “Bebe Ribbons”—words by Arthur
Adams; music (a simple, popular little melody)
by an unmentioned lady in Westralia. Myles
Clifton and Mr. Nable drag in also “The
East End and the West,” and it takes some
dragging in. Had other things been left out to
make room for these undoubted embellishments,
the medley would have been improved. The
critics unanimously call for the pruning knife ;
but it is not green wood that wants removing—
some of the dreary comedy “dialogue” is old,
hard stringybark, upon which nothing less
forcible than an adze will make an adequate im-
pression. Still, apparently with the idea that if
it can’t give quality it at least ought to give
quantity, the management notifies that in future
the show will start at 7.45. The free use of the
sharp-edged tool previously mentioned would
probably give more satisfaction in the long run.
* * * *
The piece has its virtues, certainly. No
charming girl has to philander with any tem-
porarily-discolored gentleman ; and it is a blessed
relief to find that modern musical comedy doesn’t
necessarily include a potentate of Asia on matri-
mony bent. The other chief virtue is less
negative; it is Myles Clifton. For some reason
that isn’t clear and doesn’t matter, Myles Clifton,
who is Oroya Brown and a millionaire, becomes
Private Smith, a trooper in a regiment, where he
has to wash his own horse, and the brute bites
at him, old chap, don’t you know. Don’t
require any probability whatever in the
character, but merely regard his mission as
being to amuse, and you pass Private Smith as
an excellent piece of fooling. The dry comedian’s
moralising over a bunch of keys is probably the
best thing he has done in Australia. But there
is not a song incidental to either Myles Clifton’s
character or any other that is likely to be re-
membered. Lady Madcap herself (Miss Daisie
Wallace) has none. Miss Barber has “ Man,
man in America,” which may stay in
the memory six months, and fade out
about the same time as Reginald Kenneth’s
“A Way we Have in the Army.” The chorus—•
vivacious as ever; only surpassed in that re-
spect by Alfred Moulton on the conductor’s
stand—gets one or two opportunities, as in
“We’re Flunkeys High and Haughty,” and
“ Archery Ladies” ; but there are no very striking
stage pictures to fill up the space between the
footlights and Gordon’s two very charming back-
grounds.

J. C. Williamson got no satisfactory reply
to his application for hints as to how a man could
spend a quarter-of-a-million in Australia under the
conditions that afflicted the hero of “ Brewster’s
Millions.” One man simply said that he would
hand the money over to his solicitor, and trust to
his muddling; and that nearly got J.C.W.’s five
guineas. In the end, however, the money went
to charities. Brewster’s own troubles will end on
Friday of this week ; after that, “ John Glayde’s
Honor” will have a short life—its first in Aus-
tralia —while a crowd of kiddies get their final
brushing-up in “ Blue Bell in Fairyland,” the first
of the Christmas pieces.

Clarence Tisdale has added his tenor
warble to the Tivoli performance, but beyond
that there is no change. Large ones are, however,
looming, for the agile Whiteleys and others are in
their last nights.

Hi Hi * *
Melba’s concerts in Sydney are already an
assured success. Crowds foregathered to the
opening of plans at Paling’s on Monday last, and
every day since a huge tally of sovereigns has
testified to the attraction of the singer, and to the
ruined state of this unfortunate city. The dates
have been skilfully arranged to include Saturday,
14th, the second day of the test match.
The success of the second Melba concert
was the success of the previous one, repeated with
emphasis. The same—only more so. As the
crowded audience departed slowly for the street,
to find a neighboring district ablaze, and to thank
Heaven that the dangerous Town Hall was outside
the radius of the fire-panic, people were heard
railing against Melbourne’s constitutional weak-
ness in the matter of enthusiasm. A foreign public
would have been wildly appreciative, whereas
this public had not troubled to recall the songstress
a dozen times after her final encore number.
The complainers had wanted more furore for their
guinea. Nevertheless, the city shows an increasing
aptitude to take its musical pleasures gladly.
When Melba sang the “Lucia” Mad Scene, on
Saturday night, there was a louder roar of applause
than she drew from the audience that welcomed
her back to Australia, five years ago. Then, as
now, she displayed all the beauty of her voice and
vocalism in this long piece of florid opera, but now
she seems to have a new feeling for the
character, and to sing with more dramatic power.
Letnmone played the flute obligato, in which
Melba’s imported flautist of yore came to grief
when she sang the “Lucia” scene at the Princess’s
Theatre. There was no such calamity on Satur-
day, and the mingling of voice and flute tones was
wonderful. The modern Italian school of music
got a fine show in La Tosca’s tearful prayer from
Puccini’s opera. Later on Melba sang“Pleurez
mes yeux ” from Massenet’s “ Le Cid,” and gave
“The White Sea Mist”—her best effort in
songlets—as the encore. For a nominal finale
she gave the assembled womankind a lesson in
how to warble the Arditi valse song, “LeSaran
Rose,” after which “ the house rose at her,” so to
speak. Or, rather, it didn’t rise, but remained
seated until she had thrown in another merry
Italian ditty to her own accompaniment. The
concert, apart from Melba, included Andrew
Black. Louis Hattenbach, Lemmone, and Miss
Una Bourne, as before, and the gifted ’cellist was
described on the programme as a “ Herr.” In
this English-speaking country there are no herren,
or monsieurs, or signori. They are all misters—
plain misters—and The Bulletin desires Hatten-
bach to understand that it can make no exception
in his case despite his curly crest. He must get
his Herr cut.
* * * *
General Desire, who tried hard to induce
Melba to give a concert at the Exhibition Build-
ing last time she was around, has talked her into
acquiescence this time. After her return from
Sydney, and when the building aforesaid has been
cleared of all the needlework and babies and
things that lately littered its precincts, Melba
will send her top note fluttering to the dome.
It is to be on Boxing Night.
* Hi Hi Hi
“ The Midnight Wedding” and the new
advertisement curtain, which are alike artistic in
a businesslike way, have reached their fifth week
at the Royal. Alsb they are still going strong.
In the production of a four-act drama the curtain
is an important part of the show, and this
particular curtain, being designed to present
the advertiser as a superior person whose
commercial spirit is elegantly framed in gold on a
pale blue ground, attracts more attention than
would be given to an ugly sheet of uncompromising
statements. Therefore, the present writer deplores
the absence of advts. fitted to the present bill. A
lady in a state of corsets is possibly dressing herself
for her wedding at midnight, but the picture is
not explanatory. And none of the other advts.
has any bearing on the title of the drama.
Hi * He He
This is the last week but one of “ The Girls
of Gottenberg,” at Her Majesty’s. The end of the
season draweth near, yet is there still time for
George Lauri to change his make-up and other-
wise alter himself before he leaves Melbourne.
Even his staunchest supporters have wearied of
his red wig, and his baby stare, and his nose
that is painted to look as though he had a cold
in it. Subtle character acting is not expected
of a popular broad comedian in musical farces ;
but he might at least break the monotony of his
personality as regards the wig and the nose. Just
at present. Lauri is supposed to be an ex-barber
of miraculous audacity and ready wit, but his
smile suggests the typical English village imbecile,
and his head of hair looks impossible to an
emancipated barber.
Hi Hi Hi Hi
This last week, the Opera House bill was
adorned with the portentous name of Herbert La
Martine, described as vocalist, eccentric dancer,
and baton-spinner. In the folly of spinning a
baton until it resembles a wheel in motion,
Herbert has acquired a proficiency that excites
commiseration for himself and the audience. Why
practise baton spinning, and why applaud the re-
sult? Finglas, the bogus coon, is roaring tunefully
as before. Price and Revost finish a howling turn
with unexpected somersaults into the stalls, and
(presumably, on account of the hot weather,
which induces a sort of brain-stagnancy) the two
Colliers present the “limit” in inanity. The
agile Molasso ladies will continue to display
abnormal lengths of spine for a few more nights,
what time the equally agile Molasso gentlemen
exhibit phases of continental polish.

“ Human Hearts,” at Adelaide Royal, has
been well supported in the “popular” parts of the
house. Towards the end of its week, it un-
accountably got in a nasty one at its patrons by
advertising prominently that “Even the worst
and meanest of mankind will applaud the good,
the true, and the beautiful on the stage.” But,
then, this management has not much sense of
humor, or it would not issue its striking poster of
an incoherent person asking, “ Shall I forget that
I am in the presence of the Dead, and kill him ? ”
This finely-acted American concoction has now
passed on, and Andrew Mack is on the door-
step (so to speak), preparing to knock out the real
history of Thomas Moore in three rounds.
The Prussian Army acquired last month
in the person of one Cassel, son of a millionaire
Berliner, the first Jew of commissioned rank it
has ever known—and the Continental anti-Semite
organs rage accordingly. French Royalist papers
suggest genially that the boycott system, which
finally broke down, a few months ago, the resolu-
tion even of Dreyfus, is the best treatment for
Cassel.

AT POVERTY POINT.
’Arry Rickards came back from foreign ’alls last week
to find that he had been made a grandfather in his
absence.
Granville Barker’s new play, “Waste,” was forbidden,
last month, by the British Censor of Plays, and there is
Sheol to pay among literary people. Meredith, Thomas
Hardy, Shaw and others have arisen in bitter protest,
and signed a petition for presentation to King Ned or the
Government, or both, urging that Redford (the Censor in
question) be dismissed and his office abolished.
The phrase credited to Melba “ I am an artist, not an
exhibition ” is good, and there is just a suggestion that
the Butt end of the Rumford menage might be the ex-
hibition ; but, all the same, Melba too is travelling a little
on “the kid” this trip. She brought sonny with her
here, and not a little is made of sonny. Sonny’s bride is
the other “ cheeild ” upon whom Melba splashes her
splendid affections, and one comes across frequent refer-
ences in the weekly clothes-press of Melbourne to a
certain magnificent cot purchased by Melba, and other
infantile paraphernalia suggestive of more kid—kid with
which Madame is to be associated in a grandmotherly way.
Auckland (M.L.) recently formed an enthusiastic syndi-
cate for the purpose of acquiring and producing musical
comedy, comic opera, and other musical stuff generally,
by Australasian authors and composers exclusively. As
a start, the co. has decided to offer a prize of £5O for the
best work sent in up to August 12, ’OB, and offers authors
a percentage of the gross takings of production, while
allowing them to retain full proprietary rights in their
pieces.
MISS BEATRICE BRAMAH , of Frank
Thornton’s Company.
“ Burr Lud” : Nomadic pros, paint luridly the horrors
of country tours. Like Island missionaries, they never
pause to think that the poor heathen may have dyspepsia
or other ills after their visit. But put a few good healthy
brisk young artists at the prow, df disciplinarian, with a
slight silvery lining in his pocket, at the managerial helm,
and it’s odds on the boat being filled with a paying bush
cargo, instead of 60 per cent, city stowaways or dead-
heads, as at present. What the man on the outer rim
objects to is the old, painted, worn-out, prehistoric
supers spending their declining years in fearsome, har-
rowing, bellowdramas in the bush. There is a big paying
vacuum outback for a bright, spirited young company.
“ Blue Peter : ” Re English mummers’recent access of
domestic virtues. Clara Butt and “ himself ’’are nothing
to Seymour Hicks and Ellaline Terriss. He writes, runs,
and stage-manages the plays, and he and Ellaline divide
up every bit of “ fat ” between them, with a few necessary
supers to fill in time, while they take breath or change
their clothes. If they get any applause at the finish, they
run up the curtain, dive into the wings, and bring on
their infant Betty. They each hang on to half, and bump
their heads together, both trying' to kiss her at once.
It fetches the British public every time. “Isn’t Ellaline
Terriss sweet ?” they say. The Australian blinks and
says, “But she can’t sing, and she can’t dance, and she
can’t act.” “Oh no, but she’s so refined. She and her
husband are such a devoted couple, and they have such a
lovely baby,” and the Londoner considers that there is
no more to be said ; and then they begin reverently dis-
cussing the best places to go for dinner.
“ Theorbo ”: Why not an Australian Grand Opera
Company? Here you have a superb orchestra and chorus
ready to hand. The German Company, however much
its members may disparage each other, agreed that the
choristers have been unexpectedly splendid, and
astonishingly quick. The public wants the music. Mr.
Musgrove has had to fall back on choristers as principals
now and again, invariably with good results. Let us
have Grand Opera at three shillings, two shillings, and
one shilling. Scenery is to hand. An average of five
pounds a week each, with every expense paid—clear five
quid—on the understanding that this must be a risky
experiment, would gather principals galore.
May Beatty, from England “ Touring, and so busy.
Last week we were at Coventry—such a quaint town ;
picturesque old buildings everywhere, and not one bath-
room. The place is full of motor-cars—motor-cars, but
no baths ! We return to London this month (October),
and play there till pantomime time. Igo to Newcastle
to play Dick Whittington,”
“ Rob Roy” is to be staged, December 14, 16and 17, at
Sydney Palace by amateurs of the A.M.P. Society.
Maud Thornton, the pretty Australian who was last
seen here as the drummer boy in “ Tambour Major,” has
signed on with George Edwardes for 12 months. She
spends the first half-year in S’Africa leading in “ The Merry
Widow,” “ Girls of Gottenberg,” and other tried Gaiety
and Apollo shows; returning afterwards to Daly’s
Theatre, London.
Two Melbourne girls were on duty at different concert
halls, in London, one Saturday afternoon in October.
Both of them were press-noticed as artists of rising im-
portance. At the Queen’s Hall, where Suzanne Adams
and Ben Davies were among the stars of the Chappell
ballad concert, Beatrice Miranda warbled. At the
Bechstein Hall a pianoforte recital was given by Rita
Hope, the damsel who went to Vienna and the School of
Leschetizky some two years ago. Rita, it seems, has
justified the farewell she got at Melb. Town Hall on the
eve of her departure, although the local critics dismissed
her as just a pianiste and little more. London critics
accept her as a girl with a future.
Beatrice Miranda nowadays is coupled with elder sister
Lalla in laudatory mention, and the family name sounds
loud in the London land. Beatrice writes a glowing
account of Lalla in the Big Smoke, and talks chattily of
herself : “ They mixed us up a bit in the papers. People
W'ent to the opera expecting to hear me, and were
amazed to hear quite a different voice, for ours are not a
bit alike. Lalla’s is purely a lyric soprano—a lovely
quality—and they say mine is a dramatic soprano. lam
still working hard, and it is a great advantage having my
sister here, because she is able to help and teach me such
a lot. I held quite a little reception outside the theatre
on Saturday night, receiving congratulations on my
sister’s behalf from all the Australians who were there.
There are hosts of them in London—it’s almost like being
in Australia sometimes.”
“ Star ” : I greatly admire the simple, unabashed in-
solence of those people who, whenever Melba comes into
the open, hurl at her impudent insinuations or blatant
declarations that it is her duty to sing for charity at the
first opportunity. Within three weeks I have seen it
asserted nine times in press letters that Melba should
sing for this charity or that. Melba is a fairly generous
distributor, but if she were not, on what grounds of
reason or decency do persons who run bone mills, ham
and beef shops, and rag foundries clamor for artists to
put up big jobs solely for charities ? Nobody ever hears
of the ham and beef man running his shop for a week or
a day exclusively in the interests of the hospitals, and the
person who sells fenders and hoop-iron cutlery, or mil-
linery, or summer soft goods, doesn’t do so for the bene-
volent asylums, even on rare occasions. These persons
simply cannot get away from the idea that the artist is a
rascal who gets liis or her money nefariously, and should be
forced to disgorge at stated intervals, and their distress
when he gets money in large quantities drives them to
the most ridiculous, illogical excesses.
Walter Howard, the perpetrator of “The
Midnight Wedding” and other dramas of the
same type, is an old actor with a strong,
serviceable memory for plots and “situations.”
Some of his plays, recently produced for the
first time, were constructed many years ago,
and some of the scenes he then patched to-
gether are old enough to be new to the present
generation. The style of construction differs
from that of the more modern and crowded
melodrama, and the cast of characters is not so
long. Compared with the unwieldy Drury
Lane dramas these Family Herald stage stories
by Howard give better opportunities to the
performers, and bigger profits to the manage-
ment. The scene painter is put in the back-
ground. The Blandoltian army of supers is
not required. Effects are dramatic rather than
spectacular, and the plot has no comedy side to
it, bits of fooling being simply sandwiched be-
tween layers of narrative. A touch of novelty
has been given to Walter Howard’s stuff by
the hand of Time, for in the long ago he
plagiarised his melodramas from plays, whereas
the later melodramatists have plagiarised from
one another, striving for originality only in
their ridiculous sensation scenes by introducing
a steam-roller in place of a steam-hammer, or
a “ living ladder ” instead of a “ living bridge.”
In the ’Bo’s, or earlier, Howard was acting in
Australia, it is said, but seemingly he neglect ed
to steal any of the local atmosphere for his
plays.
Paul Rubens, main fabricator of “ Lady Mad-
cap,” which opened on Saturday night last at
Her Majesty’s, is a fortunate young man. With
no deep musical knowledge, a tenuous wit,
and a lack of originality that is astonishing, he
has made for himself a definite place in the
London theatrical world. George Edwardes,
of Gaiety fame, retains him at a big annual
figure to write musical comedy, librettos, and
lyrics. Rubens is, and has for long been,
the vogue among the queer people who make London
leg-shows pay—and therein lies his value. He’s an
Oxford product, cetat 30, and a Jew.
“ Pea-green Discussing the Gawler acci-
dent, a man who ought to know told me that the
S. Australian .Railway Department is grossly
under-manned, and that the September overtime
worked by guards going out from North Terrace
was 5000 hours, while porters’ overtime for
the same period was 7000 hours. One man in the
yard got in 95| hours in one week (within 15min.
of two weeks jambed into one). The man who
made the mistake at Crawler was admittedly over-
worked. On the platforms at several stations
most of the porters’ work is done by mere youths,
at about 4s. per day. A Departmental Board of
Inquiry consists of five men, four of whoih are
officials, the fifth one representing the men. If
the decisions are arrived at by a four to one
majority, it would be no more than one would
expect.
A thoroughly Australian Christinas gift
for a friend abroad—a subscription to The Lone
Hand for 12 months. It will keep your friend
current with Australian life.
“Stony”: Over in Tassy they manufac-
ture home-made wine in large quantities. Every
housewife-with a currant-bush or a row of goose-
berries makes and bottles the pungent fermeuta.
tion every year, and if she hasn’t a gooseberry
bush, she goes out into the highways and byways,
and compels the wanderer to come in. In the
years that have fled, I rioted among the farming
fastnesses “ down Channel,” and once I started off
with the local C.E. parson for a round of profes-
sional calls. At each house, instead of brewing a pot
of tea, the hospitable housewife went into the secret
parts, and led forth the home-made wine. The
parson, after the third call, sat down by the way-
side, and burst into a loud roar of laughter. “ Old
Mrs. Smith ! ” he said, suffocatingly, and
then roared some more, and became rapt in
thought. In a few minutes, being rested, he
broke into another roar of laughter, and sug-
gested that we had perhaps better be getting
along. I agreed ; and in 20 minutes we came upon
a high road, with a farmhouse standing near by.
This was our fourth calling-place, and the parson
now distinguished himself by upsetting his glass
of wine over the cat, and calling for some more.
This meant that we had sampled four different
kinds of sweet wines within three hours—in big-
bodied wine-glasses, usually refilled. At this
stage, there being three more houses on the list,
we decided to go home. Two miles out of the
township we decided, instead, to rest. It was
moonlight when we woke up.

The gilded youth adores
The seasoned ingenue ;
Her virtue never bores.
The gilded youth adores
Her innocence, because
He knows it isn’t true.
The gilded youth adores
The seasoned ingenue !

WORTH A CROWN.
SOLD FOR A SHILLING,
Cheap Labor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAVE had experience of many kinds of
cheap labor, from Javanese to South Sea
Islanders; but the cheapest in the whole
world is the evade (escaped convict) of New
Caledonia. Since transportation began there are
1700 prisoners missing. Very few have man-
aged to reach Australia ; most have perished try-
ing. A good number have gone under in the New
Caledonia bush, and there are still a few hundred
working on various mines. These men have a
pretty hard time. They work like demons, and
to ask for anything like fair wages or to shirk
work means being reported to the gendarmes.
Then the manager of the mine receives 25 francs
“ blood money.” For the same reason the evades
never get drunk, and avoid, as far as possible, all
quarrels.
Some six years ago an owner of many mining
properties in the Island had a corner in convicts.
That is to say —the Government undertook to
supply him with that class of labor, up to 2000, at
one franc per day. The gentleman was about to float
a group of mines in London, and one of the
inducements held out in the prospectus was
“cheap labor.”
The co. was floated, and the Government sent
up a batch of 560 convicts, besides erecting
barracks and supplying a squad of soldiers to keep
order. The favorite punishment was to chain the
insubordinates on a bench, stripped, stark naked,
and leave them there for a few days and nights.
With m jsquitoes in billions and attending strictly
to business, a course of this treatment was
always effectual.
Runaways were frequent, but it generally ended
in two Kanakas appearing in camp, after a few
days, carrying something between them trussed up
to a pole, which something, on being dumped
down, turned out to be one of the evades. The
Kanakas received their reward, and retired to the
nearest wine-shop. The prisoner was soon on
his way to Noumea, to receive a taste of the
“ cachot noir,” otherwise “ black hole,” and pro-
bably have 15 years added to his term.
This class of “cheap labor” took very little
interest in the success of the mine. They used
to throw their picks,.drills, etc., down the well-
hole at the bottom of the shaft whenever oppor-
tunity offered. They also had a playful way of
heaving the pigs of ore overboard from the barges
whilst on the way to the steamers. Of course,
under the circumstances, they could hardly be
expected to put themselves to the trouble of study-
ing economy.
Amongst 500 men it would be strange not to
find one good worker. There was one from the
South of France, named Jacques. Amid the weedy
lothe stood out a regular Hercules. At the time,
of which I write he was, with another convict,
working in the face at No. 6 level. It was quite
a pleasure to see how the driving progressed there.
One' morning, at roll-call, Jacques was missing.
The Kanaka sleuth hounds scoured the country,
but day after day passed without “something”
being dragged into camp. The “ blood money”
was doubled ; still no result. All the time, to my
great surprise, work in No. 6 level went on at
the same rate as before Jacques was missing. I
complimented the men, and said I was glad to
see they had learnt something from their late
comrade. I also increased their rations.
The reef at this time was getting poorer in the
lower levels, and, as the co. was under contract
to deliver ore of a certain percentage, the manager
asked me to inspect the worked-out upper stopes,
and see if any bunches of richer ore had been
overlooked. I descended to No. 3 level, and,
with lamp in hand, proceeded to make my inspec-
tion. I had not gone more than 200 ft. along the
drive when I heard a rustle ahead. “Bats,” I
said, and walked on. Again I heard the noise ;
this time it sounded like someone shuffling along
without boots. I drew my revolver—it was the
custom never to be without it, and life is
very cheap in New Caledonia. On I went,
knowing) whatever it was, it could not
get beyond the face of the drive. When I reached
the end I found a man grovelling on the ground,
with his faced buried in his hands, sobbing bit-
terly. I stooped down, and recognised Jacques.
When he Saw who it was, and not a gendarme, as
he expected, he besought me not to betray him.
He was hiding until his beard and hair grew, and
then he meant to try to get work at the southern
end of the Island, and perhaps in two years save
sufficient money to make a dash for liberty.
“Oh, Monsieur, think of w'hat I have suffered.
Twelve years have I slaved, yet there is no chance
of ever again seeing France and my dear ones if I
stop here, however hard I work.” I told him I
was not an informer, and that he was safe as far
as I was concerned.
‘ ‘ But how have you managed to live ? ” I
asked.
“Every night shift, Monsieur, I go down to
No. 6 level and work for the men there, they in
return giving me part of their rations.”
I left the poor devil still crouching on the
ground and muttering blessings on the ‘ 1 bon
Anglais.”
Some two years passed and I was engaged to
report on a chrome mine down South. To my
surprise, I saw Jacques in one of the open cuts. I
stood for some time admiring the way he was
wielding the drill hammer. He was actually
singing.
Catching sight of me, he stopped working and
smiled. I went towards him, and, leaving his
mate, he came to me.
I remarked on his changed appearance, and said
I was pleased to see he enjoyed his work so much
as to sing over it. “ You would sing,” he said,
“if after years of this life you saw a chance
of escape. No one knows lam an evade, and I
have earned 1000 francs since I came here. A
week ago I told the manager I wanted a month’s
holiday, and, so as not to arouse his suspicion, I
said if he would give me half of the money due
me, the balance could stand over till my return.
But, no, the good manager would not hear of it.
‘Come to the bureau on Saturday,’ he said, ‘ and
I will pay you your 1000francs.’ And to-morrow
is Saturday, Monsieur. Listen, two of my
friends, evades, like myself, have a boat hidden in
the mangroves a few miles from here, with water
and provisions aboard. They are good sailors, and
we shall certainly reach Australia ; there we can
take passage to France. You see I have good
cause to sing.”
Wishing him all sorts of luck, I left. As I
walked away, the ring of Jacques’ hammer fol-
lowed me, the heavy blows sounding as if he were
at last beating his way out to freedom.
That night I camped with the manager. At
six next morning, after coffee, I strolled over to
the bureau. Jacques was there, waiting for his
1000 francs. Just then I heard the sound of horses
galloping along the road. They stopped. Two
gendarmes entered. The manager pointed to
Jacques, and he was in irons in the twinkling of
an eye.
The “good manager” had known all along
that Jacques was an evade ; but why inform on
him, so long as he did the work of any other
three men, and did not ask for his wages ? As it
was, he had had the use of one of the best miners
I ever saw, for nothing, besides collecting the
25 francs blood money for informing.
If that’s not “cheap labor,” I don’t know
what is.
Jacques, if not dead, is now in the chain gang.
If he ever has the luck to escape, I would not
yearn after being in the ‘ ‘ good manager’s ” shoes.
This is not an isolated case. A number of the
mines in New Caledonia get “cheap labor” on
the same terms and conditions.

A brutally inconsiderate message killed
a fine chance in the Freetrade newspaper offices
one day last week. A society in Berlin—in Pro-
tectionist Germany—reported that 14,000 children
were underfed, and lived entirely on bread and
coffee. Sydney Evening News, for a start,
rushed the news in under some fine big black
headlines. And then the brutally inconsiderate
later message arrived to say that thousands of
looms in Freetrade Lancashire were idle, and 8000
operatives were out of work, and 30,000 were in
distress, in the one town of Sunderland. After
that the “ curse of .Protection ” headlines were
silently dropped.
The S. Aus. National Ass is trying hard
to rope in the Farmers and Producers’ Union,
and to a great extent it has succeeded. It
assured the union the other day that the ‘ ‘ Socialist
(meaning the Labor) party fights the farmers
principally,” wherefore “the farmers’ only course
was to fight Socialistic legislation.” Well, which
joint in the monster’s tail does the National Ass
recommend the farmers to attack first the
Socialistic railways, or the Socialistic agricultural
colleges, or the Socialistic produce export depdts,
or the Socialistic jetties for shipping wheat ? A
few months ago the Labor Premier sent a man to
England to find markets for the farmers’ produce,
to advise them when to send it and how to pack it—
another horrible Socialistic scheme for ruining the
farmers. Perhaps the National Ass. will recom-
mend the Farmers’ Union to cut that piece of
Socialism off, to start with !

FRIENDS.
[FOR THE BULLEITIN.]
Life is a tune to which the keys are two —
The major pleasure and the minor sorrow' —
And, though to-day I harmonise w'ith you,
We may, perhaps, make discord on the morrow.
But should the Great Player tire of majors grand,
And o’er the strings in solemn sadness bend,
And we two still vibrate beneath His hand,
’Tis then I know you are indeed my friend.

KERANI.
The Man with the Saveloy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ONE day of festival there came upon the
lawn of the Flemington Racecourse a
man eating a saveloy, which he held in
his hand, not triumphantly, as if equipped with a
shameless authority, but of necessity.
Deliberately he took his way amongst the
sickly and infirm, attired as if for judgment.
Suppressed laughter, rude and idle comment
followed him. A policeman ordered him to move
on, which he did without objection, retiring to a
seat in a more secluded portion of the lawn.
He finished the consumption of the cheap
delicacy, and meditated :
“ I have created some consternation, and more
laughter, because it would appear to be ridiculous
to partake of saveloys in this artificial environ-
ment.
“I do not agree with their standpoint. I am
hostile.
“On the one side, there is I ; on the other,
there are pre-eminently the law, to me vitiated by
its stupidity and ineffectiveness, and the concep-
tion of these, shall I say cows, composed of
feminists, misogynists, romanticists, idealists,
theists and Pagans, poets, authors, artists, musi-
cians of the inane, apes of mime and gesture called
‘ actors ’; the balance —shopkeepers, shop sales-
men, bankers, stockbrokers, bookmakers, women
and such.
“ Arrayed against me I have what, for my
purposes, I describe as the aesthetic, the ordinary
intellectual and the mediocre.
“ Well then
“ In the first place, I endeavor to apply my
reason, not necessarily to the destruction, but
rather to the revaluation of inventions that are
superficially undemonstrable. They, my oppo-
nents, with an observation that is not individual,
but is imposed by vicious hereditary influences,
appear to have been fed with errors —-that is, if
we are both to be judged by our concepts of this
solitary action—the action, or, more truly, the
function, of eating.
‘ ‘ Let me argue this question of hostile stand-
points with as much logic as I can command, and
try to deduce something therefrom.
“We have two standpoints which, in their
* moral ’ language, are called ‘ creeds,’ obviously
antithetical, the one natural, courageous and true ;
the other anti-natural, cowardly and illusory.
“On the one side, there is valuation, or
measuring, or weighing by intense myopic prece-
dents ; on the other is the perspicuity to decide
instinct —and only instinct.
“To be sure, my reason, which I have
strengthened by study and logical exercises in
order that it may adjudge the value of all formulae,
has told me that the instinctive is natural and
good in ‘ morals ’ ; that all else is anti-natural,
pernicious and false and therefore evil, much as I
should desire to lose the moral qualifications of
merely ‘ good ’ and merely ‘ evil. ’
“ However, I purchase and eat a saveloy—two
perfectly natural actions, in no wise extraordinary,
not a whit fantastic, nor discreditable, nor even
dishonorable ; but, given the necessary environ-
ment, to them eccentric.
“ I shall pass by the action of purchasing, and
consider the function of eating, as necessary, as
natural as the function of sleeping or drinking.
“Asa premise, I wished to eat, my wish being
closely co-related to the feeling of hunger.
“ Therefore I ate, but I did not wish to pose.
In truth, I tell myself that I did not pose.
“ But these, my opponents, antagonistic by im-
pulse (which is the impulse of fear), have valued
my action by the well-learnt preconceptions of
others, rather than by the knowledge acquired by
study, since they deride the effect, while totally
ignoring the cause, attaining, shall I say, a false
value of my action solely by contrast with its
environment.
“ Consequently, they assume that environment
palliates or aggravates action, and, inferentially,
assert that environment differentiates value—that
is, they arrive at false valuation by delimitation of
values.
“ They, in truth, confuse naturalness with pose
because they believe —indeed, are terrorised into
believing—that pose is naturalness—an astounding
paradox of ‘ moral ’ values.
“ Therefore, they are terrorised into an abhor-
rence of instinct.
“ Therefore, they abhor truth.
“And, since they are enemies to truth, I per-
ceive their petty difference between eating a
saveloy upon a racecourse and eating it in the
seclusion of a room; between plundering a
cathedral and plundering a silk mercer ; between
murdering a bishop and murdering a murderer ;
between the crimes of the rich and the crimes of
the poor —I perceive confusion and error and
decadence.
‘ l l further perceive that they doubt the solidity
of such ‘moral’ structures as they have estab-
lished, since they have buttressed them with the
terrifying negative law, ‘ Thou shalt not. ’
“ I perceive that the terror of punishment is
the support of error.
’“I perceive that they live by destruction,
because they fear it ; by the promise of material
punishment, embodied in Hell fire and the exe-
cutioner.
“ Thus the consequent result is a hegemony
of terrorism or class and evangelical parasitism,
that fears for itself, that is there to destroy, that it
may live.
“ How sublime even in its baseness ! How
miserable and unworthy ! And how anarchical ! ”
Having finished his meditation, the man, who
had eaten the saveloy, arose from his seat and,
laughing at humanity, went out at the gate.
J EAN SIBI.
At Wallaroo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
YEAR in, year out, it changes not,
Nor any tree about it grows ;
A great wide landscape, bare and hot,
Wherein no tropic splendor blows ;
A long-dry “river” courses through
The stony tract of Wallaroo.
The station-house is on a rise,
Flanked by a crazy shearing-shed,
And huts, with windows set like eyes
Within a squat and low-browed head,
Gape, from the hillock, at the plains
With widely-staring window-panes.
A relic of a busier past
Within a land untenanted,
Where, dominating regions vast,
Is but one huge, neglected stead
Not fenced, nor ploughed, nor racked for gold,
But old, as all the land is old.
The dwindled flocks are fagged and lean,
From dragging droughty seasons through ;
For Fate, that sits behind the scene,
Has set her mark on Wallaroo,
And, when its old-time owner passed,
A spell of bitter days she cast.
The thirsty earth is stricken bare;
There are no wells along the plains,
Nor any waters anywhere ;
It waits, and wilts, and waits for rains—
As, when its owner came to die,
It wept its kindly waters dry.
Yet when that bitter spell is through,
And Fate has had her sorry will,
Shall sweet rains come to Wallaroo,
And spread green garments round the hill ?
A miracle shall come to pass—
A world knee-deep in waving grass !
Then shall the flocks wax fat and grow,
The gardens bloom, the waggons ply
With heavy bales, and flowers shall blow
Along the brinks where waters lie,
Reflecting limpid skies of blue
That dome green plains at Wallaroo !
VIC.
C. G. A. COLLES.

SOCIETY
Another Broken Hill advt. :
NOTICE.— I wish to APOLOGISE to my WIFE for
what was said, causing her to leave her home.
In Sydney they might go to Justice Simpson and
tell him all about it, and there would be suits for
restitution of conjugal rights, and warrants order-
ing Melinda to come back and fry the sausage and
cut the crib. But in Broken Hill they just “go
down to the paper,” and spend half-a-crown, and
peace spreads its wings again. After all, it’s
cheaper.
THE RIVER BREMER, AT IPSWICH, CHOKED WITH WATER HYACINTH.
They’ve formed a Peace Society in. Sydney.
It’s going to stop war, and when the Asiatic
comes to these shores with a gun, it will meet him
with an ethical principle from Emerson, or a
paradoxical absurdity from some vegetarian
poseur like Bernard Shaw. And the Asiatic will
go right away to his own backyard and be a good
little boy—perhaps.
The Wallace divorce case started last
Thursday in Melbourne to a fairly crowded
house; even the seldom-used gallery was
brightened by a dozen or two of the sex that
always wants to know'. But the later references
to Parisian picture postcards, and such like, brought
Block loungers and Society flutterers hot foot to the
Court, and on Friday morning, an hour before
C.-J. Madden took his seat, “ Standing room
only” was the order. Through the day an early -
door theatre crush fought and struggled in the
corridor to get near the gate of Eden.
Heavily-veiled women grabbed the gowns
of barristers in a vain hope of getting themselves
forcibly dragged inside. Dresses, veils, and even
gent, ones’ gowns and wigs were wrecked in the
strife. The respondent is a pleasant-faced lady,
in black costume, with hat and feathers to match ;
with a suspicion of warmth under the un-
veiled brown cheeks, and with clear eyes that
calmly faced the gaping sightseers. Wal-
lace, old mining-man J.A.’s son, tall, clean-
shaven, good-looking, with a bald spot just
springing up on his head, gave his evidence
quietly, and seemingly with reluctance. Co-re.
Strong was the least self-possessed of the
three. His moustache had lost its jaunty twirl,
his bright eyes looked tired. Probably he was
thinking of Costs. No matter how the case goes
there’s always Costs for everybody. With seven
expensive barristers and three firms of solicitors
to draw their fees, Costs will spend a riotous
Christmas.
How much a woman will expect a Court to
believe was shown in Sydney the other
day, when a wife asked for her marriage to be
nullified on the ground that she was forced into the
ceremony. According to her evidence, she used
to pretty frequently meet the man who was to
become her husband ; but it was always against her
will. Once the man took her to the edge of the
sea, tied a stone around her neck, and threatened
to throw her in. She continued to allow
herself to be forced to meet him. On the night of
her marriage, acting under compulsion, she dressed
herself in an evening gown, and was then dragged
by her bridegroom on to the Manly wharf, and on
to a crowded steamer. From the steamer she was
dragged to a matrimonial agency and forcibly
married. Justice Simpson cast a cold, unfeeling
eye over this story, remarked gently that it over-
shot the bounds of judicial credulity, and passed
the young woman out.
* * * *
News comes of a Yankee wedding at which
two ex-husbands of the bride were present. One
“ gave her away.” (The other was a gentleman,
and didn’t.) The growth in popularity of divorce
is beginning to raise some delicate questions of
etiquette, not only in America, but in Australia.
Is it comme il faut (one must borrow these
etiquette phrases from the French) for an ex-
husband to take a friendly interest in the new
marriage of the lady who once was his wife? In
giving a wedding present, should he consider him-
self a relative, with the right to send a cheque, or
a mere acquaintance, whose polite good wishes
must take the form of a butter-cooler or an entree
dish? Some Australian lady writer might step
into this breach and explain. We know', now,
that a man must never wear brown boots with
evening dress, but on some of these more deli-
cate points of social observance guidance is still
lacking.
That smart Sydney lawyer, Walter Parish,
who looks after the brewery interests, has already
picked a couple of seeming flaws in Wade’s
Liquor Act. That one flaw is real is proved by
the frenzied rush of the Government to cover it
over with a Gazette proclamation. The other, if
upheld, w'ill need a new Act.

A WOMAN and two boys joined the Adelaide train

The public that goes holidaying behind the
N. S. Wales’ Railway Department’s steam motors
doesn’t know what excitement there is a few feet
ahead of it. At the inquest on the victims of an
explosion, the other day, Barraclough, lecturer on
Engineering at the University, said that holes in
the boiler-plates had been punched instead of
drilled, that the design was faulty anyhow,
and that many of the stays were seriously cor-
roded—and, he added, apparently they hadn’t
been removed since the motor was built, and that
was 22 years ago. Another consulting engineer
found “an extensive fracture, extending along the
boiler-plate for 3ft. 3in.” It was an old break,
and must have been right through the plate before
the explosion happened. Coroners’ juries are
mostly very tame; but, after hearing that
evidence, this one reckoned it couldn’t help tell-
ing the Department to inspect its boilers oftener
and more thoroughly.
MRS. WALLACE, the respondent in Melbourne's
divorce case.
Blandolb melodrama is a fool to the
Portland Dukedom case; nay, even, “The Mid-
night Wedding ” seems a mild entertainment by
comparison. The very foundation-stone of the
case is said to have been buried (with other stones)
in a coffin which ostensibly contained the corpse
of a Druce, who was not dead, but duking. Also,
the same Druce is otherwise alleged to have lived
freely and openly as Druce for years after the
burial of the coffin. The law, being made for
lawyers, has thus far refused to unearth the
mystery as to whether he was an honest human
corpse, or a fraudulent hundredweight of stones
at the time of the interment. Anyway, he was
the prologue to the drama. The sequel is
a snowball of allegation, gathering astonish-
ment as it rolls along. A while ago a
governess from Maoriland, voyaging to England
with a Druce story and a diary in her possession,
complained that the diary had been stolen from
her luggage, after which she went into the witness
box with another diary, or extracts from it, and
stated that some of the secret of the Druce-
Portland case was buried with Charles Dickens.
The scene of the next act is laid in Paris,
where the enigma appears to be stained with
blood and strewn with missing papers. A certain
Madame Valette—thenamemighthave been stolen
from a melodrama—tells another long-lost nar-
rative of the Druce who may have been the Duke
of Portland in disguise. Her father, she says,
was the illegitimate son of the doubtful Druce,
and that unaccountable old man despatched him
(the illegit.) on a mission to Australia
to discover his half-brother, the legitimate
son of old Druce aforesaid. Druce, the father
of Madame Valette, came back from Australia
after a year’s absence, and was stabbed to death
by footpads. The desperadoes incidentally robbed
him of his “birth certificate and other docu-
ments.” Old man Druce, being terribly distressed
to learn that his illegitimate offspring had been
found dead minus a birth certificate (and other
documents), did his best to repair the loss by
giving the daughter of deceased a sealed envelope
—not for publication, but as a guarantee of eccen-
tricity, perhaps. Then he went away, and his
illegitimate granddaughter (now Madame Valette)
heard he was dead and buried. (This is where the
slow music comes in.) In 1874, or thereabouts,
irrepressible granddad turned up alive and well
in Madame’s cigar shop, and when she exclaimed,
in French, “Well, I never! They told me you
were dead,” he replied, “ Oui, le Druce est
mort, vive le Portland!” or words to
that effect. Owing to this extraordinary
visitation, Madame Valette continued to restrain
her natural curiosity re the sealed envelope. The
silence of its contents remained unbroken for the
next 33 years. She went on guarding her sacred
trust as faithfully as a dog—a very long-lived sort
of dog. Her father had always carried his birth-
certificate in his pocket, and she likewise clung to
the sealed envelope with inherited tenacity. But
now she proposes to hand it over to claimant
Druce, the legitimate half-brother, whom her
father quested in Australia so many years ago,
when she was young and charming. Madame is a
sort of Little Buttercup coming to the rescue with
a secret in her hand. The drama ought to end
here, by rights. King Edward should walk on
and greet Druce from Australia as the real Duke
of Portland, whilst the hitherto-Duke jumped
jauntily into Druce’s cast-off clothes, like Captain
Corcoran, in “Pinafore.” But the case threatens
to continue as before.

What is S. M. Herald coming to ? Are
law ’n’ order, King and Constitution, or even
Calico Jimmies safe from Mr. James’s lately
developed irreverence ? In a leading article on
the new Director of Agriculture the one-time
official-loving journal bursts out: —
This country in the past has been far too over-ridden
by Civil servants and the foolish traditions of their class.
We seem from the first to have been engaged in a ridi-
culous experiment to acclimatise a sort of mandarin class,
from the Judge on his bench to the humblest tramguard
or the postal official who delivers letters at his window.
“The Judge on the Bench!” The State
Governor!—the Governor-General! —where will
the new iconoclasm end ?
Chamberlain’s Tablets are indispensable to
all medicine chests. They aid digestion, promoto
the appetite, stimulate the liver, keep the bowels
open, and restore all the internal organs to a

The Federal Government has a standing
offer of £5O as a reward for information leading to
the conviction of persons attempting to smuggle
opium into Australia. Recently the authorities
at a Queensland port received intimation of the
arrival of a consignment, and seized about 20 tins.
The reward was paid. On opening the tins, they
were found to contain treacle ! The heathen
Chinee is peculiar.
* * * *
“ Hiphen ” : Truly the soulless trader will
put a thing of beauty to any base use. In a
surgical instrument maker’s shop window I beheld
last week a replica of the Milo Venus. The lady
looked as if she had come out of a railway smash
or a Women’s League meeting. On one leg she
wore a rubber stocking for the frustration of vari-
cose veins; t’other was decorated with a knee-
pad, two rheumatic-bands, an ankle-strap, four
corn plasters and a bunion banisher. Rouiid her
divine waist was an electric belt, and attached
thereto a treatise on nervous debility and the only
sure method of eradicating spots before the eyes.
Her peerless bosom was hid beneath a chest pro-
tector. The perfect column of her throat sup-
ported a teething band. Over one eye dangled a
green shade, while the perfection of her Grecian
nose was hid behind a trouser-stretcher-looking
arrangement for the reformation of straying pro-
bosces. There was nothing on her arms, as this
particular Venus has none.

In the matter of deaths under chloroform,
Melbourne keeps up its tally with deadly con-
sistency. In the latest case a patient died while
having some teeth extracted, and medical opinion
at the inquest revives a question, that shouldn’t
be allowed to drop into the background,
whether sufficient attention is given to the proper
training of medical students in the administration
of the drug. Coroner Cole, himself a doctor, is

Nauseous and mostly unprintable are the
details of the evidence published in the Paris
papers of the military scandal at Berlin, arising
out of the great libel trial. The plaintiff, Yon
Moltke, was till quite lately Commandant of
the Berlin Garrison, A.D.C. to the Kaiser, and
one who wielded immense influence at Court.
Prince Zu Eulenburg, Moltke’s friend, was once
Ambassador to Austria, and is a man of immense
ability. Moltke, actat 60, is a battle-scarred
veteran of the Eranco-Prussian war; Eulenburg
is 57. The members of the clique to which these
men belonged—-the “ Liebenberg.” or “Round
Tablets ’’—referred always to the Kaiser as
“oherie,” “das liebchen” ( Anglice , “darling”);
and claimed that they could influence him abso-
lutely. Von Moltke was “ der suesse ”(“ sweet-
est”). Von Moltke rouged freely and reddened
his lips ; so did Eulenburg. The two went to the
theatre together and “sat in fauteuils eating
chocolates with their cheeks painted.” Von
Moltke addressed Eulenburg in letters as “ My
Darling,” “My best-loved one.” And so on. The
witnesses are, for the most part, gigantic Prussian
cavalrymen, who clank majestically into the
Court, salute gravely, and at once, with utter
impassivity, reel off these astounding allegations
about rouged warriors ascribing to them all the
decayed nastiness of the later Roman Empire.
THE KANGAROO!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Why not be patriotic, and make kangaroo soup and
kangaroo haunch common on our tables I—Bananaland
weekly.
Now send your kickshaws back to France,
And let your alien dishes flee.
That joints of native venison
May deck our tables merrilee ;
Let ne’er a turkey mount the board,
Set free your fancy feathered crew,
While patriots hail, in sweet accord,
Our chiefest dish —the Kangaroo !
Long have we passed him heedless by,
While roasted beeves had pride of place,
While mutton-chops enchanting rose
To lend our board an ovine grace ;
While we to fish from Yankee port,
And potted fleshes—Yankee too—
In mad submission paid our court,
Forgetful of the Kangaroo.
We knew—-one time the legend ran —
His tail would make a soup divine ;
But we could merit see in naught
But English dish and foreign wine ;
Our heathen blindness now is past,
And visions open on our view
Of gastronomic vistas vast
That rise around the Kangaroo.
Now tell me not of Highland deer,
Prate not of pheasant fair to me ;
No charm is left in lordly cheer,
Of calipash or calipee ;
Away, ye grov’lling porkers all!
I loathe you like the frog ragout
They prize so dear in dainty Gaul —
For Io 1 he comes—the Kangaroo !
Adieu ! ye leathery fowls of spring
(Ye were, I fear, a wintry crew).
Good-bye, good-bye, O Christmas goose
(A tough tradition, faith, were you).
Depart, imported crab and prawn,
And all the mystic pott d crew :
We see a day portentous dawn—
This is the Era Kangaroo !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
THE DISHONESTY OF JONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With Jones a treasure hunt I organised,
’Twas for our local chapel’s wilting cause ;
The elders viewed the scheme with seemly joy,
Nor suffered thought of guile to give them pause.
The entries came in at a goodly pace ;
For fifty quid we made the voucher out.
And then with Jones I sought a lonely dell,"
And hid the screed, as though ’twere meant to sprout.
All through three weary days the church-folk searched,
Nor found a thing. Fear entered in my bones.
Anxious, I sought the bank and learned the worst;
I’d cached the voucher safely—so had Jones.
N.S.W.
“ 10-2-4.”

Cheerful to notice the wide range of in-
terests covered by a Sydney meeting, the other
day, to protest against further alienation of Port
Jackson foreshores and to plead for the resump-
tion of some of the harbor’s fringe which has
already passed into private hands. “ Sosh ” and
“ Anti-Sosh” rubbed shoulders amicably, and
united in resolutions affirming the superiority of
public over private interests in the matter. Is it
that Sydney at last, in spite of its lassitude, in
spite of the poison of its anti-Australian press, is
beginning to grow a civic soul?
A * * *
Pending Mr. Crick’s appeal to the Privy
Council against the decision of the N.S.W.
Supreme Court striking him off the roll of
solicitors, he has engaged not to attempt to
practise before the High Court. This was stated
the other day when he was cited before that
Court to show cause why the decision of the
N.S.W. Court should not be endorsed in the
High Court. The Australian Judges, in their re-
marks, seemed to indicate that in such cases their
attitude would be to endorse decisions of the State
Courts without question, unless some special
reason to the contrary were shown.
RUBBING IT IN.
DIVES: "Damme, sir, why should I
remember the poor ?”
LAZABUS: “Because in this Democratic country
you can never tell when one of us may
become rich.”
In the Divorce Courts peeps out the Aus-
tralian casualness. One lady told the staggered
Sydney Court the other day how her husband, in
response to persistent badgering, had at last con-
descended to provide her with a home. It was
dark when she arrived, and he met her at the
gate, and showed her into a slab hut containing one
room and a kitchen furnished with a bed and a
stretcher. He merely swung the lantern round,
gave her one fleeting glimpse of the “home,” and
then showed her out again. She got a decree.
Several ladies, having tried matrimony, just wrote
to their husbands that they were disillusioned.
One said she would not live with him if his hair
hung with diamonds, and that she was going on
her own. Another wrote her abandoned spouse
that she was “full” and would sooner “ graft ”
than keep house for him ; while a third said she
wasn’t coming back and he could do as he pleased.
Sixteen years ago an Act was passed in
Victoria amalgamating the two branches of the
legal profession. This was done in the interests
of the public. It was held that the farce
of instructing a barrister to plead in court added .
materially to the cost of litigation without in any
way benefiting the litigant. The barristers at
once formed an inner union among themselves,
and determined that they would not accept briefs
with “amalgams”—men who practised both as
solicitors and barristers. The Judges, all having
been drawn from the ranks of the barristers, also
set their faces against the appearance in court of
the “amalgam.” These things have effectually
defeated the purpose of the Amalgamation Act.
Now the barristers, of Victoria. New South Wales
and Queensland have drawn up an agreement
under which they bind themselves not to appear
in the High Court, except with members of the
Bar; “amalgams” are barred. The lawyer
evidently is above the Law.
* * * *
Writers of up-to-date Australian fiction
will soon have to erase the aboriginal corroboree
from their list of “props.” Our black brother
progresses. The fantastic war-paint and spears
and clashing shields and weird fire-light against
the sombre background of eucalyptus; the eerie
chanting and tremendous saltatory gymnastics of
warriors; the antique ’possum supper—these things
pass, and, lo ! the waxed ballroom floor, electric
light, and evening costumes. Clarence River
(N.S.W.) aborigines recently held a “social,”
“admission, Is.; whitefellows, 2s. [observe the
‘color line’]. Ladies cordially invited ; supper
provided.” The ladies wore smart, up-to-date
“confections” (colored silk was popular); gents
appeared in dancing-pumps, gloves, and other
de niggewr toggery ; programme included the
waltz, lancers and schottische; and the sound
of revelry by night went on until rooster-
crow. A mere white hireling operated on
the piano, and another supplied supper, which
wasn’t baked ’possum.
* * -k *
The first J apanese vessel commanded by a
Japanese master passed in through Sydney Heads
a few days ago, and a shiver shook the white mer-
cantile marine. Jap trading vessels are highly
subsidised by the Jap Government, while the Jap
sailorman is paid far less than the white appren-
tice. European shipowners have been already
squeezed out of most of the carrying trade in the
North Pacific, and it is only a question of time
when the little brown man will, if unhindered,
claim the whole South Pacific as his sphere.

Dear B.—l have been studying the
posters, scattered by the Westralian Government,
over various farming districts of Great Britain.
Here in Hampshire, where there is a big
rural population, the street walls are yellow
with W.A.’s emigration “ dodgers.” There
is one about four-feet square, printed in
three colors, depicting a selector’s home.
The home has a 50ft. frontage, with two
pieces of wire and some saplings to keep
out the brumbies and alligators. A
6xB tent crowds the front of the
selection, with Bill lying at his ease
in aCunarder deck chair. Three
very fat cows are studying Bill
from the side of the tent, not-
withstanding the fact that the
selection is strewn with ripe
cabbages and asparagus. The
neighboring bush is the abode
of wild fruit and cocoa-nuts
and cheese-like water-melons,
done in two colors all
wild. The Hampshire far-
mer smiles as he passes
Westralia’s poster ad. Pro-
bably he knows more about
cow and fruit and the size
of a holding than the
poster artist employed by
theW.A.G. The Canadian
litho work, usually exhi-
bited side by side with the
Australian, is excellently
displayed, and the boss
artist has learned how to
conceal his icebergs in a
masterly way. At present
the Australian posters here
seem to have been designed by
some slop printing firm in the
East End.—Alba.
Time plays its pranks. This
country’s natal breath was heavy
with the odor of British rum. Once
its legal tender was British rum.
Rum still has its influence. But Aus-
tralia now supplies the rum. Bunda-
berg (Queensland) distillery last week
sent Sydney Admiralty authorities 4500
gallons of rum as a first instalment of
20,000 gallons, ordered for Britain’s Asiatic fleet.

The youthful housebreaker who lately
toured the Melbourne suburban districts with
great success, though he was said to have pro-
claimed his identity by his “finger-prints” at
every house of call, has now implicated another
youtn in the robberies. This other youth was
also an escapee from the Salvation Army Home
at Bays water (where bad boys are converted to
righteousness), and the two early Christians, it is
alleged, went marauding together. If that is so,
it suggests that one fellow’s finger-prints are the
same as another’s for purposes of identification,
when a detective has a notion as to whose fingers
were likely to have been at work.
• * * «
The Rev. Fordyce, of Sydney, has his
own solution of the Yellow Peril. “ Support the
work of the evangelisation of China. Bring the
Chinese and Japanese under the influence of
Christianity, whereby the peril will be dispelled.”
Perhaps. But The Bulletin has its doubts.
Japan was no Peril at all in the days when it was
absolutely heathen. Now, when it has reached
the point that the Diet decided only by a, very
narrow majority against the national adoption of
Christianity, it is a very real Peril indeed.
Theoretically, the religion of the Man of Peace
should have the effect Dr. Fordyce claims ; prac-
tically, it hasn’t. Perhaps one day it will when
the Christian priests and parsons learn, them-
selves, the lesson of peace.
The more we master the forces of nature, the
more do our chances of accidents multiply, even as the
tamer’s dangers increase in proportion to the number of
wild animals which he “puts through their tricks” in
the cage. Formerly, we avoided the contact of these
forces °as much as possible ; to-day, they have gained
admittance to our household. And so, notwithstanding
our more prudent and peaceable manners, it happens to us
more often than to our fathers to look pretty closely upon
death. The South British Insurance Company of 12
Bridge Street, Sydney, issues policies covering all classes
of accident and seventeen (17) specified sicknesses for
£2 2s. per annum upwards—special policies for your
special needs.*
BEHIND THE SCENE.
JULIET: “ It was the nightingale and not the lark
That pierc’d the fearful hollow of thine ear;
Nightly she sings on yon pomegranate tree,
Believe me, love, it was the nightingale.”
Yet Loong is a Chow, and a prominent
citizen of Wolfram . Creek, N. Territory. He
keeps a store there. Till last week he was re-
puted a character almost saintly. It was the
fashion among local evangelists to point out Yet
Loong to the white transgressor as a Morally
Beautiful Thing. When the anti-Opium Crusade
started, Loong helped to get a petition drawn up,
praying for the stoppage of the importation
of the drug into the Territory. Later,
he wrote to the papers protesting bitterly
against “the injustice of certain insinua-
tions that merchants were helping smuggling.”
Consequently it amazed Customs-officer Cleland
considerably when he unearthed (26/11/’O7) 30 tins
of opium from a case of yams consigned to Yet
Loong. Yet Loong is still a prominent citizen of
Wolfram Creek, but as a moral object-lesson he ha s
slumped horribly. Of course it may be only base
suspicion, and the opium might have been sent to
Yet Loong’s stainless store by Muck Hi with evil
intent —allee same as in melodrama. But Yet
Loong is very much under suspicion.
Australia follows very slavishly in the
track of British follies. Partially-perpetrated
Limericks, of a low order of merit, disfigure many
of our papers at the present time. This foolish-
ness started in England a few months hack, be-
came pestiferous, and is now moribund from
natural causes. Because Pansy Montague, dis-
guised as a very hairy “Lady Godiva,” self-
advertised slowly through Coventry six weeks
agone, a stout young local woman is also im-
pelled to swathe herself in unconvincing false hair
and a mackintosh, and play the same game at
Bondi. Two years since no backyard in the United
Kingdom was immune from treasure-hunters,
after the sums hidden by newspapers which made
up for the slackness of their interest by the
feverishness of their advertising. Sure enough.
Wonderland City (Sydney) has started this awful
practice within its precincts; and it seems liable
to spread. Also, Diabolo, which is the latest
silly British game, rapidly infects Australia.
Can’t we at least invent an occasional folly of
our own ?

“C.C.”: Be Limerick competitions (B.
21/11/’O7). There is no further doubt in my mind
as to the absurdity of the “money for brains”
aspect of the gamble. I know two men who sent
in the same line to conclude a recent Limerick.
One of them drew £5O 2s. 9d.; the other wasn’t
even placed among the ICO consblation-priy.e
winners. In the “ missing word” competitions a
competitor knew why he failed to score a prize.
He was either right or wrong in his word. If
right, he got his share of the plunder, even
though his paper was at the bottom of the hat
after the shaking. It was a game of skill. What
could Wade do now if some newspaper revived
it? It is less of a gamble than the “brainy”
Limerick raffle.
The “ Limericking ” childishness has got
down to the bedrock imbecility of this newspaper
birth notice in the Cold Country :
A story to 3'ou I will tell,
Of Mr. and Mrs. Foil well;
On Saturday morn
A baby was born,
Young Teddy—as sound as a bell.

A BEAT’S PHILOSOPHY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I do not see why man complains,
I cannot understand.
If woman wants to hold the reins,
To take the whole command,
And so extend her bloomin’ sphere,
It ain’t for me to interfere.
She longs to figure in the law,
Likewise in politics.
All right—l wouldn’t care a straw
If she was makin’ bricks,
For buildin’ up the nation’s halls,
Or on the ’ighway breakin’ spalls.
I scorn the bloke what splits his neck,
And chucks his ’ands about,
Because the softer blessfed sex
Is crowdin’ workmen out
Of all the kinds of jobs that’s known,
To do the yacker on her own.
A proper ladies’ man am I;
That’s why I’m lyin’ here,
Admirin’ of the lovely sky,
And drinkin’ bottle beer.
I’m not a tyrant nor a jay—
I’m lettin’ woman have her way !
VIC.
SNELL.
LOVE’S GENEROSITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have made dresses for the races, and supplied the
trimmings, and had to go and beg for my earnings. One
person told me (her husband, who delighted in seeing
his “ fairest of the fair ” decked, stood by) that she had
no money.— Letter to the Age (11/11/'o7).
Go on, beloved Fuchsia May—go on
And slap the shiny silks upon your back
In splendid folds and frills ! Shove on ehifion
And all the sweet, expensive things you lack,
In breathless hose and wild, seductive skirts !
Go on —loud flounce without and frill within !
Spare not, beloved Fuchsia —though it hurts
Like billy-o, someone must pay ! Slob in !
O, lovety shoe ! O, tender sweep thereon
Of dust-bedraggled frock ! O, limb-wrap fine
That shows the alluring flesh beneatli—the scone
And palm are thine, my love, fair Fuchsia mine !
Go on ! Stack up the bills—a goodly pile !
Although it’s hard enough to make your keep,
I like to see good clothes behind your smile.
At least we’ll get your pretties on the cheap !
Rip out the reeking plume, my sweetest own,
And flench the skin from off the screaming beast —
He has no friends ; and with good Featherbnne
’Stablish your figure, north and south and east
And west—-you want it all ! Nor drop a tear
For me ! I’m but a mere, hardworkin’ man.
I have no cash ; but there —what matter, dear !
Someone must pay—just rake in what you can !
VIC.
FRANK HENTY.

In the half-year to the end of March the directors made a
contribution of £15,000 to the Buckland Fund, for the
benefit of officers of the bank : in the foregoing, this
amount has been deducted from profits, and that partly
explains the long jump shown between the profits of the
past two half-years. During the half-year an addition of
£425,400 has been made to the capital, bringing it up to
£2,425,400. The total capital to be raised by' the issue is
£500,000, so that on September 30 £74,600 remained un-
paid. The new capital at the present distribution carries
three months’ dividend. This new capital having been
received by instalments, it is impossible to state abso-
lutely what the earnings for the half-y'ear were, based on
shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves); but, taking
the new capital as being available for three months, the
result compares with that of previous terms like this :
Mar., 1904 .. £7 210 p.c. Mar., 1906.. £7 10 3 p.c.
Sept., 1904.. 7 3 0 „ Sept., 1906.. 7 910 „
Mar., 1905.. 71010 ~ Mar., 1907.. 7 4 1 „
Sept., 1905.. 7 8 8,, Sept., 1907.. 71210 „
For the previous half-year the earnings showed £8 Is. Bd.
percent per annum before deducting the £15,000 handed
to the Buckland Fund.
The capital was no doubt increased for two
reasons—(l) to allow of a greater distribution of profits
without nominally increasing the rate of dividend bey'ond
10 per cent. ; (2) to offer greater protection to creditors,
the liabilities having grown to eight times the total of
shareholders’ funds. There has, however, been such an
increase of deposits during the year that the liabilities
still amount to over £7 12s 6d. for every £1 of capital and
reserves, in spite of the increased capital. This compari-
son shows how the various items have moved in the 12
months:—

The proportion of liquid assets to liabilities is now just
over 44 per cent. ; and that appears to be unnecessarily
large. In other words, on its present resources, it
appears that the bank could safely take a considerably
larger advance business if it were offering. Probably
that business has not been pushed, in view of the fine
return which call money has been commanding in
London and the uncertainty of the season in Australia ;
while it has also to be remembered that a large amount of
money has only very' lately come into the bank’s hands.
This and other Australian banks cannot expect to reap
again for many a day such a rich harvest in London. In-
stead, they will now suffer the reaction—metals, for
instance, have already slumped badly, and the outlook
for wool, though bright, isn’t what it was a while ago.
But while this must have its effect on banking profits, it
will be seen that the 8.N.5.W., on account of its very
strong holding of coin, etc., is in a position to benefit by
any expansion of business and a call for more accom-
modation.
So far as it has been possible to judge, the Bank
of N. S. Wales had, of recent years, not adopted the sys-
tem of writing down assets hard, and thus practically
disguising some of its reserves. Its premises account, for
instance, grew steadily from £655,000 at Sept., 1902, to
£715,000 at March, 1906. During the past year, however,
the item has been written down (out of profits, presum-
ably—it is hardly likely that premises have been sold),
and now premises stand at £690,000, or £25,000 less than
they did at Sept., 1906 ; £lO,OOO has come off in the past
six months. This amount is not included in the profits
shown in the table at the top of this.

In 1893 this bank met the common trouble of the time,
and, like so many others, did not realise how bad the
trouble really was. There were 45,000 shares ; £lO per
share was written off (£450,000), £l2 10s. was called up,
and later another £lO was written off, leaving the shares
£l2 10s. paid as at present. Reserves (about £300,000)
were wiped out, and also 1845 shares, leaving the present
issue 43,155. In 1896 a rearrangement had to be made,
with a redistribution of the old liabilities. This is how
the matter stood then compared with the present :
1596. 1907. Reduction.
(1) Debenture stock ..£983,547 £983,547
(2) Terminable receipts.. 891,454 Nil £891,454
(3) Pref. insc. stock .. 889,419 889,419

fourth 3 per cent! ; but the interest on this last lot may

After putting £15,000 to reserves, and making certain
other provisions, one-fourth of the profit goes to re-pur-
chasing deferred inscribed stock. In 1906 there was
£BO9l available ; with that, the stock being at a small
discount (£659 on the whole purchase), £8750 worth of
stock was bought and cancelled ; and, on the other side,
the balance was preserved by writing a corresponding
amount off premises.
In some other respects the year’s business shows
up in this way :
1906. 1907.
Notes £41,0C8 £58,865 Inc. £17,857
Bills, etc 471,361 579,439 Inc. 108,078
Deposits at interest.. 1,603,639 1,803,040 Inc. 199,401
Free Deposits .. 1,399,053 1,571,826 Inc. 172,773
Liquid Assets .. 1,886,066 2,206,098 Inc. 320,032
Advances .. .. 4,682,819 4,891,871 Inc. 209,052
Premises .. .. 359,028 351,628 Dec. 7,400
This is as at the dates of the two balance-sheets, before
making the appropriations. The figures show good pro-
gress. The advances have not sprung abnormally, but,
in view of the recent high range of values, that is no dis-
advantage ; yet they show a good, sober increase. The
cash position is strong. The total new liabilities are
£4,013,170 : 55 per cent, of that is represented by liquid
assets. Certainly, besides the new, there are the old
liabilities ; but they are nailed down. That has its dis-
advantages, of course; if the principal is nailed down,
the bank is also bound to pay a pretty stiff rate of in-
terest whether the money can be put to profitable use or
not. It is all right to have this money in good times ;in
bad times it would be liable to prove a bit of a drag. All
the perpetual stocks (old deposits) rank, by the way,
ahead of the shareholders’ capital.
From the profits for the year shown above
£6OOO, to be paid as a bonus to staff, has been deducted.
This system of giving percentage bonuses is becoming
popular with banks : it is viewed probably as “ a good
advertisement.” Mostly, however, the money could be
put to better use by a rearrangement of salaries : the
worst of the bonus system is that the underpaid man
gets necessarily too little of the bonus. With this de-
duction, and after paying the full interest on the old
deposits, the profits show a return of £8 6s. lOd. per
cent, on the capital and reserves.

Accident Branch.
Income. Expenses. Claims. Funds.
Sept., 1907 .. £2477 £1092 £1177 £2OB
This society is making rapid progress so far as increase of
business and funds is concerned. The one doubt left in
tbe mind is as to whether the foundation of some of it is
as good as it ought to be. The quinquennial investiga-
tion, made in 1905, left behind it a suspicion that some
unsound methods have crept in. No doubt, in life insur-
ance, as in other things, unsound methods sometimes
“ come off.” More than one office has reached prosperity
by, for instance, over-valuing its assets ; this has enabled
it to show a surplus that has struck the public so favor-
ably that the job of getting people to take policies
has been simplified; consequently the expense rate has
come down with a run, and in spite of the unsound
method, the office has more than pulled through. The
art of window-dressing is not confined to the struggling
tobacconist, who lures the public with majestic tiers of
empty cigar-boxes bearing choice labels. But, though it
“comes off” sometimes, short-cuts to prosperity are
liable to lead into a bog.
Still, assuming that the T. and G. adopted some
unsound methods, the rapid increase of business, accom-
panied by a greatly reduced expense rate, looks as though
it will land the society on solid ground. The movement
in the expense rate is particularly striking :
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
Ordinary. Industrial.
1898 .. £29 12 4 per cent. £93 13 2 per cent.
1899 .. 34 12 11 „ 84 19 2 „
1900 .. 36 17 8 ~ 68 3 4 „
1901 .. 32 3 7 „ 67 0 3 „
1902 .. 25 5 3 „ 66 2 7 „
1903 .. 26 9 5 „ 66 9 5
1904 .. 221810 „ 5814 7
1905 .. 21 1 1 „ 5218 2
1906 .. 21 6 3 ~ 49 13 7 ~
1907 .. 19 15 7 „ 41 18 7
The rate in the Ordinary department is all the better
when the proportion of new business is considered.
Assuming that the old or renewal business costs 7 per
cent, of the premiums, and that the balance of the expenses
is the cost of the new business, then the latter was
got and managed at a trifle over 80 per cent, of the
first year’s premiums ; and this rate ranks among- the
very best in Australia. Even the A.M.P. new-business
expense rate, computed on the same basis, was 84 per
cent, for last year. Yet the average size of the new
policies was only £9O ; and in the ordinary' course that
low figure should mean a high expense rate. The fact no
doubt is that the Industrial department is used as a
feeder for the Ordinary—that the persistent Industrial
policy-holder is passed on to the Ordinary department as
fie gets better acquainted with insurance ideas. The low
average of the policy suggests, too, that the medical
examinations are probably "in many cases dispensed with.
That would help to produce a low expense rate ; but later
on it may very likely produce a high mortality experience.
A low expense rate puts money into the pocket of
an insurance office at once ; but in a few y'ears
inadequate medical examination (or no examination at
all) may take the same money out at an abnormal rate.
To further divide up the expenses an Accident
branch has now been added. The expenses here, as in
the Industrial branch, are high ; and under the present
wasteful system they can’t be anything else. The Miners’
Accident Relief Fund, of which every employee on every'
mine employing more than 10 men in N. S. Wales is a
member, is an example of what is possible when this
waste is done away with —the waste involved in chasing
men to insure and to keep insured. The working ex-
penses of that fund are about 5 per cent. That is to say,
for. every'l/-paid in, it can afford to pay out about 4d.
more by way of benefits than even this cheaply-run T.
and G. office. Unfortunately', however, the Miners’
Fund is confined to miners, and to certain special risks.
The T. and G. interest earnings are satisfactory,
considering the disposition of the assets :
Interest Earned on Average Funds.

Public securities represent 44.9 per cent., mortgages
21.3 per cent., and properties 13.7 per cent, of total
assets. Public securities, however, include municipal
loans, and, as the Commercial Bank of Australia and one
or two others in N. S. Wales (including the bailiff in
charge of Camperdown municipality) could certify,
municipal loans are not always as good as they ought to
be. Still, even with this reservation, this section of the
year’s post-mortem can be written down as very satis-
factory. #
