When in 1905 this co. decided to go into the accident
and employers’ liability business, it found that, owing to
some flaw in the. old Companies Act, it would be
necessary for a new co. to be formed for the purpose.
The North Queensland Accident Insurance Co. was
therefore organised, and hung’ out a signboard early in
1906. Subsequently it became possible to amalgamate
the businesses, and the welding was finished recently, so
that all the operations for the past 12 months are
presented in one balance-sheet. The paid capital of the
co. is now £75,000 ; and, though it is only during the last
few years that it has got into its stride, the reserves
already exceed the capital. Presumably, like the rest,
this co. has had a bad marine-insurance year ; yet the
profits constitute a record, and, better still, just
over 70 per cent, of them have been laid by as
reserves for the better protection of insurers.
For years past the lay-by has been highly
satisfactory, until now the reserves are 144 per cent, of
the net premiums for the year—a better showing than
the British offices make, on the average. The proportion
of capital to premiums (and, roughly, therefore, to risks as
well) is also nearly twice as great as the British average.
Combining the figures for the two offices for 1906, in
order to make the comparison with 1907 fairer, these
further results are obtained :

The loss rate is high ; this is no doubt because of the
large marine business done —a business that means a high
average loss rate, compensated for by a low expense
rate. The assets are, on the whole, a good lot; of the
total of £170,934,' £36,000 is in Government securities,
and £69,418 in bank deposits ; £34,999 is in properties,
the balance in branch balances, etc. No satisfactory
estimate can be made of the interest earnings, because a
considerable sum must recently have come in by way of
new capital. The total earnings on interest account were
£4146. The rate would, however, necessarily be low
from the very nature of the securities,

(and Div. Tax)
Mar. ,’O7 24,601 24 p.c. = 12,783 617 11,201 176,943
Sept., ’O7 14,528 24 „ ~ 12,783 1020 725 177,668
The Westrali an thirst ebbs and flows with the regularity
of the tides, and this co.’s profits ebb and flow in
harmonv. An amazingly profitable concern it has been ;
and the'Westralian conditions have been so uncommon
that ordinary rules don’t apply to the case. For
instance, a few years ago the co. actually wrote up some
of its assets ; ithad started to provide a fund for writing
down hotel properties, and then it changed its mind,
presumably on a revaluation, and wrote them up again.
All the same, most of the hotel properties have,
according to the accounts, been bought within
the last few years; that is to say, they were
not acquired before Perth and other Westralian property
had its great jump in value. At September, 1900, hotel
properties stood in the books at £18,793 (cost) ; they now
stand at £176,781, less £5312, “part proceeds of goodwills.”
This last is a writing-off which hasn’t hitherto figured in
the accounts ; probably the amount ought to be included
among the half-year’s profits in the above table, and then
be written off, only it isn’t at all certain that the sales
referred to were made during this half-year. The
amounts shown in the table as written off are charges
made against the leasehold liability, guarantee, and
trade account. The provision for depreciation of brewery
plant, machinery, etc., is charged direct to profit and
loss ; apart from amounts spent on maintenance, these
depreciation items totalled £2158 for the half-year. Not
only hotel properties but brewery lands and buildings
(£73,292) stand in at cost ; and this is the possibly weak
point in the situation. The rental return is certainly
large—just about 8 per cent.—but in a place like
Westralia, where values have been inflated by an output
of gold which obviously cannot be maintained, rental
value is no criterion of capital value. The high rent
siniply ought to mean that a substantial part of it is put
aside as a sinking fund for the rapid return of capital. If
it is all regarded as profit pure and simple there is apt to
come an awkward awakening later on.

The co. which has until 1918 to get rid of its old liabilities
by instalments, has not been making much progress
lately,
Property and Cash
Capital. Deposits, etc. Advances. etc.
1899 .. £36,589 £56,029 £94,113 £3828
1902 .. 46,771 42,137 96,525 2846
1905 .. 47,291 35.559 94,478 757

In eight years £22,937 has thus been whittled off the
liabilities ; but, of that, £10,715 has been provided by
capital (meantime, however, £4632 has been forfeited).
The profit derived from various sources in those eight
years has amounted to £7215 ; but £43 had to be pro-
vided out of that last year to cover losses on foreclosed
properties that were sold. Of the “property and ad-
vances ” item shown above, £36,151 represents advances,
but how much of this is new and fully interest-bearing it
is impossible to say. . The total income for the year was
£2028. On the average of the property and advances
this means a return of little over £2 3s. per cent, gross ;
so there is either serious over-valuation or the property
account must consist almost wholly of unproductive
land. On the advances alone, the total income would
show only £5 12s. per cent, gross. The liquidation is
nowadays painfully slow ; and nearly everything seems
to depend upon the future of the market for such land as
the co. owns. And that future is a mystery, so the co. s
prospects are a mystery too. No doubt the advances be-
fore mentioned, helped along by realisations and a little
profit now and then, will enable it to scrape through so
far as the scheme for repaying depositors is concerned ;
but what will remain for shareholders, only the future
can say. Certainly, those who still hold on have the_ ad-
vantage of the forfeited capital ; that and the nominal
reserves will provide a pretty substantial fund for writing
down properties.

Some of the figures in the last line are approximate only,
the amount which will be absorbed by the dividend being
at present only an estimate —the dividend is paid on
“ equation value.” The profits are shown net, after de-
ductions from the conting’ency reserve, which is provided
to meet probable losses. The accounts for the past four
years show :
Written off
Gross Contingency Net
Profit. Fund. Profit.
1904 .. £3431 £lB2O £l6ll
1905 .. 3474 1350 2124
1906 .. 3946 419 3527
1907 .. 4363 1116 3247
Apparently amounts are written off as assets are realised
—they are referred to as “ ascertained losses.” In
that case the net profit is not a criterion of the co.’s
success during the year to which the figures relate ; for in
a dull year, when there are no realisations and no “ ascer-
tained losses,” net profits would show up well ; while in a
good year (as during last year, for example) the calls on
the contingency fund would be large, simply because the
society would realise foreclosed properties freely. In 1905
the capital had worked down to £41,480 ; it is now up to
£49,074 —Bendigo investors seem to look to this society
when there isn’t much in the mining way to engage their
attention. The deposits are £86,479, as against £95,578
twelve months ago ; and the double movement, reducing
the disproportion of liabilities to capital, is distinctly
satisfactory. Even now, however, the co. has non-liquid
assets amounting to £154,959 to be protected by £70,361
of capital and reserves; and, though formerly good re-
serves were built up, nothing whatever of that sort has
been done during the past seven years. In other words,
the dividends and ascertained losses have absorbed rather
more than the profits for the past seven years ; and that
is distinctly not the sort of finance which has made
Bendigo what it is.

The advances are nearly three times greater than they
were seven years ago ; and a very large proportion of
them have been made during the last three years of un-
usual prosperity and high prices ; so it is at least probable
that the writings-off in the future will be much larger,
wherefore the net profits may be smaller, than they
are at present. Certainly very fine progress has
been made, especially in the matter of getting rid
of properties thrown on the society’s hands. That
can be looked at in two ways, though. It means that
latterly there has been an unusual demand for properties
at unusual prices ; and the question is whether the very
large advances haven’t been made on the basis of unusual
prices too. Of course provision may have been made for
all that, apart from the reserve funds, but it isn’t visible
in the published accounts. Beyond this extra risk, there
seems to be no speck on the wonderfully-improved out-
look of the society.

The City of Brisbane Building Society,
which began nine years ago with a deficit of £42, re-
presenting preliminary expenses :

1904 ..260 5 ~ ~ 241 19 64

The formal reserve fund has been increased by £27, and,
though this increase is necessarily included with the
year’s profits in the foregoing, it is probably derived from
the forfeiture of investors’ shares. The business, though
it does not progress very fast, appears to be conducted
on safe lines : there are no liabilities to the public what-
ever, so that shareholders have only to risk the deprecia-
tion of assets to the extent of their own funds. The
society’s own resources are, in fact, capable of meeting a
o-reater rush of business : the advances have actually
shrunk during the year from £5277 to £5034. Queens-
land is prosperous, but Brisbane itself does not reflect
the prosperity to any great extent, in the building way
especially. That, however, isn’t an unmixed evil, if it is
an evil at all. In the past the cities—Brisbane like the
rest—were bloated beyond legitimate requirements ; and
now, if they mark time to let the requirements catch up,
things will be all the better in the end.

The capital is £250,000, all in £1 shares paid, half prefer-
ence, half ordinary. As set out above, £198,000 of
this was represented by the intangible thing called
“ Goodwill’’—which, boiled down, chiefly means the
habit people have of buying the Herald and
the Weekly Times. The habit is, no doubt, worth a
good deal while it lasts, but it is much too in-
definite and impalpable and unreliable a thing to figure
in a balance-sheet for a day longer than is necessary.
Nevertheless no attempt whatever has been made to
reduce it. Certainly, a reserve has been built up ; and for
most practical purposes that is probably as good. This
reserve which now reaches £60,000, in addition to £2294
of unappropriated profit, is invested in Board of Works
debentures and fixed deposits. The amounts shown above
as written off are allowances made for depreciation of
machinery, at the rate of about 6 per cent, per annum.

The Electrolytic Refining and Smelting
Company of Australia, Ltd., have placed their order for
complete boiler-house equipment with Babcock and
Wilcox Ltd. , Haymarket, Sydney. The order, which com-
prises five large boilers, each with superheater and
mechanical stoker, one economiser and two induced
draft fans, was obtained in keen competition with other
makes of water-tube boilers. Babcock and Wilcox have
also received an order from the same firm for a Reavell
Air Compressor for which they are sole licensees in
Australia.*

The co. shows the effects of Charters Towers dull times.
Gas rates, £2929 as at October, 1904, are down now to
£2663; and coal is no cheaper. Fortunately the co.
owes nothing beyond a few trade bills; on the other
hand, apart from a fixed deposit of £lOOO, its reserves
and what it has written off to cover depreciation have all
gone in extending plant and mains.
* * * *
The Newcastle miners have scored the first point.
Judge Murray and the other Commissioners appointed to
inquire into the coal-cutting trouble at Hebburn report
(1) that fire damp has appeared “ at least twice in serious
quantities” in one section of the mine ; (2) that there
have been “a considerable number of electrical burn-
outs”; and (3) “these burn-outs are sufficiently start-
ling in their appearance to justify genuine alarm
on the part of the miners.” Some of the principal
colliery-owners got it into their heads that the
alleged alarm of the miners was all flam, and that what
the men really objected to was the coal-cutting machine.
The miners insisted that their alarm was genuine, and
that the colliery-owners’ suspicions were quite un-
founded; and this report ought to satisfy the public
that the men were telling the plain truth. It is all
very well for an owner, sitting in his office, to say that
things are safe enough. Coal-miners can’t forget that
if things are not safe it is they and not the owners who
will become corpses ; Mt. Kembla disasters are not for-
gotten in a day. “Safety” in mining is at best only a
comparative term ; one manager judges as to what is safe
merely by comparing his “ show ” with the one next door,
and it is thus easily possible for the standard to fall
too low. On that point, it is worth recalling that, on the
question of mining at shallow depths under the ocean, a
foremost English engineer (Professor Arnold Lupton)
wrote lately that the risk taken at Newcastle “ proves
that Australian engineers have strong nerves.” The
other way of putting it is that Newcastle owners
have expected the miners to take risks that would make
English engineers feel very uncomfortable.
_ * • * »
The Broken Hill Prop, is going to have a cut-in
at the custom-smelting business. It can do it without
any large capital outlay, because it has four furnaces at
Pt. Pirie out of commission, the desulphurising and other
intermediate processes having increased the work that
the other nine can do. To what extent the co. will
launch out hasn’t yet been decided; the directors and
general manager Delprat will shortly discuss that matter.
For some time past the foreign smelters’charges have
been fairly low, but the demand for Broken Hill concen-
trates has eased off, and the charges have been pushed up
lately. With special facilities for treating these slightly
zinc-bearing sulphide concentrates, and with a handy
market for much of its silver and lead in Indiaand China,
the B. H. Prop, ought to be able to compete with Belgium
and Germany, even though the latter does offer some
subsidy-assistance to the business. Anyway, the B. H.
Prop.’s new idea deserves encouragement. It has been a
standing disgrace to Australia that, with splendid cheap
coke and coal available, it has permitted its smelting
business to drift to foreign countries.
* * * *
Of the Broken Hill Prop.’s 960,000 shares only
214,000 are now on the Australian registers.
* » » ■*
Putting the half-yearly accounts of the Broken
Hill Junction through the mill last week, it seemed cer-
tain that already lead had fallen too far to allow of the
mine making a profit. The fact struck the directors later
on, and on Saturday it was announced that the mine
would shut up indefinitely—the first of the Broken Hill
main-line concerns to cave in. Luckily, there is at least
£2 per ton between the J unction’s costs of producing lead
and those of the next-worst Broken Hill mine.
At Broken Hill the Sulphide Corporation’s
second granulation plant for dealing with tailings is doing
better work than any other plant on the mine, and there
are several. It is turning out a zinc concentrate going
over 46 per cent., with a recovery of 81£ per cent. It
treats about 15 tons an hour, and gets one ton of concen-
trates out of three tons of tailings. A scheme is in hand
for overcoming the difficulty with the slime-treatment
plant, which was stopped some weeks ago because the
qil in the concentrates prevented a separation of the zinc
over the tables. Whether the concentrate, as it comes
from the granulation plant, can be successfully dealt with
by the" smelters is a question which will be answered when
the trial lot sent to Europe has been tested. But it is so
questionable whether the concentrates turned out at the
slime-treatment plant on the Extended lease will be com-
mercially acceptable in their present condition, that an
expert has been engaged to cleanse the concentrates from
oil before carrying their treatment any further.
* * * *
Block 10, Broken Hill, continues to take risks
in order to mine the rich friable ore that long since proved
the rain of Campbell shaft. After taking out a couple of
thousand tons of this ore from around the old shaft on
the 615 ft. level, all hands were piped to pick up the
moving ground. On the 1315 ft. level the ore body in the
north drive is still full-breasted, but it tails off in the
south end—sft. is the width of a good portion of the
shoot south of the crosscut.
* * * c
The burning of Block 14 poppet-legs is a deep,
dark mystery. The worst feature was, that when the fire
was at length discovered, there were no ready means
available for coping with it. Block 14 will, no doubt,
instal a fire-service now.
Junction North mill was put to its top pace
during the week before the half-yearly meeting, appar-
ently for show purposes, and when the run was over the
Krupp tables were out of repair, and things were loose
everywhere. One of the electric pumps is at work on the
1034 ft. level, and is behaving decently. The second one
will deal with the bottom level, where there is an accu-
mulation of black slush. Just now it will be difficult to
get men to tackle that slush.
* * * *
After a game struggle to prop it up, the market
in Broken Hill Junction Norths was allowed to take care
of itself last week. The collapse badly shook one section
of Pitt-street, Sydney.
* * * *
A cable mentions that Lloyd’s (N.S.W.) made
£32,000 in the past year. It would be more interesting
to know what it is losing now. The output must have
been over 1000 tons of metal, so the profit was under £3O
per ton ; and there has been a fall of more than that
below the year’s average.
* * * *
The disturbance over the cancelled Operator
lease, Wyalong (N.S.W.), still continues, owing to Com-
plications of the new Mining Act. The Warden, loth to
say anything unpleasant to any of the 26 applicants,
passed the case on to the Minister, who apparently will
send it back again to the Warden.
• * * *
At the True Blue, Wyalong, gold-bearing stone
has been followed to a depth of 1360 ft. This is the
greatest depth yet attained on the field.
* * * * *
When William Knox reaches the gate of eternal
rest, he may be asked to unravel some nice little political
and financial contradictions. As they are hard task-
masters at that place, it will be no use for him to wave
his hands and say “ Pouf !” He will be lucky if he finds
the shade of W. R. Wilson to help him out, as W.R. did
so often when in material form. Knox, speaking
through Bowes Kelly to the Mt. Lyell shareholders,
would not agree that it is gambling to sell copper
forward. Perhaps not; but will he deny that the Yankee
co. which his Board has had as its agents was not
the main instrument in forcing up copper to an
unsteady height? Will he deny that that crowd, from
H. H. Rogers down, are written about as the most exe-
crated set of gamblers in the States? Will he deny,
further, that Syme, the Lyell director, has gone to New
York to find out whether the copper, which it was said
could not be sold, had really been sold ? If Symes’ mission
was not to discover this, it was a mere wild-goose chase,
and the Lyell crowd wasn’t likely to send any member of
the Board off on a holiday to the U.S. Knox says
more —that forward selling is the best way to handle the
co.’s copper, so as to provide steady returns. Go to !
Why cannot the copper be refined in Australia and the
product sold on the lines followed by other metal pro-
ducers? Brother Jonathan does not do work for bare
commission. No ;itis a handy thing to get control of a
nice little punch of 9000 tons of copper a year. One point
more. Why didn’t Knox explain how it was, when New
York didn’t sell, that the co. failed to try the European
market at the risk of the contractor? Doesn’t the con-
tract provide for such a contingency?
■w * # *
Horace J. Stevens, the American editor of
“ The Copper Handbook,” is fairly comforting on the
question of the outlook for the metal that he knows best.
There will probably be no boom again for years, he ;
but he looks for a sure level “ near the present range of
quotations,” which at the time he wrote were a shade
over £6O. One trouble is that, after a boom, you don’t
get average prices straight off ; “ some little time will be
required to work off the accumulated stocks,” wherefore
things may be worse before they are better.
* * * *
Smart miner Robert Ferguson humped the
family swag from Lyell (Tas.) the other day, Lyell Blocks
having about shut up shop, owing to the copper slump.
Ferguson managed the show for two and a half years,
produced 1843 J tons of copper and concentrates, valued
at £136,326, distributed £104,000 for wages, etc., cleared
off an overdraft of £14,000, andearned two divs. totalling
£15,000. When Ferguson took it over the concern was
grating against the rocks, and the directors were
more or less breathless. If they could only get a
year’s successful run out of the concern, great would
be their joy, so they said in effect. The shares were at a
point less than “ saxpence ” a time, and Ferguson
hustled manfully until he carried them to well over half
a sov. Meanwhile, Tom Harvey and Tolhurst unloaded.
The former held 84,000 shares. J. Bull, in good after-
dinner humor, bought with avidity; but later on passed
a lot of the shares over to the Paris market. The French-
men have them still. The Blocks will be given another
“ go ” when copper climbs back to about £BO a ton ; but
that will be a good while yet.

* * * *
Chairman Govett, at the Lake View Consols
meeting: “Sooner or later some brave man will have to
tackle the wages question and bring the Labor party face
to face with the fact that out of these low-grade mines
the miners are now getting too much and the mine-
owners too little. I conclude that sooner or later I shall
have to be that brave man.... Whatever the Labor party
or platform politicians may think or say about the obli-
gations of the mining cos. towards the State, it is not my
view that it is our duty to wear out our machinery and
use up our ore reserves solely in providing wages for
miners. At the present time we are earning profits from
the mine at about the rate of £14,000 a year, which is
just 4 per cent, on our capital, and which represents the
ridiculous profit of 2s. per ton of ore treated. If this
meagre profit is reduced, the Lake View will be shut
down ; and instead of earning a very handsome living
some 400 men or more will find they have got no work
and no wages' at all.” So the proper thing, Chairman
Govett finds out, is to pay the men according to
the earnings of the mine. Somehow these people
always find that out when a mine is about worked
out. They never invite the men to share the cream ; but
when there’s only skim milk left, they point out that it is
outrageous for men not tc be satisfied to share the skim
milk diet.
* * * *
Basing his calculations on the ability of the
enlarged mill to earn a monthly profit of £3OOO, the
chairman of the Hainault (W.A.) told shareholders, at
the recent meeting, that the payment of regular
dividends, which ceased last January, would be resumed
soon. When he had finished, somebody asked why shares
had depreciated 50 per cent, during, the past few months.
Finally, directors were asked straight out whether they
had been selling. They said they hadn’t. Really, how-
ever, in view of the poor results and the drop in the
extraction value of the ore, the wonder is that the shares
did not slump sooner and more suddenly.
* * * *
Chaffers Co. (W.A.) recently nibbled at the
Transvaal leases, Southern Cross, but didn’t bite. A
high percentage of arsenic is the fly in that ointment.
* * * *
Past year’s receipts of the Hannan’s Reward-
Mt. Charlotte Co. (W.A.) were £19,502. Of this sum
£18,034 was spent, leaving a profit of £1468. In January
last the chairman visited the mine, and sounded some
of the large Australian holders as to their
willingness to put up more capital. As a result,
it is now proposed to reconstruct, with a nominal
capital of £17,500 in 140,000 paid shares of 2s. 6d. each,
these shares to be allotted share for share to existing
holders. The directors also want power to issue a
further 160,000 shares at par on any favorable opportunity.
By this arrangement it is thought the co. would be able
to pay small dividends, and raise further capital for
development when the money market takes a turn for the
better. These and other matters were considered at a
shareholders’ meeting held in London on the 30th ult.,
but what was done is not yet known this end^
Golden Ridge’s income for the half-year to
September 30 amounted to £17,862, and, after meeting
all claims, paying a Is. 6d. dividend, and placing £2OOO
on fixed deposit as a reserve fund, a balance of about
£3400 remained. Since then an additional profit of £2OlB
has been earned from ore treatment. During the half-
year 7561 tons were crushed for 42460 z., and of the ore
treated more than one-half came from development. The
manager states that it is extremely difficult to make an
accurate estimate of the reserves, owing to the variations
n the width, value, apd angle of dip of the reefs. How-
ever, as near as he can judge, there are 36,000 tons in
sight above the No. 3 level, of an average recoverable
value of 455. per ton, besides the prospective value of over
700 ft. in length of slightly poorer ore going underfoot at
the No. 3 level and not otherwise developed. On these
figures, the mine has a dividend value above that point
of about 245. per share.
*#■*{•#
Oroya-Brown Hills (W.A.) slumped 10s. on the
depressing announcement that an intrusion has been met
with in the rich shoot, and that values are cutting out.
Some months ago, 8., M. and Co., who boss this show,
stated that the present monthly output of £30,000 could
be maintained for two years, and possibly longer. Now
it looks as if this cannot be done. One cheering fact,
however, is that the shoot dies hard, and has an extra-
ordinary knack of beating the estimates.
Chamber of M:::es (W.A.) is alarmed because
the Government th. to frame a regulation to shake
up those cos. that make a pretence of working leases by
letting tributes in shallow ground. The usual plea about
such a regulation operating harshly in the case of cos.
that have spent all their money without getting any
return is put forward ; but seeing that the practice of
gammoning to work leases in the manner alluded to has
become a fine art in the West, it is time something was
done to stop it. No matter how stringent the proposed
regulation is made, the bona-fide co. can still have its
interests safeguarded, while sufficient power can be vested
in either the Minister or the Warden to bring the schemer
and the shuffler up with a round turn.
* * * *
Mararoa (W.A.) still shows a loss on the month’s
operations, but the lldwt. per ton of gold left in the
tailings, coupled with expenditure on new plant, accounts
for this. Good profits should be earned when a start is
made to treat the residues ; but when the whole reduc-
tion works are in full swing, it is almost certain the grade
will come down.
•* * * *
Golden Treasure, Yundamindera (W.A.), is
getting a little newspaper notice. A few weeks ago this
property, then known as the Maori Queen, was acquired
by a Melbourne syndicate, and hastily floated in that city.
Since then a 10-head mill has been ordered, and admiring
shareholders have been shown specimens, and told what
the previous owners took out of the mine. There is some
gold in the Yundamindera district, but unfortunately it
seems to give out in depth. At any rate, cos. like the
Potosi, which tried systematic and deep mining in the
locality, spent large sums of money, and finally went
bung. The Golden Treasure may prove the exception,
but it is safer to back rules than exceptions.
* * * . *
Cumberland (W.A.) turned out £15,671 worth
of gold for the half-year to September 30, but after allow-
ing for cash previously in hand, cost of production, and
earmarking £2OOO for dividend purposes, only £3243 re-
mained to be carried forward. Of this sum £l5OO has
been set aside as a reserve fund ; but this amount is not
enough, and no more profits should be paid away until
much larger provision is made for the future. The ore
reserves are given as 8000 tons, estimated to mill loz. per
ton. Seeing that this estimate is 2000 tons less than the
previous one, and that only 3942 tons have been extracted
in the interim, it is remarkable that the £4OOO odd spent
in development furnished so little return in the shape
of ore reserves.
* * * *
There is still a more or less bitter controversy
about the relative merits of Oroya-Black Range and its
neighbor, the Sandstone Development (W. A.). The latter,
at 180 ft. in its Juno shaft, lately got what it believes to
be the Black Range shoot of ore. The Black Range party
says that the find isn’t the Black Range shoot at all —that
the Juno shaft is several hundred feet north of the Black
Range workings. The Sandstone people haven’t had the
run of the Black Range mine; still, they 1 hink they know
enough about it, and have accurate enough plans, to
satisfy themselves that the Black Range has actually
worked the shoot further north than the Juno shaft. The
whole point is as to the flatness of the underlay. If this
is the Oroya-Black Range shoot that the .Juno shaft has
got (and the Sandstone people are satisfied it is), then
Oroya-Black Range can’t have a long life, though it may
have a merry one.
Hustlers’ Reef is, next to Virginia, the pride of
Bendigo city, now that the members of the Moon group
are in dignified seclusion. The termination of Hustlers’
No. 1 Co.’s tribute agreement by Minister McLeod played
right into the parent co.’s hands, for it meant giving back
to it the richest end of the claim. McLeod in principle
was right, but he needed a blind eye for the moment
over that tribute. Out of the 1327 ft. saddle the two
cos. knocked up £120,000 in divs., four-fifths going to
No. 1 holders. Work further south on the shoot has
proved that the gold makes again as the reef rises.
Hence the jump in co.’s shares. The promise of steady
returns, of course, helped. The co. has done a lot of
shaft sinking since the tribute fell in, and will soon be
crosscutting at 2100 ft. to reach the Great Extended
Hustlers shoot at that depth, so there is room for a
gamble over that.
• « • •
“ You are now in possession of a plant which in
our opinion will be able to treat between 450 and 500 tons
of stone per month ; and, as the stone is worth at least
7dwt. per ton, you will readily see that the mine will
(when the liability on the plant has been cleared off) soon
emerge from the call-paying on to the self-supporting and
ultimately dividend-paying stage.” This pretty story is
all about the Mt. View, Maldon (Vic.), which has passed
through a good many hands since the Lisle shoot
was cut and worked out. Maldon is so given to magni-
fying microscopic traces of gold that hardly anyone out-
side the district now swallows the estimates till the battery
speaks. That is the worst of Maldon. Long years of
punching “ the blue,” as the country rock is called, with-
out dividends, has not killed sweet hope in the bosom of
the old-timers. Even when the battery does speak, and
doesn’t tell the tale that was predicted, they can always
trace the missing gold in the tailings, whence unfortu-
nately it never is extracted up to the local estimate.
Mt. View may be an exception, but it has to be proved.
Anyway, after all the work done at the mine, it would be
foliy not to try the stone. That, however, could have
been done on a fair scale at one of the local batteries,
until it was made clear whether or not Mt. View is
another Mt. Tarrangower.
* * « ♦
Victorian Cornish, which brought Daylesford
(Vic.) out two years back, has retired to its corner. There
was more fiction talked over this show than over any
other mine Victoria has seen for years. Most of it was
talked in London, where, on the strength of gold
being got just when the co. was in a hole, people
were told that a second Bendigo had turned up.
The mine’s yield to date has been 24,0000 z.,
from 42,000 tons, or about £96,000 in cash. Most of that
came from one or two fortunate hits off the old
workings. The co. would have been wise to have fol-
lowed the gold down rather than to have undertaken a
big capital outlay in shaft-sinking and mining luxuries.
Latterly the stone has been unpayable, so money has
been wanted from J. Bull; but London is tied up with a
7 per cent, discount rate and a disorganised share market,
so there are no pennies to heave this way. Perhaps
Minister McLeod and Mitchell, two of the originals, might
help.
* * * *
Alas, poor Warrandyte! One show after
another has shut down, and now the Caledonia, which
began the boom, is reconstructing, so that calls can be
made. This happens, too, so soon after the letters that
the co. was greater than the Long Tunnel Extended,
with its million gold yield ! As the co. has turned out
over £40,000 worth of gold, and paid £ll,OOO in divs.,
there ought to be no need to reconstruct. But the
narrowness of the reef, the breaks, and the risk of the
gold making less in tight country at depth were never
thoroughly taken into account; otherwise shares would
not have been hustled to 505., and a bit more money
would have gone into reserve.
* * » *
O.K. copper mine (N.Q.) reports a profit of
£44,228 for the half-year. Apparently there isn’t ropm
for a subsequent announcement that the copper on hand
was over-valued, as the accounts were not issued till the
metal on hand at the end of the term had been finally
realised. But out of the £44,228 something like £lO,BOO
is written off to cover depreciation, and on account of
development previously charged to capital. The liquid
assets, exclusive of stores (coke, etc.), were £31,980;
including coke, etc., about £44,000. The weak point is
the quantity of ore actually blocked out ; this is put at
22,300 tons—say 2000 tons of copper ; and last half-year’s
costs were £44 12s. 6d. per ton. That is for production ;
marketing costs have to he added to that. The mine
has other ore bodies, but not yet sufficiently developed to
be included with the reserves.
* * » *
Some of the directors of South Gian mire and
Monkland, Gympie (Q.), seem to have got the hump
because prospecting the'western ground has been discon-
tinued for a time. The co. crushed 10.928 tons during
the half-year for £12,520 gold, and the few hundreds in
the till may slip out and leave this old concern on a lee
shore if there isn’t a Scotchman on the Board with a
knowledge of the value of saxpence—a coin that the co.
despised when the Monkland was paying out a quarter of
a million in dividends. There is probably a bright yellow
spot yet in the mine somewhere ; and, anyhow, it has a
manager named Jewell, who works the show to the best
advantage, when he is allowed to.
* * * *
Brisbane has been circulating a rumor that Mt.
Morgan will not exercise the option over Peak Downs ;
but no definite announcement has been made. There is
little doubt that the Peak Downs ore is poor; and
the lode is very small as well. The probabilities are that
Mt. Morgan will not find enough to justify it in working
the property. Yet, if Mt. Morgan rejects it as not good
enough for a flux quarry, the Peak Downs directors will
have a hard job to justify some of their recent state-
ments.
* * * *
The Aerial Tramways which have been erected
for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These two plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated
their worth and ability. Both Aerialways were supplied
by W. &J. Lempriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.
Bleichert & Co., of London, the largest firm in the world
for the manufacture of transport appliances.’ 1 '

* * * *
After conducting exhaustive tests in the con-
centration of the ores at the Broken Hill South mine, the
manager, Mr. Wainwright, has finally decided to instal
the Wilfley type of table, modified in the direction best
adapted to his particular local requirements. The great
advantage with this type of table is that it admits of so
many slight variations which adapt it to meet all classes
of pulverised ores. Provided the pulp for treatment is
graded it can be used to treat all varieties of ore from
slimes up to coarse grades. When treating slimes and
lightly mineralised ores, care must be taken to have the
slope of the table accurate, and a; fine, smooth dressing
surface beyond the feather edged diagonal termination of
the riffles. When treating coarsely ground ores or ores
heavily charged with mineral, as at the Broken Hill South,
the riffles or grooves can be carried further along the table
and in some instances right up to the concentrates dis-
charge end. Mr. Wainwright ordered six tables as an
experiment, with fewer riffles or grooves than customary,
and with some of them—the lower ones—carried to the
extreme end of the table. So successful has this type of
Wilfley table proved that he has ordered 50 more from
the Austral Otis Engineering Company. This same firm
has been placing several of the patent Callow Screens at
Broken Hill and elsewhere. This ingenious and simple
mechanism has solved the problem of grading prior to
concentration.*

The Irish Investor (London) writes regarding
The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society Limited in its
September, 1906, issue as follows: —“An intending
assurant who realises that the stability of a company
depends to an exceedingly great extent on its care in only
accepting risks that come under the heading of good
lives should feel every confidence in the Colonial Mutual
on this score.”*

“ The Plebe ” : There’s one hush girl I
want to tell about. She is squat, and lacks waist.
She will stand by von in all your most lurid
drunks ; her arms will surround your neck on the
longest and dustiest of tracks ; scoffs and kicks
she takes in silence. Many an aching, swollen
head has sought solace on her bosom. She neither
swears nor affects blucher boots. And her name
is Matilda.
“Brad” : The discursiveness of the Celt.
One of him assailed me recently as follows :
“ Top o’ th’ marrnin’t’ yez. Hnd is there e’er a
chance of a job o’ fincih’ til’ day ? Or th’ morrer ?
Or th’day afther? Or this wake, or nixt wake,
at all, at all ? Oi dunno. Oi shpose no.” Then
he lit out without waiting for a reply.

“ Quan ” : Magnetic Island, north of
Townsville (Q,), which was named by Cap-
tain Cook because of the deflections of the
magnetic needle observed when passing it
iii 1770, is not, and has not been for many
years, magnetic; It was still magnetic in
1821. In that year Captain King, who was in
charge of a surveying party in the Mermaid,
measured a base line on the shore opposite it fot
the purpose of triangulating the bay between it
and the mainland, and, in doing so, observed the
same phenomenon as had Cook,
» * * *
“Mallet”: To “ Perish’s” query, “Does
the boy get sick?” (Bulletin 12/9/’O7), can posi-
tively answer, from personal experience, that he
does not. The Australian-made stomach—what-
ever the importer may say or do to the contrary—
is not such shoddy that it cannot withstand the
simple assaults suggested. Remember a time,
some years ago, when coming into money—2s. fid.
—I sought a couple of bosom friends and sallied
forth in search of edible enjoyment. Our quest
led us to the Prahran (Melb.) market, and we
were at once attracted by a display of dis-
colored tins of condensed milk—salvage stock
from some wreck-—which were being re-
tailed at a penny per tin. We purchased
half-a-dozen, and sat on an empty stall and
demolished their contents. We then invested in
a twopenny ice-cream each, followed by two
plates of peas apiece. This left us with a shilling,
which we expended on ginger-beer, apples,
sheep’s-trotters, and sweets; and we finally went
home with that curious feeling of elation and
exquisite satisfaction that I now experience only
after my seventeenth beer. The only injurious
effects resultant were experienced when my dad
discovered the absence of the aforementioned
half-dollar. Being an only child, I was in the
unfortunate position—when questioned concern-
ing the coin—of George Washington : I couldn't
tell a lie.

“ Paul Cupid ” ; Note that all the snake
charmers alluded to by “Abo.” scribes use an
antidote. But one amazing cuss of my acquaint-
ance doesn’t, for snakes simply won’t bite him.
Have seen him pick up all kinds of snakes in the
bush, and carry them home insid.e his shirt. At
any time while having a chat with him a couple
of snakes are liable to poke their heads put of
some part of his apparel and startle you. He has
never been bitten, and he doesn’t know (so he
says) how it is that the reptiles won’t bite him.

“ Nodrog ” : If it be, as “ Janor ” asserts
(B. 21/11/’O7), that a snake cannot get out of a
shaft deeper than its own length, will some
Aboriginaliar explain the process of elevation of a
4ft. snake to the wall plate of a building the walls
of which are 12ft. high and of galvanised iron ? In
the engine sheds on mining fields it’s a common
occurrence for young carpet snakes to be perched
on these plates, doubtless attracted by the constant
clank of machinery.
“The Batcher”: Re “ Batlow ” (B.
21/11/’O7). What’s this nonsense about snakes
and the. earth’s vibration? And what vibration
do rabbits cause on the earth, anyhow ? I live in
a mining centre, where blasting, thumping
engines, and a rumbling cracker, etc., day by day
is enough to wake the dead for 40 or 50 miles
around ; and yet within a radius of 15 yards from
the spot where the ground had been vibrating, 30
or 40 times during the last month snakes were
unearthed ; and the mountains around are alive
with them. The snake troubles little about his
environment, as long as the other necessaries for
existence are handy.
“Kangaroo”: Snakes, snake bites, their
antidotes, and lies, remind me —it was our annual
school picnic. The revellers were replete, peace-
ful, calm, when suddenly Bercival Hayseed
leaped in the air yelling “ I’m bitten with a snake !
Quick ! Quick ! I can feel the poison ! ” The
picnic surrounded the doomed man in a panic-
stricken state. Women turned pallid ; others
cried as they contemplated the youth’s short hold
of life. Hogan, the snake-bite specialist, hurried
up. Hogan located the bite, and with a rabbit
trapper’s knife, amputated an immense lump of
flesh. Then he vigorously sucked the wound.
Percy’s uncle took a hand, and sucked heartily
also. The schoolmaster rubbed salt in, the while
the bitten one yelped agonisedly. At last Per-
cival gained sufficient strength to walk a few paces.
He screamed, “Oh ! oh ! My God ! I’m bitten
again ! The snake is in my trousers ! ” The thing
was growing tiresome. Hogan was once more
called upon. As a preliminary, he decided to
evict the snake, and to that end made an aperture
in Percival’s pants, adjacent to the freshly-bitten
part. Forthwith an excitable red bulldog ant
looked wildly out at the bewildered picnickers. I
never saw an ant meet a quicker or more despised
death than this one did.

“ A. Zelwood ” : Morrissey, the queer little
Cockney, whose notoriety as a snake handler has
eclipsed Judkins’s, had a close call at Elaine
(Vic.), a few years ago. A snake was sighted not
far from the township, and Morrissey, who was at
the adjacent pub, was brought on the scene.
Without the slightest hesitation he picked up and
stuffed 3ft. fiin. of copperhead inside his shirt.
Having returned to the sound of the beer-pump,
he wound up a series of snake-charming feats by
trying to poke the reptile’s head in his mouth.
The snake evidently disliked Morrissey’s breath,
for, despite his efforts, the head would glide on
either side of the man’s face. Impatient and
angry, Morrissey at last caught the snake by the
middle of its body in his teeth and bit it. Quick
as a flash the poisonous fangs were sticking in his
lower lip. Pulling the snake off, and sobered at
once, Morrissey got one of the bystanders to
scratch the wound with a knife. Having rubbed
some of the antidote on the wound, and swallowed
some more, he betook himself to the stable with a
face swollen to twice its normal size. He was
asleep in a few minutes, and in less than two
hours he was back again kicking up a row for
beer. The Morrissey antidote is black in color,
and is suspected to contain fern-root extract,
while the Shires antidote, of which the secret
died with its discoverer, because Vic. Government
wouldn’t pay £lO,OOO for it, was as clear as water.
A bottle of the latter antidote may be still found
in a house not far from Morwell, Gippsland.
For constipation there is nothing quite so
nice as Chamberlain’s Tablets. They always pro-
duce a pleasant movement of the bowels, without
any disagreeable effect.*

“ Hepheestus” : At the Hawera (M.L.)
District High School, paddock accommodation is
provided for the horses ridden by the pupils.
Don’t know just how many humans attend the
institution, but the average daily attendance of
horses is 70. Can any Commonwealth school
show a record like that ?

“ J.G.” : Re Murray cod as a quick grower
(B. 21/11/’O7). Would like to ask “P. Winkle” if
those cod and perch they are fishing out of the.
half-dry dams at Restdown (Rochester) settle-
ment really arrived, by way of the Goulburn
channel. Didn’t they, as a fact, get into the
Restdown dams when the Campaspe rose last year ?
Murray cod and golden perch have, since that
flood, been found in dams miles further away from
the Campaspe than Restdown.
“ William N.” ; Re “D.T.” and the Aus-
tralian pitch. I have often known a bushman
pull up on the road and yarn sanely enough ; then
glance at the heavens, gather up the reins, stir his
gee into motion, and remark, as he moved off :
“ Oh, well, it looks like rain. I must be movin’.
S’long!” This when there is not a cloud in the
sky. The remark that it “ looks like rain” (when
it doesn’t) has often worried me. What is the
spirit that prompts it ? Is it quaint bush humor,
rank sarcasm, or pure foolishness?

“ Edyson” : I came upon him in the Mel-
bourne Domain. He was small and grizzled, and
dried with wind and sun like a mummy ; but
the swag by his side was no mug’s bundle, the /
lay of it was the way of an expert, and I
knew him for one of the men who have walked
the earth. There was a curious light in his (I
eye, and he mumbled to himself. I moved
nearer, and spoke to him. “I’m lost!”
he said, speaking more to himself than me.
“Lost, lost, lost l Lost here in the centre
of the plain. No food ;no water. No
water for ten days. Lost! Lost!” “Non-
sense!” I said. “You’re all right.”
“ Lost! Lost! ”he mumbled. “ Lost in
the sandy plain. No water anywhere, j
Dyin’ of thirst.” He picked up his billy, I
and started scratching a message on
the bottom, where the word “Lost”
already appeared many times repeated.
“Water! Water !” he moaned. “Look
here, old man,” I said, impressively.
“That’s a water tap not a hundred
yards away, and down there you can
see the river.” He looked into my
face, and shook his head. “God pity
us,” he said, “you’re mad too!” I
drew the attention of the police to the
stranded swaggie, and now he keeps up
his quaint fantasy in an asylum yard.
“ Cecil P.” : Rc slang. Dean Raim
say, in his “Recollections,” refers to “let
on” (meaning “to tell” or “inform”) as
“a Scotticism that, in its land of origin, has
passed away.” And yet the expression is still
firmly embedded in the Australian vernacular.

“J.Gr.”: Agree with “F.R.H.” (B.
21/11/07). For street tree-planting the imported
trees such as elms, oaks, etc., are pestiferous.
Bendigo has some miles of streets planted with
elms, which latter are responsible for more im-
potent blasphemy among the local traders during
the autumn and winter months than the annual
sectarian disturbance. Tons of dead leaves
tumble into the open city drains at this time.
Also the said leaves rot and get into the house-roof
spouts, and cause trouble in about 2/r dozen other
ways. Would advance as infinitely preferable to
the elm, etc. (1) the Crimson-flowered Gum (eucal-
yptus fictfolia), (2) the Pink-flowered Gums (c.
leucoxylon rosea), (e. calophylla rosea), (3) the
Lemon-scented Gum (c. citriodora), or (4) the
well-known Sugar Gum (e. corynocalyx ) —all trees
that are unequalled anywhere in the world for
street-planting purposes.
One Tachinotus Ovatus lately created a
scare up North, and the ruffian’s doings will pro-
bably set learned scientists arguing one with
another. At Her vey Bay (Q.), 28 bags of oysters were
laid out at noon on an unprotected bank, and next
morning over 20 bags were missing. Then 1500
bags were laid out, and about 1000 bags were
lifted in the same inexplicable fashion. The
affair was mysterious; no oyster felt safe. The
police had no clue; no “suspicious strangers”
were seen at the local: pubs, and the bewildered
inhabitants were' just thinking of placing the
robberies in the list of unravelled Australian
crime-mysteries, when - unexpectedly someone
made a smart capture, the suspect being ...slain
while resisting arrest. The accused was oft. gin.
long ; was caught nosing around the oyster banks,
and dissection revealed the innards full of oysters
and shells. Verdict of guilty. A fish expert
classed the marauder as the snub-nosed swallow-
tail (Tachinotus Ovatus), which, according to the
books of reference, isn’t supposed to grow to more
than lOin. long. Apparently T.O. is a cantanker-
ous sort of thief on the Queensland coast, for
others of him have been seen sft. and 6ft. long.

CHRISTMASSIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the wife is choppin’suet, and the gals is stonin’
plums,
An’ I'm settin’ in the corner, simply twirlin’ my thumbs ;
I can see amid the smoke rings from the old pipe’s ruddy
glow,
The dead and buried Christmas times of many years ago.
Though I’m gettin’ stiff an’ slow,
An’ my hair is white as snow,
I shook things up a-Ghristmassin’
A-many years ago.
Oh, I mind the trips to Sydney with old Bill (who’s
crossed the Rise),
When the cheques we got from shearin’ was a record for
their size ;
And the marble public houses, an’ the ladies in the bars,
With their hair like golden sov’rins an’ their eyes like
shinin’ stars.
(Little Maude and Flo.)
The pace it wasn’t slow
When Bill an’ I went Christmassin’
A-many years ago.
Oh, I mind the Randwick races, an’ the green an’ shaven
lawn,
An’ the ladies’ lovely faces that were fairer than the dawn,
An’ the strainin’, glossy thoroughbreds—Good Lord ! but
it was grand—
The thunder of their hoof-heats as they galloped past the
stand.
It makes my old heart glow
When I see the colors show
Of the horse we backed a-Christmassin’
A-many years ago.
But best of all the Christmasses—the night of Regan’s
ball—
When I kissed sweet Norah Regan ’neath the bushes in
the hall.
Oh, her eyes shone bright as diamon’s ’neath the glorv of
her hair ;
But ’tis streaked with bands of silver—for she’s sittin’
over there
Kneadin’eggs an’dough,
Bustlin’to and fro,
The girl I wooed a-Christmassin’
A-many years ago.
N.S.W.

“ Agricola’l: Prevented a mean crime
just at dusk a
few evenings
back. A nest
of meat or
gravel ants had,
like good union-
ists, knocked
off work at sun-
down, and had
gone below for
rest, leaving the
manna, which
they had been
collecting for
the past week,
gathered about
the nest. Scar-
cely had the
last worker dis-
appeared,
when, from no-
where in par-
ticular, came a
■crowd of red
sugar ants, who
swarmed noise-
lessly over the
nest. I watched
them carry
away a fair
amount; then I
scratched my
stick rapidly
over the nest.
Talk about the
“Johns” de-
stroying a
happy two-up
- school ! In less
than three
seconds, three
thousand of the
meat ants had
hurled them-
selves on deck,
and were deal-
ing it out to the
discomfited bur-
glars, who be-
gan to feel very
sorry for them-
selves. Most of them got away, thanks to a good
foot, but there were many that wouldn’t be on
hand to sign the time-sheet that night. I saw
dozens of them being hauled below for dissection.
This raiding happens every night in manna
country; and I wonder the meat ants haven’t
dropped to the game, and posted sentries.

“Paul Cupid”: “R.S.R.’s” whistling
method of dealing with rowdy sleepers may be
all right, but I doubt it. Here’s a better, anyway.
Provide yourself when retiring with two pieces
of soap, each about the size of a blowfly, or anti-
Tariff grievance. The instant the nasal disturb-
ance begins, deposit a pellet in the face-crack,
which will gape in sympathy with the music.
The snoring will cease. Presently you’ll hear
other sounds. Heed them not; endeavor to for-
get they ever sullied your pure ears. In case of
failure you have your other piece of soap, but in
truth a second dose is seldom necessary. After
the first you’ll be sure to get to sleep before the
snorer. I claim for my patent the merit of
absolute physical safety. The subject never
thinks you had anything to do with the taste in
his mouth, but ascribes it to a visitation of the
Devil.
If you wish to get well, and stay well, to
enjoy your meals, and not feel dull and stupid
after eating, use Chamberlain’s Tablets, and your
wish will soon be realised.*
“The Batcher ” : “ Jaybell ” (B. 21/11/’Cb)
got a cartload of atrabilious nonsense off his chest
re the bush parson and horse. I can take “ Jay-
bell ” to .a parson who does a large amount of bush
work, and who owns good horses, and feeds them
well too. Same parson is a first-class ,rider and
busliman into the bargain. Also know a goodly
number of others of the same brand ; but happily
none of “Javbell’s” assortment. In spite of
dogmatic statements to the contrary, the bush
parson does a vast amount of good —though he
gets little credit for it.

“Cict” : “Jaybell” (B. 21/11/07) terms
the bush parson’s moke “4s. 3d. worth of Zoo
tripe.” From which I deduce that J. Bell never
saw a sky-pilot’s prad. Have met parsons of all
creeds on the coast, the tablelands, and the great
plains of New South, and am open to wager
unlimited refreshers that ninety per cent, of them
possess horses well above medium quality. At
present visiting the small union temple in this
meagre settlement are parsons from Corowa,
Howlong, Albury, Walla Walla, and Culcairn, all
of whose quadrupeds are the envy of eight-tenths
of their disciples. These steeds are, as a rule, the
products of bazaars, concerts, dances, collections,
and wealthy cookies, and the gentle shepherd who
would flash an animal answering to J. Bell’s
account is dead, or else in the Starvation Army,
whose priests never yet steered aught but a fourth-
rate hack. Admit that the average bush mission-
ary rides like a parcel and drives poorly, but he
also does both long and furiously, and none,
barring the well-fed, well-bred equine he supports,
would endure it. Also the Lord’s representative
owns gloves, buggies, harness, etc., second to
none but his bishop’s.
“Jimmie Pannikin” : Saw a number of
fat sheep eating out of garbage-tins at Barren
Jack to-day. Was informed by a bright yet
believable local man that the sheep acquired their
curious taste when natural feed was scarce. Now,
grass and herbage are plentiful, yet so keen
have the sheep become on meat-scraps, vege-
table refuse, and fruibskins that it is quite
common to see them go up the steep slopes above
the garbage-tins (which are all provided with
closely-fitting lids), and charge them full tilt. A
sheep’s head, travelling at 103 miles an hour—
which is the average pace achieved by sheep on
Barren Jack down-gradients—makes no mean tin-
opener.
“ The Prooshan ” : One of the liveliest
steeplechases I ever witnessed happened when my
two eager dogs tackled a koala, perched on my
back fence. I went to try and coax the innocent
away to a safe retreat; but, upon my approach, he
jumped down on to the younger dog’s back, and
stuck like glue. The dog danced and screwed and
yelled, but the bear sat as firm as if he was a part
of the dog. At last his mount made off in a bee-
line at top speed till a wire-fence cropped up in
the near future ; then, up went the ko. and oyer
went the dog, yelping. While the dog was getting
his wind, the bear mounted a tree.
NAMED!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John Johnston bought a motor car.
He meant to drive it near and far,
But everywhere his motor went—
Poor gent !
A fakement broke, a fixture bent.
It bolted with him down a hill.
The little boys it didn’t kill
It damaged in a curious way,
' . And aye,
The hapless, Johnston had to pay.
It ran into a country church,
And knocked the parson off his perch.
It dashed into a flock of sheep.
A heap
Were slain, and chops were cheap.
It jibbed, blew up; and overturned,
And John was broken, bashed, and burned.
The motor runs that.he could get,-
As yet,
Were rash excursions into debt.
And going out or coming back,
You’d drop on Johnston, bruised and black.
Stretched in the dust; with mind distraught,
He fought
Beneath his awful Juggernaut.
One morning, maddened by the strain,
He rushed his motor back again,
And through the big front window slid—
Hedid.
Which meant “ Per contra, 40 quid ! ”
“ Here take it back in Heaven’s name ! ”
He yelled, I’ni ruined, lost, and lame.
A motor ear ! Itis infamous !
You cuss,,.
This thing’s a blighted Blundkr-’bus !”
VIC.
SILAS SNELL.

“Pick”: “ Yarrum ” has not conferred
upon dogkind any marked benefit by passing
along his tick remedies (B. 14/11/’O7). They are
known to everyone possessed of a canine in tick-
infested localities, and ; are about as ineffective as
the other 998 cures. The well-bred or highly-prized
dog usually succumbs. The mongrel, however, will
withstand an army of ticks. The reason is not,
as some think, that his inferior blood is not sus-
ceptible to the aristocratic tick’s poison, but
simply because no one cares whether he dies or
lives, and, therefore, no one fools around him
with cures. The remedy observed in my camp is
to put the disabled dog in a cool, damp place, and
leave him there till he walks away, or has to be
towed. A sun bath is always fatal.
“ H.W.M.” sees things :
.Tfkr'track was hot and dusty, the billy empty, and
Matilda-heavy, so John (my friend) and I reclined beneath
a tree to rest. A large, hairy monster appeared, snorting.
John, after hasty but minute...inspection, pronounced
same to .be a mad bull—a deadly enemy of man. Mad
bull arrived just in time to meet a large, pronounced
blank, John and I having scooted up the tree in fairish
order. Mad bull ate John’s straw hat, and appeared to
derive great comfort therefrom. Then he attacked my
beaver with a mighty bellow, and bored his horn into the
bowels thereof, and wore it that way, a useful shade for
one horn, one ear, and the upper part of the left eye.
Next he discovered, with pleased astonishment, John’s
gun leaning against tree, with the muzzle pointing up-
wards at the owner. John watched the plot unfold with
consternation. Mad bull advanced cautiously and licked
gun tenderly with tip of tongue, and jumped back to watch
developments. Gun took no notice—then mad bull butted
gun playfully in ribs. Gun still slept on. Then mad bull
attacked gun with appalling ferocity. Gun woke up in
great rage, and went off. John screamed affrightedly,
toppled off branch, and fell with sickening thud on to
mad bull. John, mad bull and sickening thud lit out
with surprising swiftness for the Great Unknown. Went
in search of John with dire forebodings at heart. Met.
charming young milkmaid of 60 summers milking mad
bull, the latter still wearing my beaver. Asked after
John. Milkmaid of 60 summers and more (plenty more)
flew off handle and went nineteen to the dozen, and then
was bereft of speech for a season. Left during season to
continue search for John. Found him camping under
shady tree number two, sleeping the calm and peaceful
sleep of the unrighteous, with a foolish smile on his face
and an empty lemonade bottle by his side, from which a
pleasant and robust odor came.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Sydney last week smiled as G. Reid
gave his assurance that he would separate him-
self shortly from £353 of big notes absorbed by
him in the Myerson case. Assignee Palmer is to
be congratulated on a nerve which could draw
teeth from a shark.
G. Reid is still a bit of a wag. After
having to part up some of the Big Notes he got
from the Myerson lady to the N.S.W. Official
Assignee, he went to the Methodist Young Men’s
Institute, and lectured on “Chivalrous Econo-
mics.”
Tom Bent recollected, the other day, that
he used to sing with Melba in the old Masonic
Hall. He does not say that he was put out for it,
and Melba is too generous to remind him.
William McMillan, who used to be N. S.
Wales’ Heaven-born financier, is likely to settle in
London for some time. The outlook of the
McArthur firm was lately much improved by
some important finaiicial readjustments.
Chief Justice Darley (N.S.W.) reached the
21st year of his service last week. Francis Forbes
had 14 years’ service; James Dowling, seven as
Chief and nine as puisne ; Alfred Stephen, 29 as
Chief and five and a-half as puisne ; James Martin,
13 years ; and J. E. Salomons, 12 days. Samuel
Way, of S. Aus., holds the Australian record,
with 31 years of service.
Miss Ruby Lindsay, fourth artist in the
Ci eswick family that produced Norman, has had
a remarkable series of successes in connection
with the Women’s Work Exhibition, Melbourne.
She secured the prize for a design for a diploma,
she took first prize for a poster, first prize for best
art exhibit in a special class, and has had several
other posters hung in the show, each of which
carries a fair financial reward.
Governor Chelmsford’s “tight place” in
Queensland constitutional history is not to .be
attributed to any exceptional ignorance. His
antecedents are superior to those of the old type
of Australian Governor, who “ put his foot in it ”
regularly without knowing where he was. He is
an M.A. of Oxford, a barrister, and has been a
London County Councillor. He is disposed to
plume himself on his intimate acquaintance with
constitutional practices. It’s probably a case
where full ignorance would have been safer than
partial knowledge.
A son and a grandson of Charles Dickens
found themselves facing one another across the
Bar table in a recent English larceny case.
Died at Kumara (M.L.), Solomon Maurice
Spiers, at the age of 76. He was a cousin of
B. L. Farjeon, novelist, and came out from Dublin
with him in ’65. Farjeon, however, didn’t take
kindly to “gold-rushing,” and soon got back to
Europe.
Florence Nightingale, who has just re-
ceived the Order'of Merit—the brand invented in
1902 by Edward to mark a maximum of two
dozen conspicuous Britons —was nearly per-
suaded many years ago to come and take the
management of Sydney hospitals. Parkes had
conceived a scheme for remodelling the institu-
tions. He communicated with Miss Nightingale,
with the result that that lady entered heartily
into the matter, and sent out organising
“sisters.” The system has been the Nightingale
system substantially since, but during one period
of the negotiations Miss Nightingale thought of
inaugurating the system in person.
An Australian Historical Society man
recently made the inexcusable blunder of saying
that Sir William Denison succeeded Sir George
Gippsin the Government of N. S. Wales, whereas
Sir Charles Fitzroy was the man. A Mr. Lees,
of Paddington, was in the guard which saw
Gipps off and received Fitzroy. Mr. Temple
Nathan also comes in with an interesting note
about the departure of Gipps in 1846: “ It was a
sad procession to Circular Quay. His only fault
was, I remember being told, that he was too good,
i.e., he wanted to save expenses, even to insisting
that letters should be written on half a sheet of
foolscap. This is all I remember of Sir George,
except that I believe he was very kind to my
father.” Said father was Isaac Nathan, the com-
poser of some of Byron’s Hebrew melodies, and a
personal friend of the poet.
An old Monaro (N.S.W.) pioneer has
passed out, aged 99. Patrick McCoy came
“ down by Kosciusko ” 71 years ago, and he and
his wife, who is over 80, were the first white
people married in that district of big white men.
From the “Letters of Queen Victoria”:—
I had a great horror of bishops, as a child, on account
of their ugly wigs and aprons.... I was brought up very
simply—never had a room to myself till I came to the
throne.
Then, including Balmoral and Buckingham
Palace, she had two thousand acres of bedroom for
her private use, and several hundred brass and
gold-canopied beds to lie in. Some civilised coun-
tries have no sense of proportion.
George A. Brown, an ex-Australian
preacher, who died at Lincoln (Eng.), recently,
was well known in Melbourne in the ’9o’s,
when he published a pamphlet, “The Christian
Socialist,” and tried out his theories in practice
by founding an unsuccessful Christian Socialists’
settlement on the Murray, in S.A. After the
burst of the land boom, Melbourne unemployed
selected him to co-operate with the Revs. Canon
Tucker and Dr. Strong in trying to set a village
settlement movement going. He founded the
Church of Auckland (M.L.).
Monaro runs the Hawkesbury close as a
land of long-livers. Patrick McCoy, who has just
gone under with 99 years to his credit, has a
widow, over 80, described as in flourishing health.
N.S.W. possesses a number of rural spots where
vital forces, though apparently stagnant, survive
for remarkably long terms. The longevity may
be partly due to stock, but it seems also to be
influenced by the reposeful character of the sur-
roundings. A Windsor scientist holds that old
age is contagious, and points in confirmation to
patriarchs there who go on living simply because
older patriarchs in the district will not die. “ I’ll
live as long as Jim,” said a Windsor nonagenarian
lately, “ see if I don’t.” At Bombala they wanted
to start a cemetery once, and waited for years for
a corpse. But no one would die. And finally a
corpse had to be imported to give God’s acre a start.
THE CHURCHES AND AMUSEMENTS.
At the annual meeting of the Church of England Association, last night, the committee’s report said that the Anglican Churches were being transformed into
ecclesiastical music-halls. Organ recitals, solo singing, tricky advertisements, and such like, were the-order of the day, while simple evangelical services were put in
the background. —Sydney Daily Paper Report.

Childers (Q.) has a policeman named John
Dunne. So have a lot of other places.
Bushman Frank Hann has returned from
another little jaunt. Last September, supported
by a party and a S.A. Government camel team,
he started from Oodnadatta (S.A.) to the Layer-
ton (W.A.) district on a prospecting expedition.
He had a rough trip, but got through with the
loss of a horse and a camel. He reports that the
yarn that a blackfellow recently brought lOOoz.
of gold into Jinjin (W.A.) is a “ snake,” that salt
lake Carey extends 100 miles farther north than
the maps allege, and that, although he found no
minerals, he is convinced there is gold beyond the
Laverton district.
“Maori Mac” on the man who the
other day left £150,000 to endow homes for
‘‘indigent gentlewomen” and their children,
and some more to endow other homes for plain
women and their kids :
Allan McLean was one of the Shepherd Kings of Canter-
bury. He was also a Scotsman, and a bachelor. With
his two brothers, 500 sheep, and some cattle, he arrived
at Christchurch from Sydney in 1852. He obtained
50,000 acres from the Government on lease, and began to
look around for more. Accompanied by a packhorse and
camp outfit, he explored the then unknown country of
North Otago. The examination was carried out with
Scottish thoroughness, and the result was wealth. The
explorer secured a lease of 400,000 acres in North Otago,
now known as the Laghmor Estate. Later the family
acquired further portions of the earth’s crust, and their
affluence became robust. Allan McLean lived a retired
life in Christchurch, occupying what is alleged to be the
largest dwelling-house in the Dominion. This edifice is to
become the home of the hard-up lydy and the clamorous
cheeild. A Board, comprising bishops and other effulgent
characters, will manage the affair.
Anyone in any part of the world who wishes to
take up land in Queensland should write to ex-Senator
the Hon. Wm. Guy Higgs, No. 35 Post Office Chambers,
Pitt Street, Sydney. Mr. Higgs has been authorised by
the Queensland ' Government to give information
absolutely free of charge.*
“F.”: Apropos Australian natives and
Acting-Governorships (B. 28/11/’O7). Edward
Macarthur, Acting-Governor of Victoria from the
death of Hotham, Dec. 31, 1855, to arrival of
Barkly, Dec. 26, 1856, was not an Australian
native ; he was born at Bath in 1789, and was
brought to Australia in the following year by his
father, John Macarthur, lieutenant in the 102nd
Regiment, and father of the wool industry of
Australia.
It has come at last. Josiah Symon has
come into collision with the courtly and cynical
Judge Gordon, and has taken second place.
Symon’s domination of the S. A. Bench has not
been a good thing for either party, and there is a
general feeling of relief now that Gordon has
said, “My forbearance will soon be exhausted.
Sit down, Mr. Symon”—and Symon has sat
clown. When Chief Justice Way once told him
to sit down, he replied quite pleasantly that
he was not going to sit down, and he didn’t. As
for the third Judge, Homburg, he would never
dream of telling Symon to sit down.
Petitioner Wallace, of the Melbourne
divorce story, is making his second appear-
ance in a cause celebre. Years ago—a dozen,
perhaps—he was a party in the combined
family effort to restrain papa “ Johnny ”, Wallace
from doing as he jolly well pleased with his
children’s money. The young Wallaces inherited
each a bulky fortune from their mother’s father
—the notorious “Tommy” Monaghan—who left
son-in-law Johnny the sole trusteeship of the
estate under stringent conditions as to investment.
Tyrannical Johnny—always up to his bull neck in
mining speculations—exceeded his powers. He
“ pooled ” his own estate with the Monaghan
estate, and played up the money in all directions.
The boys eventually got him under control, and
the shock of discovering that his authority had
departed from him was perhaps partly responsible
for his death. When the boys came of age, and
meekly desired the old man to furnish an account
of his stewardship, he replied by shaking a thick
walking stick at them. So it was said in Court.
“ Larry ” Finn, one of the institutions of
Broken Hill, died last week. Years ago he built
a hotel on a bush track, and the spot presently
turned itself into about the centre of Broken Hill;
so the beer-pump was kept busy. Yet he was
almost an anti-drink fanatic at times, and it is
pretty safe to say voted “No license” at last
elections. A man had to be fairly sober to get a
drink at Finn’s bars.
Count de Rossi, on whose widow’s account
a Select Committee was asked for in N.S.W.
Parliament, last week, was a man of chequered
fortune. His father, a soldier of no mean
achievements, was appointed Superintendent of
N.S.W. Police by the Earl of Bathurst, as,
though a Frenchman, he had taken service in the
British army, and followed the flag over
the battlefields of the period. It would
appear that he was disinherited for that
change of national faith, as the count
title was transferred to a younger brother. The
Australian Rossis lost touch with the French
branch of the family until about 10 years ago,
when it was found that, owing to want of a French
heir. Captain Rossi, eldest son of the Superin-
tendent, was entitled to the rank of fourth Count.
Captain Rossi, or from that time Comte De Rossi,
was appointed Registrar of the District Court
at Goulburn, with a salary of £3OO a year, and,
owing to a Parliamentary reduction of the salary
to £75, he entered on a career of grievance-mon-
gering, which he maintained till death. He
reckoned his losses on that account at £lO,OOO.
Government after Government got tired fighting
his claims, and at last (shabbily, some held)
squelched him by pleading the Statute of Limita-
tions. Then he ventilated land-claims against the
Goulburn branch of the Church of England, and
for many years kept the local Bishop in hot water.
Those who hate nasty medicines should try
Chamberlain’s Tablets. They are easy to tab;
and pleasant in effect.*
Australian Governors of to-day are on the
whole a scholastic lot. Northcote and Chelmsford
are Oxford Ms.A., Strickland Cambridge 8.A.,
and Le Hunte Cambridge M. A. The Sea-Dog
Governors, such as Rawson and Bedford, are also
moderately bookish. One of them seldom travels
without a stock of histories and Parliamentary
speeches, while another devours biographies with
insatiable appetite. In fact, Australian Governors,
like the rest of the world, are being educationally
levered up, and may be taken now as on a par
intellectually with the professional classes.
It is more than probable that the ‘ ‘ prophet ”
Te Whiti, who died at Parihaka, the other day,
was no better than he ought to have been. He
spent the better part of his life in prophesying a
Day of Reckoning, that hasn’t arrived yet, when
the pakeha should be driven from the country and
the Maori come again into his own. Te Whiti
was a great talker and a good bluffer. He made a
lot of mischief in the early ’Bo’s by setting on his
braves to pull down fences and uproot survey
pegs. When an expedition was organised to stop
his capers, he sat in front of his whare, surrounded
by his recumbent braves, and challenged Major
Tuke to walk over the bodies of his warriors and
arrest him. To the astonishment of the “prophet,”
the challenge was accepted. Tuke stepped as
carefully as possible, so as not to hurt Te Whiti’s
warriors, and captured his prisoner without firing
a shot! After getting out of gaol, Te Whiti and
the late Tohu resumed the-collection of a fund
from the Maoris to celebrate the Day of Reckoning,
now overdue. As in the case of ’Lijah Dowie,
there was trouble over money matters, and Te
Whiti and Tohu had such a row about “the fund”
that they neyer spoke again. When Parihaka
sobers up, after the mighty tangi, there will be a
vigorous hunt for the £26,000, or thereabouts,
which ought to be lying about the camp.
The Tourist will appear in Sydney at
Christmas as a monthly outing magazine. It is
to be edited by “Narranghi Boori.”
“Where to Look” (Isaac Pitman and
Sons, London, price, Is.) to hand, is a well-indexed
guide to the most important (about 300) year-
books and annuals, etc., in universal daily use,
including several trade and profession publica-
tions.

HEREDITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Into thought we come, and find
Passions bred for us to rear,
Imps or elves of some old mind
Whose strange heritage we bear;
Fire and fuel, we but feed
Upon what is in the breed.
We are toned and tainted still
In a thousand ways, nor can
Fathom what is good or ill
Since the mystery began,
As we rear the imps and elves
Who are made out of ourselves.
And so into thought we spring,
As a lyre touched by those
Who have tuned each curious string
To the primal joys and woes ;
Brain and body, we but play
That which lives in their decay.
x.s.w.
R. CRAWFORD.
