A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 3, 1907.
My Dear Arini,
Women’s Exhibition has closed in Melbourne
on a high, triumphal note. All doubts about its
success went to roost when the Cup week closed.
It could not compete with the races. Nothing
may except other races. Fifteen thousand
pounds are about the actual expenses of the Ex.
It is not generally known that Lady Northcote
largely financed the undertaking. Her Ex. deserves
the success it has won, and any odd crowns, and
arches, and twined laurels, as well as the flowers
that were showered on her when she left the
building at the close of the show.
A Sydney man, in his reply to the question,
“ What is she like ? ” described Mrs. Wallace, the
respondent in the Melbourne divorce case, as “A
superb spectacle.” A complete and significant
summing up of the rosy and golden lady, as she
was two years ago. And Mrs. Wallace was always
beautifully dressed. Which reminds me of a
bit in a delightful book (from theLocklev Library),
“ 2835 Mayfair. ” The heroine, a girl over whose
beauty and wit London raves, is damaged in a
motor car accident. When she is convalescent,
her fiance, an eminent K.C., says, in reply to her
entreaty to tell her of what he is thinking, “ I
was thinking that you’re not a really beautiful
woman—that you were not, even before
your accident, a beautiful woman.” “You
are perfectly right,” she. laughed. “I was
never beautiful. But the secret of being beautiful
is to behave as though you are. In England, if a
man thinks he is an architect or an actor, people
take him at his word. Perhaps we English
are so stupid that we think the man ought to know
best. As a matter of fact, I’m a very peculiar
type. If I were badly dressed I should be ugly.
When I am well dressed—as I shall be at our
wedding—l shall be beautiful.”
Only a month or two previously the K.C.’s old
clerk, in great trouble, showed his boss a photo-
graph of his daughter, who had mysteriously
vanished. The K.C. noted that she “ wore
spectacles, that her hair was done in an unbe-
coming way, and that her dress was clothing
rather than clothes, and he concluded that, as she
took no trouble with her hair and was photo-
graphed in spectacles, she regarded herself as un-
attractive. A girl who takes that view is almost
certain to be right.” And he thought it
didn’t matter how many of that kind of girl
vanished. Nobody ought to want her. Yet the
girl whom nobody ought to want, and the girl he
and every other man desired, proved to be identi-
cal. Sartor resarta (is that right, as applied to
the she-tailor, I wonder ?).
Doubling back to the Wallace case. Aren’t the
letters in the case almost incredibly vulgar and
silly ? The literary seasons of about two, three,
and four years ago blossomed thick with
love letters, written for publication—and pelf.
Divers known and unknown men and women
writers broke their hearts into their ink-stands,
and wrote around all the convolutions of passion
with a fluid that they fondly hoped the public
would mistake for good red blood. The public
bought the long letters for ready money, but
pretty well everyone save the Alices in Wonder-
land knew that the hearts were of chalk and
didn’t have any blood in them. Still the letters
they dictated were good imitations of the thing as
it might be—five-ply, and the pattern showing on
both sides, like a good carpet. And they were
not vulgar or puerile. Nowadays !—Well, I give
here a fair average sample :
Dear Old Silly Face, —Why didn’t your lumbering pre-
sence blot the landscape to-day, as promised ? I had to eat
my gum-leaf sandwich without you, and I do hate to see
good prog wasted. Fido buried your share. Really you
must know by now that all my affection not already
snavelled by Fido is yours to have and to hold—the whole
lot would quite fill your pipe—once. That fat Bouncie
girl was looking at your photograph to-day, and she re-
marked that you were good-looking—after the licensed
victualler build style—but that you hadn’t any brains,
and that if I married you we’d never be able to keep body
and soul and motor together. She must have sneaked
that last phrase out of a book. All the same, I wish you
wouldn’t let her find out things about you, such as your
not having any brains. Next time she turns her cross-cut
eyes on you, blow smoke in her face and say, what Mr.
Hopkinson said to the Duchess, “ Bunkidoodle.” Your
loving Lop Ear.
That, of course, is not a real document. But it’s
a type.
A tremendous tribute to one of the Ma State’s
young society men (noted in the Wallace case):
“I love you—anyone would love you.”
Petitioner Wallace, of the Melbourne Divorce
suit, is a youtig giant of six feet three, a great
sportsman. The Bona, an auxiliary schooner
craft of 80 tons, was built in Sydney for him
while the handsome brace were staying here.

Dailey to Mr.! J. Chester. He is good-looking,
employed by a shipping firm, and a junior officer
in the Garrison Artillery (militia).

the Sydney public what is proclaimed by local
cognoscenti to be a new Voice. Miss' Ruby
Adams, one of Sydney’s prettiest girls, is the

For the Kite-Wolstenholme alliance, on Thurs-
day last, Bathurst woke up, rubbed a tired,
collective eye, and drove out to the quaint, old
historic Kelso Church. The newly-wedded pair
scrutinise the sea-waves down Pittwater way for a
while, and thereafter settle permanently at
Mount Tamar—which was once a McPhillamy
home.

An English paper describes with gorgeous detail
the marriage of Mr. George G. Garrick (only son
of the late Alfred Garrick) and Miss Joyce Reid,
only daughter of Mr. Russell Reid, of Yernon
Gardens, Brighton. Bridegroom’s parents were
former residents of Hunter’s Hill.
It must be the great percentage of moisture in
the air that is at the back of the suicide
outbreak in Sydney. Con over the list:
the King pair who died at the Metropole—
though there was no evidence to show that the
poor young wife desired to call “ curtain ” on life ;
the young bank clerk, Ball, a fine youngster, who
died deliberately three months after his wedding;
and young Horton, a rich Aucklander. Fancy
anyone deliberately putting six feet of clay and a
daisy-quilt between him and the sight of the
dawns and sunsets and gum forests.
Mr. Frederick George Waley, general manager
of the Bellambi Coal Company, has been thrust
high into the ken of the public through the
miniature case. He is a man with a taste
for books and pictures and bijouterie. He bought
a miniature, alleged to be that of Captain Cook by
a noted painter, and carrying the date 1788, for
£4O. Now someone says it’s a fraud, and three
men have been committed for trial.
It may yet be shown that Mr. Waley got what
lie paid for. But collectors generally would be
wise to only collect pictures of their own time,
and within their own knowledge. It’s the only
absolutely sure guard against fakes which, nowa-
days, are most ingenious. For instance, a
European dealer in fakes got an artist to do a
Vandyck for him. It was done effectively on old
canvas, given a rich antiquated look, and, of
course, signed. The faker, when he saw the
work, professed to be delighted. In a voice of
deep emotion he told the hack artist, “It is so
good, you ought to put your own name on
it and take the credit of the work.”
So the artist painted out the name of Vandyck,
and brushed in his own. The painting was sent
to America for the millionaire market, and there
went before it a warning to the Customs that a
genuine Vandyck was being smuggled into the
States as the work of an unknown, living man. If
the Customs knew where to look, they could find
the name of the Master under a coat of paint.
The Customs sought and found ; then they piled
on a terrific duty. That was cheerfully paid, for
the faker had now a full guarantee that it was a
“genuine” Vandyck, and a millionaire was
easily victimised. Subsequent confession by the
artist gave the show away.
“The Muffs” shortly stage “The Village
Priest ”at Sydney Palace. Mrs. Allan, who will
be remembered as Miss Chatteris of the Brough
Co., and Miss Jean Maclurcan will take part.
Mr. Fletcher, a distinguished Muff, will be the
Priest who says “Lord, Thy servant heareth”
with such profound piety when the mysterious
turning of the leaves of the Sacred Book occurs.
Talking of amateurs, that flaxen-headed young
baritone, Nield, has been married to Miss Con-
stance Dansie.
From the D.T. : “A ‘salon’has been formed
in connection with the Women’s Patriotic Club,
aiming at the intelligent discussion of suitable
topics. The inaugural meeting will be held next
Monday evening at the club, and the subject for
discussion will be, ‘ Should Women Work Outside
the Home?”’ I shall offer my valueless services
later in the reading of the three following papers :
“Why is a Piece of String ? ” “ Did Cain Marry
a Monkey?” and “Should Earthquakes, Tidal
Waves, and other Great Natural Forces be
Tolerated by Mankind ? ” The last would be
about as easily settled by discussion as the initial
subject the “ salon ” selected.
Sydney Operatic Society will produce the jolly
“Cloches de Corneville,” in aid of the Home for
Incurables, from the 14th to the 20th of December,
at the Criterion Theatre. Tenor Kenningham
will take the principal male rdle. Conductor
Asprey will see the piece through ; and Mrs.
W. H. Eyres, Mrs. Arthur Tighe, and Captain
Forsyth-Cheffins are the hon. secs. Dr. H. C.
Moxham, of Macquarie-street, will act as business
manager and hon. treasurer. Funds are needed
to complete the Home (at Ryde), and increase the
accommodation from twenty to that for fifty
patients.
A Sydney man has a capital idea to help that
most worthy institution, the Waitara Foundling
Home. And he is trying to make the idea catch-
ing. He asks the journalists, poets, painters, and
musicians of Sydney to help the good women
of Waitara who give home and care to the
“unwanted” infant. Writer, artist, and
music-maker are to give original contributions
of their various works and pomps. An
exhibition of exhibitable arts will then be
held, and a literary and musical catalogue
will enclose verse articles and stories. This cata-
logue the man with the idea thinks “ may well
aspire to the dignity of a high-class literary pro-
duction, well worthy the shilling of our citizens.”
At the close of the exhibition of contributed
work, an auction sale should bring a few
coins into the Foundling Home money box. The
Man with the Idea will not narrow contributions
to those under the heading of Fine Arts. He’ll
take a cheque ; and if the figures marked on it
are big enough to make you prop-eyed, the cheque
might be exhibited. Do you want to help ?
Address, “ D.G.,” care Waitara Foundling
Hospital.
Miss Harrie Ireland has been engaged by

in Newcastle. “A Modern Adventuress” is the
exhilarating title of the piece Miss Ireland will
worry and be worried by. Another of Bland
Holt’s stock—actor Maxwell—will play juvenile
lead.
A fresh injustice to Reggie and Gwen, who are
under the age of eighteen :
The Postmaster-General has issued a new regulation,
under which parents or guardians of persons under the
age of 18 years may give written directions to any post-
master as to the delivery of any postal articles addressed
to such minors. The new regulation deals with cases in
which young people receive letters contrary to the
wishes of their parents or guardians.
It is far too despotic an act of the P.M.G. to use
the post as a means of parental control—or
tyranny. “Dear Postmister, —My Mary Ann,
who is seventeen years and nine months come
next Xmas, is getting letters from young Beelze-
bub Smith. The spelling and grammar are most
objectionable. Be sure to send them to me in
future,” writes Mary Ann’s mother. Mary Ann
merely tells young Smith to send the letters to
the pastry cook’s round the corner. Parents and
postmasters notwithstanding, Gwen will contrive
to hear from Percival, and Mary Ann from young
Smith.
Massage nurses took most of the admiring eye
when, on Friday, the P. A. Hospital Directors’
guests assembled to view the new operating
theatres and a lot of other departments. The
rub-and-pat-you damsels wear pale-blue linen
frocks, turn-down collars, and elbow sleeves.
Their elbows are enough to make a man abject.
Softly-cushioned elbows are so rare. Heads of
ticks, cancers, plague germs and such like deer,
under microscopes, held receptions. One of the
visitors had the X-rays turned on her head, with
its splendid mane of hair. And the men on-
lookers gasped. They “wouldn’t have believed ”
that one head could carry so many hairpins.
Warren’s Featherbone methods got an artistic
exposition from Miss Mabel Lewis on a day last
week. Featherbone is made from turkey-bird
wing quills, and is the modern dress-boning
material. Such a smart group of Featherbone
girls the Expositioness got together. Their collars
—regular neck-corsets—their belts and sleeves and
underskirts were of the dignified stiffness that
the turkey stuff alone can give. Anthony Horderns
stock the new thing.
Several English papers are illustrating Miss
Rose Musgrove’s best frock in “ The Rest Cure ”
(silly name !). It is of pale wild-rose pink:—
The bodice is fashioned in the similitude of a deep
cape, slashed up all round and cut into sharp points
weighted with heavy pink silk cords and tassels. This is
outlined with trails of miniature rosebuds, worked out in
pink soutach6, while the front of the bodice is cut into
the similitude of a wide pouched panel, outlined on either
side with trails of rosebuds and leaves, and tucked into a
deep swathed belt of the palest myosotis blue satin. The
overskirt is fashioned in tunic form, and slashed up to
the waist at intervals over a plain underskirt, the seams
and border being likewise soutachb and cut into points
while it is hung all round with enormous tassels, and
furnished in front with a narrow front panel outlined
with embroidery.
_ Mrs. Izett, hon. sec. of the Child Study Asso-
ciation, appeals on behalf of a pathetic case. An
invalid, three years in bed, is in want of bedroom
linen and bedding to replace that which the long
illness has exhausted. The hon. sec. adds that
the articles need not be new. She will be pleased
to take worn things.
From my Brisbane correspondent:—
Lord Chelmsford, with the gentle Verney in tow,
arrived bright and early, on Saturday, at the England v.
Queensland match, and sat oblivious to aught else until
the stumps were drawn. The seats in the vice-regal
enclosure at the Brisbane Cricket Ground are hard, and
her Firmness, together with Miss Hart, Col. Foxton,
and Major Trevor, showed an evident anxiety for
the tea interval. The tea, when it did come, was
awful, likewise the lunch. The only circumstance more
remarkable than the lamentableness of the provender
was the keen interest taken in cricketer Braund. Why ?
Who shall say?
Government House has had a wedding. The bride,
Miss Lane, is a townswoman of Lady Chelmsford, and,
for eight long years has silently adored one Hone, cleric
and hereditary soul-salvager. The said Hone being
anchored immovably hereabouts, his lady-love thrust an
ivory satin and Brussels lace wedding garment into a
carpet bag, and proceeded Brisbanewards herself. The
knot was tied by the Archbishop, who misquoted Scripture
during the process. De Witt Batty and the two Hone
sisters filled the bill as assistants, and Lord Chelmsford
gave the lady away, while his wife, in a pastel blue con-
fection, and two small daughters, looked on, resting
after their labors of decorating the church. The service
over, Dr. Donaldson carried the entire party back to
Bishopsbourne, where quite an unecclesiastical breakfast
was served, and bride and groom retired to coo at the
Tweed Heads by the noon train.
By the way, our Archbishop will be on the wing a good
deal sooner than was expected. “ For private reasons ”
he leaves on the 16th to catch the Ortona at Adelaide.
Rev. Batty, the chaplain, jaunts too, and the New Guinea
Bishop, just out of the Dalby Sanatorium, takes a hand
on the clerical ribbons. Mrs. New Guinea was, you will
remember, a daughter of one of your Judges.
The handsome widow of Leonard Turner goes Frog-
ward with her chicks by the Ortona.
I hear that fortune has smiled broadlj' upon our own
soprano, and that Mrs. Wilson has been offered, and
accepted, engagements to sing in London and the pro-
vinces that will keep her going for the next 11 months.
Yours Affectionately, Akenehi.
Yic. Government Statist is finding it hard
to get cockies to give particulars of their grain-
crops yield. A small minority dreads the acquir-
ing of inside stable information on this matter by
the Income-Tax people. The majority holds
doggedly that such knowledge plays into the pre-
datory hands of wheat buyers. Main result of the
Government Statist ceasing to give estimates of
the amount of wheat in the country would be the
withholding from the growers at large of this
valuable knowledge. The wheat buyers would
make it their business to know, all the same,
through local agents. Latter could, and un-
doubtedly would, in that case, arbitrarily under-
or over-estimate crops, and force agriculturists to
accept payment on faked bases.
“ J.P.” 6n Outrages :
“ 10-2-4” is right. Oadogan, the scion of the ham-and-
beef family, and ’Erb Beauchamp, the chemist’s son, who
pose jointly as disguised aristocrats, are social pests and
frequent ones ; but there are others more offensive. Has
your correspondent encountered the horror who, being
possessed of a moderate education and an atrophied sense
of humor, pants to impress conversationally? William
Brown owns an office, spectacles, and a small private
income. William has never earned a bean in his office,
nor ever will; but the office buttresses his self-
respect in some vague fashion, and so he clings
to it. He is an earnest student of his morn-
ing paper. His views on any question the paper
handles are decisive, not to say blatant. In the matutinal
tram, and later in the office, Brown absorbs his paper
eagerly—his paper on the tariff, his paper on Lyne, his
paper on Sosh, on the cricketers, on anything. For the
rest of the day those views are the views of William
Brown. They belong to him. He retains them for in-
fliction on his acquaintances. One speaks unwarily of
Watson. Instanter comes from William a flow of plati-
tude that some sad leader-writer ground out the night
before, with his tongue in his cheek. But Brown is at
his shameless worst when he gets home, or to any place
where defenceless women are. The strains of purloined
comment then simply flow from him. With plantigrade
feet outspread and an authoritative manner, he proffers all
the awful wedges of thought he has accumulated during
the day, as though they had just occurred to him.
He is for the nonce a Mind, a thing to revere. Surely
the people who flaunt relatives they never had are
inoffensive beside these first cousins of theirs who play
the same sort of confidence business in regard to ideas.
“L.E.T.”: Be the big Melbourne fluff
shop that has opened itself in Adelaide. Par
(B. 21/11/’O7) might be read that its business keeps
going on Saturday nights ; as a matter of fact it
shuts up on Saturdays at the usual hour (1 p.m.),
but opens on Friday nights, and that is what is
worrying other firms and will probably drive them
to the same bad habit.
Yic. Justice Hodges’haste will presently get
someone into trouble. Only lately he left a
country jury considering a criminal case, and
caught the train to Melbourne, leaving the 12 to
come to a decision as best they could without his
assistance. The other day, in a Melbourne per-
jury case, he again left the Court before the sta-
tutory six hours were up, and, apparently
assuming a disagreement, fixed the bail for the
accused’s retrial. After he had left, the jury
found a verdict of acquittal, and His Hastiness
had to be sent for to discharge the accused.
“ Eastern ” : Acheen is the name of a place
which will hum mysteriously in the ear of every
Cosmopolitan journalist, and yet few will under-
stand what it signifies. I drop on an explanation
in the last file of the London Times. Acheen is
the ache-ing of the Dutch in the East. Bismarck
said, “The Dutch will bleed todeath in Sumatra.”
He meant that the reversion of the Dutch Colonies
would come to Germany—a matter of vital
concern to Australia. Acheen is a savage part of
Sumatra, a vast region, as unconquerable as
Arabia, which is said never to have been con-
quered. Acheen has its many thousands of
warriors, comparable to the Maoris, but
unquenchable by the forces at the Dutch command.
The Acheenese have been at war with the Dutch
all the time. It is chronic. They are inex-
pungable—with existing white for-,es there —like
the savages of Mallicolo, in the liew Hebrides.
Fighting them takes all the gilt edge off Dutch
Government profits in the East Indies.
Sydney Telegraph has been trying to get
up a mild Imperialist excitement by printing
some “Tales of the Mutiny,” as told by the old
soldiers who were exhibited at the Birthday
Review. The attempt has been a lamentable
failure for the very simple reason that the old
soldiers had nothing to tell. Here is a sample,
from an old Corporal of the Queen’s Bays, who
was at the Relief of Lucknow :
Asked for his story, he said :—“ I have not got one.”
“ You saw some exciting incidents ? ”
“ Not. one,” was the disconcerting reply. “ You see,”
he added, “ I was only 19 then, and a young fellow when
he goes into the field is too green to take notice.”
“ But there were hardships?”
“ Yes, but what does a boy of 19 care about hardships?
.... I have read lots of yarns, and I know most of the
men who told them. They are all exaggerations. I
expect they have told them so often that they believe
they are true.”
Which is no doubt the simple, unvarnished truth
about most war yarns. There is no time to note
the exciting incidents of war, and if the soldier
had time, his mind is too intent on listening for a
word of command, or watching the people who
are waiting to kill or be killed. And, after all,
why all this fuss about the second relief of
Lucknow ? The back of the mutiny was broken
by the capture of Delhi, and though there was a
great deal of hard fighting and harder marching
to be done till well into ’59, the real danger was
over by the end of September, ’57, when Delhi
was finally occupied after a fortnight of street
fighting.
The N.D.L. steamer Bremen, which is at
present in Sydney, was for a time on the Atlantic
run, and on Thursday last a number of people
were on board by invitation of the agents, Messrs.
Lohmann and Co., and Captain Yolger, to see a
demonstration of the “Telefunken” system of
wireless telegraphy. On the Atlantic liners the
passengers are kept in touch with the stations in
Germany, Britain, and the United States, and
stockbrokers travelling to and fro get their regu-
lar reports, and gamble uninterruptedly the whole
journey. A daily paper, giving a brief sum-
mary of the world’s news, is published, and
people can telegraph to their friends what
hour they will arrive, and so forth. On
one run from New York to Liverpool a grandson
of the man who drafted the Yankee Declaration
of Independence was on his way to England to
be married. He was a typical hustler, and two
days out developed toothache. He wirelessed a
dentist in Liverpool, making an appointment for
2.30 the afternoon the steamer was due in Liver-
pool, and received a reply as to how long it would
take to get the tooth stopped. Then he wire-
lessed the girl : “Marry you 3.30 p.m., Tuesday
—Love.” This sorb of thing is very entertaining,
and, occasionally, very handy ; but it destroys a
sea voyage of half its charm, that of getting right
away from everybody and all worry. The
man who gets his wife carefully shipped
off on a -boat fitted with wireless ma-
chinery is likely to have his good time
interrupted by messages : “ Be sure and feed the
canary. Aie you having dinner at home to-night?.
Reply immediately.” But about the Telefunken
system. It is very simple. The operator has his
electricity generated by mercury turbine dynamos,
and sends a stream of it out into the universe.
Then he breaks it up into dots and dashes, or long
and short waves chat make dots and dashes, by his
sending key. These waves are caught by the in-
struments that are tuned to the same pitch, and
the current rushing through relay batteries works
a Morse printer, which inks the message in dots
and dashes on a paper tape. What could be more
simple ?
Have you a girl friend away in the backblocks —
perhaps in Northern Queensland or out west of Bourlce ?
What gift do you think she would value higher than the
gift of a good complexion ? Why not send her a jar of
Valaze packed in its dainty, little porcelain jar;
it would make a dainty present, and one that
you can be sure would be very highly appre-
ciated? Where the climate is hot and trying
to the skin, Valaze must be used if me wishes to preserve
one’s youthful looks—it does no hurt to the most tender
skin, and purely by assisting Nature endows a woman
with the soft complexion of early girlhood. Any chemist
will sell you Valaze at 3s. 6d. and 6s. 6d. a jar. You can
either buy it from them and post it yourself, or if you send
us the price and 6d. extra for postage, we will post it to
any friend whose name and address you give us. Perhaps
you would like to add a set of Valaze Massage Rollers?
With these one has a full Beauty Outfit, and can employ
in her own home the exercises that complexion specialists
find make for the “face beautiful.” They cost Bs. 6d. each ;
small set, 12s. 6d. ; large set of two, 18s. 6d. ; and complete
set of three in case, 255. You can get these—and any
other Beauty Equipment you desire at any of the Valaze
Institutes : 274 Collins-street, Melbourne ; 158 Pitt-street,
Sydney ; or even at 9 Brandon-street, Wellington, away
in far New Zealand.*

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 2, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The Wimmen’s Ex. ended on Saturday, in a
blaze of glory. And now the public waits to hear
how much the monetary loss or profit upon
the scheme may be. To the astonishment
of critics in general, a profit is predicted,
although the whole affair has been an
argument against women being permitted a
say in their own advancement. With a leader
so stimulating as Lady Northcote, men would
have been galvanised into magnificent energy and
achievement. The ladies, instead, have squabbled
over precedence and prestige, and every other
thing that is trivial and unnecessary under such
circumstances. And little advantage has accrued
to the competitors even. No one is satisfied with
the awards in any section, and some irate females
talk of claiming compensation for damage done to
their pet antimacassars. So, beyond the adver-
tisement given to its ambitious promoters, and
some enterprising fancy-workers, who sold their
ware 3 at fancy prices, it may be said that the
women’s first big venture was their last huge
failure.
Socially, awful ructions have taken place. One
Dame won’t even glare on the same side of the
street as the Forrest Dame, and some of the
eatmittee look all round the Frank Madden lady
before they even see her, and there is a blissful lot
of sorting out to be done. Nobody’s so
glad of the Finish as the creche madames.
They so enthusiastically took up the promise
of helping week by week in turn —but
those hot ( ays and those squalling babes !
The nurses murmur that the babies were prac-
tically no trouble in comparison with the visitors.
Twenty or so would gather round and goo-goo one
poor defenceless baby. The youngster would
rub its nose on the pillow in sleepy protest; more
delighted gurgle and goo, and he would rise
up in his awakened wrath and bring the Exhibi-
tion down with his yells, and a nurse (who would
otherwise be looking after two or three or half-a-
dozen) would spend hours in the back-blocks of
the building pacifying the wrathful mite of
humanity.
Women’s Exhibition was overrun, its last
few days, with lithe, straight, self-confident
maidens, each with a natty rifle case across her
shoulder. They have cause for perkiness. In the
two days’ matches the target was not missed once,
and nearly a dozen shooters scored “possibles.”
A woman either shoots well, or not at all. She
either shrieks at the sight of a gun, or she is an
expert, with iron nerves unassailed by either
whisky or tobacco.
Tea scrambling was revived with the last week
of the Women’s Exhibition. The frilly eatmittee
rushed about wildly, waving dress-boxes, and clad
in working togs. The State Gov.’s lady Was to
be tea-ed. Hence the need for extra frill. Hence
also a tale. There are degrees of dressing-
rooms in the Exhibition (some are marked,
'"Ladies only—no tvaitresses allowed”) but the
chief one is a toney and select corner. It
was rushed by the Elect on Talbot-Tea day ;
but one of the classiest Gats got in first, and held
the fort solus with the irritating persistency of a
shipboard bathroom invader, whilst her feline
colleagues meowed dismally in their discomfiture.
Following the lead of one golden starchy dame of
rigid demeanor, the routed ones proceeded with
their band-boxes to the draughty shelter of some
tree for the lightning-change of frocking or,
perhaps, horror ! they were compelled co seek the
shelter of a waitresses’ dressing-room ! In which
cases they probably wept for their awful degrada-
tion.
The guest was presented with frozen roses.
Symbolical possibly. An iced bouquet seems as
inevitably appropriate for the Coldbottle dame as
a fatted calf, served up roasted and hot, at
another brand of welcome.
For the Wallace divorce the courtis crowded
each day by the prurient and the curious, who
find ample reward in tbe exhibits of letters. The
respondent, Mrs. Wallace, makes a dramatic
figure. Supported by one or two loyal partizans,
she sits, hour after hour, immobile, pallid, and
beautiful in a strange, chill fashion. Usually she
appeals to the (esthetic sensibilities of the jury in
trailing gowns of soft black, with a girlish hat of
mournful hue, which emphasises the honey-yellow
of her hair and shades the violet of her
haggard eyes very effectively. Charlie Wal-
lace, the petitioner, is wan and thin. Evi-
dently domestic upheavals don’t agree with the
Scotch temperament. Co-respondent Strong
bears up with every superficial indication of in-
nocence and insouciance. His attitude of moral
certainty doesn’t compromise the lady in the
least, and gives his friends new heart to ad vertise
his blamelessness and jolly good-fellowship. So
far half a score of popular names have been
dragged through the Divorce Court dust—to the
undisguised delight of a mob of men and women
who ordinarily make some pretence of mental
modesty and social esprit de corps.
Indeed, the blatant indecency of the well-dressed
folk who tight for admission to the court each day,
is the most unpleasant feature of a painful
case. To hear a horrid story of domestic
misery, and watch the demolition of some man
and woman’s self-respect and future prospects,
dozens of people, whom everybody knows, scram-
ble and push and squabble in the law-court’s cor-
ridors. In the court, barristers’ gowns are torn
and their wigs dislodged by women struggling to
get a good view of the unlucky principals in a
squalid play. And the same faces may be seen
hour after hour, intently watching, listening,
smiling, while their friends’ most intimate life
is being exposed and dissected by a flock of law-
yers. "Phew! The modern Divorce Court is
comparable only with the Neronian arena. It’s a
hideous exposition of sordid, mean immorality—
this vicarious enjoyment of other persons’ alleged
sins.
Cup-tired Melbourne is stirred muchly by its
long-looked-for divorce case. The affair seems
more suited- to the fashionable taste for its long
brewing. Fashionable Johnniedom is on the qui
viie. Will its name be mentioned or will it not ?
It is rapidly coming to be conceded that not to
have one’s name mentioned somehow in this con-
nection is to argue oneself unknown and a social
back-number.
Melba got her second house on Saturday night—
and got it hot and strong. Crowds who got badly
“left” at the first made a do-or-die rush for this,
and by the concert morning there was not a seat
to be bought or stolen. Folk paid their half-
guineas and more to sit on the narrow rim of an
organ seat to watch the dimpled back of the
Diva, and others wedged among the flowers
on the platform, and peered from behind trophies
as big as a house. One dainty lot of flowers looked
like the phantom ship in “The Flying Dutch-
man ” as an usher bore it jerkily along; it had a
water-lily “Nellie” in poster letters on a harp.
A hideous thing. “My goodness!” murmured
the Staring Eye, “ What it must have cost! ”
Funny things happened in the foreground at
the Melba warble. The amazing Bent, who must
be going demented with self-conceit, waddled up
to the platform when Melba was arranging her
“ floral tributes,” and gave her what appeared to
be a bundle of waterlilies, tied with a bit
of string, but which might have been a bundle of
asparagus or a cauliflower. Melba pinched his
giddy old paw in taking the bundle of mystery,
and bent down for some jocular remark. During
the interval, Bent waddled to the Talbottles and
made an elephantine attempt at polite conversa-
tion, after which he collided with Archbishop
Clarke (who was there on his gaiters), and Church
and State exchanged moral observations —on the
Wallace divorce case, perhaps.
Melbourne was an excitable place on Saturday
night. Melba’s crowd was wedging the streets
and blocking the traffic, the Women’s Ex. was
being wound up, and a section of commercial
Melbourne was blazing up to Heaven in a fiery
chariot. There was a stage fire, with quite a
swagger “house.” All the Melba crowd col-
lected at the 10.30 finish, and the Theatre push
arrived a little later, and risked the sparks in
its frillies. All male Melbourne arrived home
about breakfast time —“Helping at the fire, my
dearsh,” was the triumphant explanation.
To the crowd coming down uhe steps from
Melba’s warbling, the sparks seemed to be
flying right into the porch. Someone shouted
“Moffat’s.” There was a distinct thrill through
the packed wedge of shoulder and chiffon.
“Bargains!” murmured a small dame ecstatic-
ally. “ It’s the Exhibition ” ventured another
excitable voice, and there was a wild surge
and squirm. “Fire and the Exhibition” has
been a horrible bogey to women all through
the Show, and the “Brigade” has watched the
Big Barn like a cat with a mouse. There were
three incipient blazes in the Exhibition during
the six weeks. Ex-mayoress Pleasance was quite
a heroine in one scare. The engines rushed
past the tea-room annexe (the Pleasance lady was
on duty). There was a shout and a stampede.
The room was crowded, and Scare just clutched
the mass. Mrs. Pleasance worked up an Excelsior
voice, and called out that it was a practice for the
night’s “demonstration.” Then she grabbed the
waitresses, and threatened them softly with
instant death if they squealed ; and in the back-
ground the men conquered a real fire. Thus the
stampede was stopped.
Item in Collins-street parade daily is a dashing
dog-cart, with two smartly-frocked and veiled
dames and a jolly little youngster, Mamma Veil
with a tender, protecting, protesting arm round
boy, Sister Veil driving smartly. “ Will they
take my chee-ild from me?” the tableau seems to
say. It’s most effective. The child seems puzzled.
It doesn’t understand Mamma’s new and violent
interest.
The Wrixons’ party reclaimed State Parliament
House from its normal condition of utter dreari-
ness last week. Guests got tangled up in Exhi-
bition turnstiles, and motors had giddy times with
Exhibition barriers across avenues and such.
Wherefore quite a few tempers arrived with the
guests who had tried short-cuts.
In the Hall the folk sat and stewed the while they
awaited vice-regality. At long last the cool and
lengthy Governor folk sailed in, with a lofty
handshake for the President and a compre-
hensive bow to the House. A daily paper said
the Talbot lady wore “pink souples,” which
sounds so like tights. But, of course, she didn’t.
It was flesh color, certainly, but quite full and
chiffony.
After a while, an amazing thing happened.
The lights went out, and whilst the more youth-
ful clasped each other’s hands reassuringly, a
cinematograph started to whir and flicker. A
tender and romantic story-without-words started
at a brisk hand-gallop across the sheet, after
which (also briskly) the lights switched on and
showed some interesting, unrehearsed side-shows
in the corners. One pair of doves were fully old
enough to know better.
Corridor supper proved too elevating for one
notable guest. She was guided (quite early) to
the intricacies of the cloak rooms by obsequious
officials. “This is your cloak room, madame,”
they murmured, and left her. Then gaping
attendants rushed for her cloak and robed the
lady in its dusky depths, and comforted her until
her motor arrived.
At one of our big hotels lives a little society
matron who is envied for the spun gold of her
thatch, of which she is very proud; and so is
hubby. The other day at dinner he was enlarging
on the foolishness of women who buy curls, and
enthusing over the pleasure of watching his dainty
wife just “ throw ” her hair up in about five
minutes ; all this much to the irritation of a
fellow-diner, whose curls were obviously pur-
chased. Her revenge came next day. YVifie’s
maid was summarily dismissed for publicly claim-
ing a clump of golden curls that had been found
in the corridor, and now the wife glares coldly
when anyone mentions coiffure.
St. Kilda gave.the popular Booty maidens a
warm though regretful send-off. The Booty
parent bird is taking his chicks for a flutter
abroad. They stay in Sydney for a month, then
visit Canada, and finally England—England, ’Ome
and Booty.
Cards are out for a “Juvenile Rose Fancy
Dress Ball ” —organisers being Mrs. William
Green and daughter Florrie. It whirls —to the
aggrandisement of many deserving charities —on
December 9 next. The Misses Hyams give a
Bal Masque—also juvenile—on December 5.
The Mrs. Doctor Lavertine baby was christened
recently, and snared enough cheques to start a
solid banking account; also a sufficiency of
pawnable silver ware to stock an Aladdin’s cave.
Baby’s mother was a handsome Melbourne maiden
who married a gilded gunboat surgeon. She has
since been spending her time between Plymouth
and the vicinity of the warboat and the family
castle, in Emerald Isle. Wealthy bachelor Levers,
of Mt. Levers, and Miss Court-McSharry were
godparents to the new arrival—and a more likely-
to-be-useful brace no reasonable-minded kid could
demand.
All the State schools holidayed on Friday for a
final Exhibition fling, and, despite the heat,
demonstrated very energetically. The pupils fed
at the Schools Cookery annexe, and there, as is
youths’ wont, grew replete and altogether happy.
Mrs. Peters, who belongs to the clan of the
Grants, of Bowenville station (Q.), gave a tea-
party at the Paris, the other day, for Mrs. Will
Grant. The fair-haired charmer is considered the
glassy agate in far Brisbane. She made a centre
for other visiting Queenslanders while here—the
Chelmsfords and other considerables of the sort
being generally on hand.
Mr. and Mrs. ’Arry Rickards returned from
Europe on Monday. He promises an endless
pageant of great little people for his loving, pro-
fitable public. Mrs. ’Arry sports the most gor-
geous gowns, and many large new diamonds,
while she confides to her friends that she’s real
glad to be back in Australia. By the way, the
much-travelling husband talks of being back in
London by April.
Miss Mars Buckley went off to Bombay last
week, and the amiabie spinster will remain there
some time. Probably, having a well-developed
taste for erudition, she’ll return with a knowledge
of Parsee literature, and a repertoire of Brahmin
folk-music. Meantime, she leaves a gap in an in-
telligent circle.
During the tariff debates, tbe House of Repre-
sentatives has seen a lot of the Fatman’s wives
and daughters. The galleries are filled with dis-
approving dames, who used to pretend to a
national spirit. Under stress of the tariff propo-
sitions they have cast aside pretence, and stand
confessed as shameless amateur lobbyists. One
candid member tells that he and several other
prohibitionists have found life a misery of late.
A woman who before had no other significance
than as a pleasant hostess or a friend’s wife, now
is a subtle schemer who doesn’t scruple to propose
certain tariff amendments calculated to profit
some relative, while she hints engagingly at
incredible bribes.
Steve, of the Fairbairn brothers, put in a record
gorgeous sprint round his city carnival time. Now,
with the slack and re-action, he departs for Lon-
don and his Dalgety directorship. Brother
George and Mrs. George depart by the same
boat, but they brave the Indian heat for the set
purpose of collecting by force Mrs. Daughter-
Alison, Captain Balmain, and the marvel of a
grandchild who is to be brought home for select
exhibition.
From “ Rosna,” of Adelaide :
Government House is closed for the season, and the
Governor and Lady Le Hunte have gone off to Marble Hill.
Miss Clowes and a few aides accompanied them, though this
State will soon be almost aideless. Captain Ryder, who
is to commit matrimony with Miss Angas by-and-bye,
leaves for Britain early in the year to join his regiment;
and Mr. Walter Stuart Baillie-Hamilton sails off to visit a
br 'ther, or a tiger, or something, in India.
This State has oome out of the Women’s Work Exhi-
bition with a surplus of over £3OO. At the Melbourne
Ex. we grabbed also a satisfactory number of honors.
Lady Northcote’s special prize went to the Crfeche ; and
Miss Goliy, a damsel who dwells on a small stray island
with a lighthouse on top of it and a gulf all around, has
been recommended a special for carving a bookcase
“ under adverse circumstances.” Also, we grasped seven
first prizes, 10 seconds, two silver medals, three specials,
two recommendations to mercy, and our School of Design
was patted on the back.
At the final meeting of the S. Australian committee,
last week, Lady Le Hunte cast many rhetorical flowers
upon Mrs. J. R. Anderson, the hon. sec., and, on behalf
of the committee, presented her with a neck-chain and
pendant, set with diamonds and amethysts.
Musical-Doctor Harold Davies, who trains up Adelaide’s
little Bach Society, manipulated a concert of unaccom-
panied part-songs, t’other evening, with fiddler Nora
Kyttin Thomas and pianist Elsie Hamilton to provide in-
strumental interjections. The Bach Society awards two
scholarships every year, and present holders, Miss Olive
Brown and Mr. Murray Barlow, dispensed solo melody
for the and exhibited a promising voice apiece.
The Butt pair had such houses in this musical village
that they have determined to drop in upon us on the way
back from the West, and warble a few more hundred
pounds’ worth of melody on December 10. ’Tis rumored,
by the way, that Clara has presented an old girl-chum in
this city with a glorious trousseau.
Yours affectionately, Johanna.
“ Whitepen ” : With reference to the re-
sponsibilities of bank clerks in Victoria (B.
21/11/’O7), we in N. S. Wales have the same bless-
ings. Though we pay into a fidelity fund for the
purpose of meeting defalcations, all shortages in
the cash have to be immediately paid in ; but any
surplus must go to surplus cash account. All bad
coins have to be replaced with good (you can do
anything you like with the bad ones). But our
worst trouble is the sweated sovereign. There
are hundreds of these in circulation now, and they
are increasing daily. I think they are the pro-
ducts of the plating batteries, and wily Ah Chow.
The latter cuts off the milling, and re-mills the
coin. As the face is not interfered with at all,
the only way to detect the fraud is by holding
several together between the thumb and finger,
when the small ones will fall through. While you
go through this performance the look on the
Chow’s face is a study in sublime innocence.
“A. Wood ” : Asked a boundary rider the
other day what he thought of an Aboriginalities
par. re horses that hid behind trees and had
strange devices to keep the bell from ringing.
“Yes,” said he, “ I guess that’s all true. I had
an old mare that it used to take me half a day to
find sometimes although she had a bell round
her neck. I found by her tracks that she used to
dodge me round a tree, and do it, too, without ring-
ing her bell. One day I tied the bell to her tail. I
reckoned the flies would make her ring it. I
haven’t seen the mare since.”
“ Coalopolis Nobody ever mentions any
horse-trams, except those of Adelaide. Yet Roe-
bourne, nine miles inland from Cossack, on the
North-Westralian coast, is also the proud pos-
sesser of a horse-tram. You ride in a glorified
dog-box ; that is, you ride in it if you catch it. I
missed it. Then I walked. The distance is given
as nine miles. I think it is nearer 90. It was 115
degrees in the shade. There was no shade. Half-
way was a shining lake. I was horribly thirsty.
I hastened across. How lovely to plunge my
scorched lips in the cool refreshing—ugh, salt !
Four miles more of sun-bake. Then Roebourne.
I managed to catch the tram back ; even riding in
a dog-box is better than walking in weather 115 in
the shade (no shade).

POLITICAL
POINTS
A suggestion by John Norton in N.S.W.
Assembly, that there should be a special income
tax exemption for large families, was ruled out of
order. But the Australian tendency is towards
legislation in that direction.
CLEVER.
FIRST MAN (in background): “Everybody says he
has an exquisite sense of humor."
SECOND DO. : “Fes. He makes a point of laugh-
ing at everybody’s poor jokes.”
Another result of the, accursed: Deakin r
Lyne duty schedule is, to, hand from Yarra,
Junction (Vic.)- There works have been opened;
for the distillation of wood and the conversion, of
acetic acid into acetone—an essential element in
the composition of smokeless powder. One; effect,
will be the distribution of £IOOO a month ,in wages
among the tariff-stricken workers.
Philp’s Cabinet—which couldn’t even get
Supply from Parliament—has been already named
“The Queen-street Ministry”; and, that, of
course, will naturally become “ The Queer-street
Ministry.”
The late Kidston Government was dis-
tinctly representative of the different geographi-
cal divisions. Kidston (centre), Bell and O’Sulli-
van (Darling Downs), Blair (Moreton), Airey
(North), Kerr(West), and Hawthorne (metropolis).
The Chelmsford Government is all metropolis
except for Philp.
The frantic efforts of the Brisbane dailies
—Mail, Telegraph, Observer and Courier —to
tempt the Downs members to desert Kidston’s
standard, resulted in the apostacy of one only,
and he only half apostasised, being now an
“ Independent.” If four mighty daily papers can
only make half a convert, how many votes can
they influence with the electors ?
Tolerably certain that when Kidston
makes his next attempt to shake up the House of
Hinderers he will not consult the Governor, but
go straight over his head to the Colonial Office, as
Ballance once did in Maoriland with complete
success.
Brisbane dailies—to serve party ends, of
course—persist in alleging that ex-Premier Kid-
ston resigned because he “ couldn’t carry on the
business of the country.” Rubbish! Kidston
had a solid majority behind him in the elected
Chamber, and was never once defeated during the
session. He resigned because the Governor
accepted the advice of the leader of a minority in
Opposition, while refusing to follow the advice of
the leader of the House.
Bath, of the Labor party, carried an in-
teresting amendment through the Westralian
Assembly, the other day, in the teeth of the
Government—an amendment providing that for
every child he has, a man shall be free from
income-tax to the extent of an extra £lO of
income.
The Commonwealth is advantaged by its
nationalist and strongly white-Australian Govern-
ment, not in legislation merely, but in administra-
tion. Thus S. Mauger jumped very firmly last
week on the amazing S. A us. Deputy-P.M.G
who accepted through the State Board a tender
for Chinese-made helmets. Mauger’s action
shocked the local Freetraders and upholders of
the cause of the colored brother ; but Australia at
large judged it good.
Cabled that the number of returned
steerage passengers to the Cold Country from the
TJ.S. and Canada recently established a record.
I his is Australia’s cue to hitch up its pants firmly,'
and aggressively poke its chin into the business!
Hitherto, the mild statement of its claims has
been drowned in the Dominion’s roar over the
blissful advantages of a seven months’ rigorous
winter, and the enthusiastic whoop of the
Pacific Railway Cos., which had a continent of ice-
caked acreage to get off its chest. Now, let us
roar a little, with the new tariff as a text.
When cash registers and computing scales
came on for discussion in the tariff struggle, Tariff
Bill Lyne made a mistake. Asked were
cash registers made in Australia, he said
they were not. Therein he erred. They
are made in Pitt-street, Sydney. At the
present time the man who is making
Australian cash registers is also making
a tobacco-cutter of unique and Australian
design ; and, given a decent duty, will
proceed to employ more hands, and make
computing scales also. Further, The
Bulletin has it on very good authority
that, had the duty remained high, the
great American firm which has now
practically a monopoly of the world’s
supply ot cash registers was going to
open a factory in Australia. With the ridi-
culous 10 per cent, duty that has been im-
> posed, the firm will continue to make its re-

HALF-AND-HALF.
BILGER (at a King's Birthday celebration): “Gentle-
men (hie) ‘ The Quink!’”
TheS. Aus. Farmers and Producers’ Union
was authoritatively informed by the local National
Ass'the other day—“ Every time they bought a
locally-made article they were hindering them-
selves.” Certainly. Flour, for instance. That
would make a fine motto for the S. Australian
alleged wheat-growers’ union—“ Support Free-
trade and use Manitoba Flour.”
“More Protection for Melbourne,” Syd-
ney D.T. squealed in a headline the other day. It
was over the duty on horseshoe nails. Then it
transpired that there was only one Australian
factory—established in Victoria under the old
effective State tariff—and, having got a start, this
factory didn’t want any more Protection. With
more Protection, the Melbourne factory saw the
prospect of other factories starting in Sydney.
Yet the wretched, mischief-making Sydney rag
had the impudence to call it “more Protec-
tion for Melbourne.”
The crisis in U.S.A. suggests again the
correctness of Ricardo’s theory of crises—that
they are due to production passing consumption.
Stacks of produced goods thus accumulate ; they
are for a while a basis of credit, as banks, etc.,
will advance money on them ; then comes a hint
of uneasiness ; credit totters ; the stored-up goods
are found not to be quickly realisable; and a
crisis ensues. The remedy is rather an increase
of consumption than a decrease of production.
For wages to advance rapidly with the growth of
prosperity provides an element of stability and an
insurance against crises.
Wade’s backdown in the income-ta,x
remission business is a bad augury for the fate of
Law Reform and Greater Sydney and Newcastle.
The new Premier was said to be somewhat keen
on law reform when he first became A.-G., but his
zeal only manifested itself in the bright idea of
copying the British Judicature system. He
has apparently smothered this in the cradle, and
we hear no more rumors of law reform. Here is
a field in which some sweeping work could be
done ; and the first step would be a readjustment
of appeal work and conditions generally in view of
the Federal Judicature machinery. The public is
asking why a litigant cannot go straight from a
Jury Court or a chamber Judge to a the High
Court, without having to make a circuitous journey
via the N.S.W. Supreme Court, and secure a
judgment which is generally over-ruled by the
High Court later-on. If all the appeal work were
passed on to the High Court, the N.S.W. Judge
could sit fairly continuously in jury causes and
circuits, so that the doors of all branches of the
Temple of Justice would be open all the year
round, instead of, as now, being closed for months,
intensifying the evil of the law’s delay.
In the German city of Leipzig there is
published a piano-makers’ trade newspaper,
Zeitschrift fur Instkumentenbau. In its issue
of October 11, 1907, it was announced that the
Association of German Piano Makers was to hold
a meeting on October 19 to discuss the increase
in the Australian duties on pianos. The council
of the association was to be instructed to demand
that the Imperial Government should take ener-
getic steps through the Consul-General to get the
duty reduced. It was mentioned that Australia
had only one piano manufactory, and it was stated,
on the authority of Australian papers, that the
pianos it turned out were rubbish. Foreign
musicians who had given the pianos excellent
testimonials were attacked in their private and
public characters, and charged with having sold
testimonials. The Australian legislators who
were likely to support the increased duties were
declared to be shareholders, and corrupt. Follow-
ing this, there was held in Melbourne a meeting
of piano importers, the chief business of which
was the raising of a fighting fund to contest the
new duties. Was there any connection between
that meeting in Melbourne and the other one in
Leipzig of October 19, and is German money
being subscribed for a lobby campaign against
Australian industry ?

From Messrs. Angus and Coote, the well-known
jewellers of 492 George-street, Sydney, we have received
a copy of their Christmas catalogue. This is issued to
assist out-of-town residents in the selection of suitable
presents for the season. It is a very complete list
for the purpose, and contains about 800 illustra-
tions clearly described and priced. Messrs. Angus
and Coote claim to do the largest postal business
of its kind in Australia, and their wonderful
growth during the last few years is testimony
to the considerate treatment they extend to their
customers. A great feature of the business is that in
every case where the article fails to come up to the
customer’s expectations, the money will be refunded if the
goods are returned. The “A. & 0.” Christmas catalogue
is indeed well worthy of perusal, especially to those who
realise the desirability of jewellery and plate for presenta-
tion purposes.*
Cigar-boxes, said to be equal, if not
superior, to the imported article hitherto used hy
Australian cigar manufacturers, are now being
made by a Townsville (Q.) firm. Odd ends of
cedar are largely used for the purpose.
Party Government got another fine advt.
in the N.S.W. Parliament last week. David
Storey was growling about the Government’s
income-tax proposals, when Wade rushed into the
Chamber to say that he “ wanted loyal support—
that was all,” and Storey ‘‘didn’t give it.”
Wherefore (as Wade’s colleague, Wood, then
fiercely told him) he could take his support else-
where. In short, Wade and Wood and Waddle
don’t want members with any opinions of their own;
they want lay figures that will move when the
showman pulls the string. It’s a fine ideal for a
“Liberal and Reform ” caucus-hating party.
The British Weekly prints (9/10/’O7)
what it terms “ two important letters from a pair
of eminent Australians ” —Rev. W. H. Beale, ex-
President of the N.S.W. Methodist Conference ;
and J. E. Carruthers, also a Rev., and brother
of Joseph Carruthers. Australia’s statesmen are,
in the Rev. Beale’s eyes, grasping, sordid persons,
bent on bringing about the “ lowering of wages in
England, and the raising of the price of bread
and clothing there.” At the back of the above
is a sinister and selfish motive. An open door is
to be kept for “Australian stuff,” which will be
enabled thus “to go in and grab the artificially-
raised prices.’’ British workmen are barred out
from the Commonwealth, contemned, treated as
helots and undesirables. All local talk concerning
preference is (according to Rev. Carruthers) hypo-
crisy, “a cunning juggling with terms.” Deakin’s
preference proposals (those same proposals which
British papers calculated a month ago were to
make a difference of £1,250,000 per annum in favor
of British industries) are “selfish and one-sided.”
The prosperity of the Commonwealth is to be
“ attained at the cost of the toiling millions of the
mother country.” A fine pair of Stinking Fishites !
Thos. Bent, writhing, as sinuously as a
man of his globular proportions can writhe, under
the lash of newspaper criticism, talks splenetically
of a measure compelling press writers to sign their
articles. “When you see who these fellows are
that write about us,” says Bent. “ you won’t give
a button for their opinions.” This sagacious idea
satisfies Bent that it is only necessary to
have signed articles in order to render null
and void all press criticism of a blunder-
buss Government. In such circumstances,
T. Bent could be a large, shapeless politician
wallowing contentedly in the bog of error, careless
of all written criticism. Tommy does not under-
stand that the name continually associated with
trenchant articles would presently be invested
with more power and dignity than the impersonal
“ We” that now staggers his complacency. What
is more, individual pressmen of ability, having
associated themselves with a crusade against a fat
mistake like Bent, would, for their credit’s sake,
and in the spirit of personal combat, be impelled
to push it with more vigor and vehemence than
they now do when sinking individual feeling in the
editorial “we.” There’s a lot to be said against
the signed article; but in that lot there is no justi-
fication for alleging that it would act as a means
of restraint.

YUSSUF KHAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As the camel-train is stringing - there’s a tall man with it
swinging,
With a figure straight and slim, and with sinew in his
stride ;
’Tis that strange and silent man, grey and wiry Yussuf
Khan :
A bit of ancient As. ~ full of ignorance and pride.
He is nob devoidof grace, but inscrutable of face ;
He’s a son of sand and sunshine, and he’s up in desert
lore ;
From the old Semitic strains, that were smitten in the
veins,
With the instinct of the nomad, he must wander ever-
more.
He is not a yellow man, old and hardy Yussuf Khan,
But he’s Asian, and he’s colored, and of-alien thought and
race;
Hisst he propagate and grow, he must mount his packs
and go
From the newer, cleaner country—lest the ill weeds grow
apace.
Not for hate, O Yussuf Khan, of a harmless, colored man ;
But the thin end of the wedge you are slowly working in.
Though there’s room for all, you say, yet we cannot let
you stay,
For there’s menace in the color of your Asiatic skin.
Look ye, Yussuf, we are right; for we’d have Australia
white
For the future generations, for the children of our race ;
You must shift your kit and pack, you must take your
passage back—
We will pay you for your camels, O inscrutable of face !
Tell your countrymen we mean to keep this our garden
clean,
That we do it not unwittingly, nor yet in hate or
doubt;
You may be a splendid man, but you’re Asia, Yussuf
Khan,
And it’s written very plainly—We Must Keep the Asian
Out.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Even though Tatt.’s Sweep should survive all
attempts at repression by the Commonwealth and State
Governments, it must inevitably be at last squeezed to
death by grasping horse-owners. As a lottery, discon-
nected from “ the evils of the turf,” it would always
appeal to the gambling spirit of the community, but the
turf connection grows positively disgusting to common
sense. Tatt.’s Sweeps on the less important races have
lost all appearance of offering the advertised prize in its
entirety to the dnawer of the winning horse. To ensure
a run for his (or her) money the lucky drawer must
nowadays “ approach ” the owner with an offer of
about half the stake. The fact is proclaimed in
the daily press as though the Sweep were an Owners’
Benefit Fund, subscribed by the public for purposes of
self-taxation, the penalty for non-compliance with an
owner’s demand being the scratching of the geegee that
might have won. What else can be inferred from the
following par. in Melbourne Age ? “ Sweep considerations
had much to do with the composition of the field yester-
day for the Sandhurst Cup. It is said the connections of
Pierus were laid a substantial stake by the holder of the
gelding in the ‘ consultation,’ but the drawer of Tulkeroo
remained incognito." Of the two stable companions,
Tulkeroo was said to be the better at the weights, but
the person who had drawn the horse in Tatts. made no
offer of prize money. He thought he had a chance to
win the first prize, and was entitled to the whole of his
luck. Because he had bought his ticket under a wrong
impression, he lost his first-prize chance after he had
drawn it. Illogical punters are in the habit of saying
that half a loaf is better than nothing, if the loaf is worth
£4OOO, and you have bought it for five shillings and two-
pence. But the injustice of the tax on ticket-drawers is
palpable enough when viewed in a magnified form. A
syndicate that bought 12,000 tickets would seem lucky to
get first prize in their lot, yet if they had had to part with
half of .their luck in order to secure a run for the other
half, they would make a loss on the transaction.
* * * *
The Gambling Bill now before Maoriland Par-
liament is a masterpiece of Judding, complicated with
symptoms of Iltey Cohen. Its principal features are (1)
abolition of the double tote, (2) compulsory licensing of
Ikey Jones by the Racing Clubs, (3) one race one divvy,
and (4) the diverting of portion of the totalisator per-
centage from the Treasury to benevolent institutions.
As regards the double tote, its tendency has been to
lessen gambling. When Bill’s quid is locked up for the
greater part of the day in the double machine, William’s
ability to back bis fancy is accordingly limited. Where
the double machine has been used, the volume of busi-
ness on the straight-out totes has diminished. The re-
appearance of Solomon Mclntosh will not assist the
reformers to anticipate the millennium ; neither will it
conduce' to clean sport. But it will enable youths
possessed of the infrequent bob and the elusive half-
dollar to indulge in modest wickedness. The abolition of
the second divvy will simply increase the value of the
first. Even the wildest, maddest Jud cannot expect it to
do anything else. Another clause decrees that the
machines shall cease doing business ten minutes before
each race; this was apparently inserted at the urgent
request of Cohen O’Flaherty.
* * * *
Considering how often a gee-gee- gets beaten
because the rider didn’t “put any money on it,” The
Bulletin is almost moved to tears by the story of how
Hawkeye came to be disqualified at Moonee Valley
(Melb.) races the other week. The trouble with
Hawkeye was that its amateur rider, Mr. Rea,
did put money on the animal. When he weighed
out he had 40 sovs. in the pocket of his riding
pants, and the clerk passed him as all right. After
saddling up, the sanguine jockey handed 20 of the sovs.
to a friend to put them upon Hawkeye. But in thus
taking them off the horse he lightened its burden by six
ounces, and what with his profuse perspiration and ex-
haustion of nervous tissue during the race the
triumphant amateur couldn’t draw the weight on return-
ing to scale.
• * * *
At a recent meeting in the Poverty Bay district
(M.L.) the “ silent tote ” made a bad blunder. An investor
calmly walked up and bought a ticket on the prad that
was ; leading by lengths, with only a few yards to go.
Then the bewildered onlookers rushed up to repeat the
dodge, but the man in the machine suddenly became
aware that the race had just finished. Theblame did not
lie with the tote, but with the steward, whose duty it is
to ring the “ close up tote ” bell, and had forgotten to
do so.
• • ■* *
Colin McKenzie, who has been doing big cricket
things around Melbourne lately, is a nephew of ex-
Minister McKenzie. Eight years ago he left Scots College
in a hurry, fearing blindness (both his father and uncle
have been afflicted that way), but two years’ rest fixed
him up, and for awhile past he has been the star cricketer
of the Seymour district (Vic.).
* * * *
The names of the first Australian team which
visited England in ’7B are worth recalling, now that the
best of them has gone to play in the Elysian fields :
Harry F. Boyle, J. Conway (manager), Tommy Horan,
Jack McCarthy Blackham, Frank Allan, Dave Gregory,
Tom Garrett, Alick Bannerman, Charlie Bannerman, W.
L. Murdoch, F. R. Spofforth, Midwinter (who died
three or four years ago) and G. Bailey, of Tas.
Everyone knows how that splendid combination put the
reputation of Australian cricket on a firm basis when it
accomplished the feat of putting the M.C.C.—practically
an all-England eleven—through for 33 and 19 runs—
Boyle taking 3 wickets for 14 and 6 for 3, and
“Spoff ” 6 for 4 and 4 for IG—and winning by
nine wickets. The long, lean six-footer went to
England with five teams after that, the last time as
manager. In bowling he “ broke ” but little, from the
leg side, but he kept a splendid length, with persistent
accuracy, and he got his wickets mainly by picking the
batsman's weak spot. His fielding was as good as his
bowling, and the unique position he created—at close
mid-on—has never been filled so well by anyone else.
“ C. and b. Boyle” used once to be a standing line in the
cricket report's. A thin, brown, wiry man, grey of late
years, but straight as a rush always and in every way,
Harry Boyle played the game for his side, not for his own
reputation.
The Australian Board of Control would be
justified in asking Joe Darling to resign his position on
the Selection Committee, which is to choose Australia’s
reps in the test matches. Darling, it is announced, is off
to Tasmania to look after his station, and doesn’t intend
to play inter-State cricket. It isn’t reasonable to suppose
that a man is of much value as a judge when he hasn’t
seen the teams at play. Young players who are battling
for a place in the team view with dismay Darling's
absence from these matches, and his fondness for players
of old Australian Xl’s.
• ■* * *
Official figures of the Yic. v. M.C.C. match
give the attendance at 40,332, of which only 19,427 paid
at the g-ates, while 20,895 walked through on members’
passes. More than half the attendance, therefore,
yielded no profit to the Board of Control—a circumstance
which emphasises the need of a percentage from mem-
bers’ fees as well as from the gate receipts.
