Victoria’s total of 699 against S.A. is the tallest
by the Bent State in rep. matches. Armstrong’s 231
constitutes a record for a Victorian against S.A. ; while
the same player also has the highest tally for a Vic.
player against N.S.W. *
Captain A. O. Jones, of the M.C.C. team, is a
good footballer as well as a cricketer, and one of the best
referees in England.
Sydney Grammar School-boy E. P. Barbour
keeps up a run of big* scores in Great Public Schools
cricket, and last week scored 112 runs out of a total of
224 against S.C.E.G.S. Yet no steps are taken to try
this brilliant boy cricketer in better company.
Saturday last saw the First Grade Cricket com-
petition resumed in Sydney, and Victor Trumper, having
recovered from the effect of the awful Limerick about the
“champagne of cricket,” was himself again. He divided
the slogging with Noble, and the pair smacked up 206 in
65min., Trumper’s little lot being 128. No less than 94 of
the total came through boundary strokes. This is a
solemn warning against allowing ribald newspapers to
make Limericks about men who have to uphold the honor
of the country, and the outcome will probably be an
arrangement by which a man who has been Limericked
will be allowed to stand down till he has recovered from
the effects. Other men who wielded the willow
mightily were Nicholls and Barnes, of Redfern, who col-
lared the Burwood bowling for 122 and 100 respectively.
Noble carried his bat out for 64, and the great little Syd
Gregory left 63 on the board when he went pavilion-
wards. E. L. Waddy made 98 for Central Cumberland
against Middle Harbor, and in the bye-match against
University, Folkard, of Callan Park, totted up 166, and
was going strong when the stumps were drawn. Spite of
all this fine batting the bowlers also put up some fine
figures. Wadsworth, of Redfern, knocked down four of
Bunvood’s for seven runs. Johnson, of North Sydney,
smashed up six Balmain wickets for 45 ; Pitt, of Gordon,
got five of Sydney’s wickets for 52; and M’Oloy, .of
Paddington, downed six Petersham men for 64. The
only fly in the ointment is that they do not do these sort
of things against the Englishmen—but there’s plenty of
time yet.
* ■» » •*
Prince Alfred Yacht Club’s ocean race for the
Petrel Cup, on Saturday, appropriately enough took
place under stormy conditions. The nor’-east gale ripped
great gullies in the waters, and the resultant cliffs fell
down with roar and surge, burying the yachts and sweep-
ing over the decks of the steamer Robbie Burns in a way
that made that stout ship behave in a very inebriated
manner. And the “ wild white horses of the Lord ” went
racing past, flashing in the sunlight, smothering the
yachts and making navigation more than risky. Nine of
them had faced the starter, Rawhiti and Culwulla being
on the scratch line. Magic came late and got away 15min.
late, went outside, looked at the leaping ocean, and
seeing the hopelessness of making up time in such a sea,
wisely returned. White Wings came out under a full
head, making a brave show with topsail and all,
but only got as far as Taylor Bay, in the Harbor,
where the bucket blew out, and the skipper lowered
away and retired. Culwulla walked through the fleet
and was leading till opposite North Head, where some-
thing went' wrong, and Mr. Marks hove to, effected re-
pairs, and again rushed to the front. Approaching the
mark, which was laid five miles nor’-east of South Reef,
Awanui flew through the seas like a bird and swept round
the mark five seconds ahead of Culwulla, behind which
came Norman Murray in the gallant little Heather, with
Rawhiti plunging along in close pursuit. As they came
round the spinnakers flashed out, and the yachts bounded
away on the race back to port. So fast they flew that
when the Robbie Burns had finished fishing up the round-
ing buoy they were out of sight, and all details lost, ex-
cepting that Rawhiti passed Heather on the homeward
rush and that Awanui, maintaining the lead, beat Culwulla
by 2min. ssec.,and Culwulla led Rawhiti by lmin. 35sec.,
with Heather 2£min. behind Rawhiti.
* * * *
Although the season has only just begun,
Awanui has done big credit to Saxton. In five starts the
splendid boat has scored four wins and one third. No
wonder the Melbourne people fear for the Sayonara Cup.
* * * *
Pair-oared Championship of Victoria over the
mile lower-Yarra course on Saturday afternoon caused
a surprise. An Essendon junior pair obtained a foot
win alter a desperate battle with Civil Service—who
really deserved to win, if correct rowing goes for any-
thing. Six crews started, three being paddled by top-
notch inter-State men, but their reputation went for
nothing, as they were blotted out before half-way. The
dead birds were Parks and Mercs, and the knowing
brigade fell to zero when they fizzled early. Righfon to
the finish gritty Billy Anketell kept Service mostly in
front, till Essendon assumed command nearing the old
river.. Anketell came at ’em, but the extra stone in front
told its tale, and Essendon got the verdict by a foot in
the very slick time of smin. 23 3-ssec. Parks, two
lengths away, were third. T’others survived the ordeal.
The win upset all calculations, and the Parks and Mercs
haven’t quite got over it yet. On the day, first and
second crews showed undisputed championship form.
* * * *
After a desperate struggle, N.S.W. champion
sculler Tresidder has raised £3OO of the £5OO stake for the
championship race. .Unless some more generous response
is made to the appeal for subscriptions, it is more than
possible that the race may fall through after all. The
editor of the Sunday Sun, Sydney, has appealed to Corn-
stalks to put up the balance just to give the old State a
chance of regaining the honor it held so long. Already
Maoriland is in a fever of excitement, and the whole popu-
lation of the Isles threatens to heave itself into Wanganui
to see the struggle. The same whole population recently
had a narrow escape from falling down dead en masse,
some person, variously described as a scoundrel, lunatic,
idiot and cow, having started a rumor that Webb had
broken a blood vessel. However, it turned out to be the
usual untruthful rumor, and Maoriland placed both hands
over its heart and drew a long breath of relief.
* * * *
Maoriland admirers of world’s champion sculler
William Webb presented him with a cheque for £435 and
a gold watch the other day, and the lionising is said to be
not half through yet.
* * * *
After much newspaper correspondence and
defiance the match between Mr. Davies’ champion boat
Fairbanks and Mr. Knowles’ Baby Simplex is off. Hard
to say who is to blame—if that is the correct expression—
and a general idea is that one is afraid and the other not
game. Davies wants to race over the Shark Island-
Manly championship course, and will agree to no other.
Knowles wants to try conclusions over the measured
knot, and won’t consent to any other water. Both have
said “ Yah ! ” and there the matter stands. In the mean-
time, there is no word from Mort’s Dock about building
that fast boat to hoist the Australian flag over Austral-
asian waters.
* * * *
Freely stated in British yachting circles that
there will be no more racing for the America Cup.
Under present conditions the contest has degenerated
into a farce—a struggle between a racing shell and a sea-
going yacht. The Yankees, holding the Cup, can build
any sort of gimcrack speed-machine capable of holding
together for a few weeks, while British challengers have
to build a vessel capable of battling across the Atlantic
on its own; and it would be straight-out murder and
suicide to risk lives in a craft of the Reliance type on an
Atlantic passage.
* # ■* «
The Australian cycling slump has spread to
Maoriland. A recent meeting of the M.L. Wheelmen’s
League spluttered out for want of a quorum, the total
attendance being the secretary, the chairman, and a
solitary delegate.
* * -X *
“0.K.”: Predictions of failure for the Austral
Wheel meeting, at the M.C.O. Ground, strike me as being
in direct opposition to probability. In the years when
Melbourne’s biggest cycling event was run on the grass
track, the M. 8.0. made always a large profit, which dis-
appeared when the fixture was transferred to the banked
asphalt track at the Exhibition Oval. Evidently the
public in general preferred the Cricket Ground as a show
place, and now that it offers increased comfort in seating
accommodation, it is not likely to be less preferred. As
for the disadvantages of a level grassed track, they are
not seriously considered by the thousands of sightseers
who care nothing for “records” in bike racing. There
are no new objections to Melbourne Cricket Ground as a
racecourse for wheelmen. It has lost nothing that it,
formerly possessed. The wooden track (which is said to
be an indispensable requirement) was never there. More-
over, the M.C.C. will not .permit a wooden track on the
ground. The M. 8.0. is glad to take what it can
get, and it gets the best show place in the
city on the same terms as of old. The racing
will be more attractively managed than it was in
the palmy days of yore, for under the new conditions
everybody, except the men on the extreme front and
back marks, will be botli paced and chased, in a measure.
Also the handicapper will have the power to readjust the
scale of distances for the finals, if necessary. Cyclists
ihust, in the natural course of things, be conspirators to
some extent, even in the best contested races, but as far
as the public can judge the game, a fast track is more
favorable to combination tactics than a slow one. In
short, an Austral on the grass is likely to be a rather
better display of individual pluck and endurance than an
Austral on the wood. Why then should the forthcoming
meeting be foredoomed to failure ?
* * * . «
The latest brand of madman scatters tacks on
the high roads as a means of being offensive to cyclists
and motorists. He makes the roads dangerous not only
to them, but also to horses, dogs, and human beings. Some
enormous tacks have lately been scattered about the
roads of the western suburbs of Sydney, and so far more
horses than motors have been injured. The .only motor
that punctured was one conveying a doctor to attend a
sick woman, and owing to the tyre being punctured he
took a long time over the journey, and an unfortunate
woman had about an hour’s unnecessary agony in conse-
quence. When that woman’s husband meets a man
scattering tacks in the roadway there will probably be a
death in the tack lunatic’s family. On the other hand,
this scattering of tacks suggests that motorists are gene-
rally unpopular, owing to the vagaries of a few hogs. Yet
most of the motorists around Sydney are decent sorts, as
the spectacle of the Automobile Club taking for an outing
in their cars 200 little orphans from. the Bandwick
Asylum, on Saturday last, suggests.
4c * * .
E. L. Apperley, who was runner-up for the
N.S.W. amateur golf championship, played for last week,
is but a youngster, and is generally supposed to be
N.S.W.’s hope as a rising amateur star against the
Victorian golfers, who rule the roost in Commonwealth
golf matters. His pa is H. W. Apperley, boss of the
A.M.P. Industrial Branch. He generally perambulates
the landscape after the elusive golf ball at Killara, on the
North Shore line, where a few gullies and hilltops have
been roped round and advertised as golf links. On the
civilised “Boyal’s” links at Bose Bay he was at a dis-
advantage. Yet he finished the final round all square
With E. P. Simpson, a Boyal’s champion. On playing an
extra hole, the 37th, Apperley junior lost by one stroke.
* * » *
J. T. Lake, the Adelaide civilian rifle-shot,
whom many people wanted to send to Bisley instead of
Lieutenant Addison, has been steadily beating the English
King’s Prize winner since his return. In the big indivi-
dual match at Adelaide—the British team’s last—Lake
beat the whole held, Baven, the British sailor, being
second, and Addison fourteenth. Lake is a King’s Prize
winner in four States.
* * * *
Australia has a sporting interest in G. H.
Druce’s claim to the Portland title and estates. A
previous Duke of Portland was the purchaser of Carbine,
and the bones of that late quadruped are included in the
family estates.
* * * *
Edison’s reported announcement that he has
perfected a storage battery that will drive a motor car
50,000 miles without being recharged, has caused lots of
seasoned motor men to wink the other eye and tap their
temples expressively. Either Edison has achieved some-
thing like a large hunk of perpetual motion, or he is
breaking down, or he is the victim of some wild-minded
reporter.
The Wade Ministry is introducing a Motor
Begulation Bill in N.S.W., which will, if passed in its
present state, do a lot to restrain the Hog in the Car.
Speed, lights, bells, and other alarms are all regulated,
and cars are to be registered and drivers licensed.
Upon conviction for any offence against the Act,
registration may be withdrawn, and the owner of
the car will have to show good cause why it
should be restored. One thing very necessary is that
the registered number of the car should be painted in
large conspicuous figures on its side and back, so that, in
the event of any callous brute trying to make off after
having injured a pedestrian, recognition may be easy.
The Bill will have the enthusiastic support of the
automobilists, who are more anxious, as a body, than
anyone else to suppress the Hog. The Hog makes the
tacks grow on the suburban road, and is an enemy to
automen and humanity in general.
* *■ * »
The Australian abroad, in the person of S. F.
Edge, has proved what he can do in the motor line, and
now the “Butlin Change Speed Gear,” which was in-
vented and made in Sydney, proves that the Australian
at home is not a helpless user of imported notions.
This new apparatus makes it impossible to strip the gear,
and will considerably reduce the danger of hiringout cars
to fools. It also makes it possible to avoid some of the
accidents that have brought motors into disrepute, as the
car can be reversed when going at full speed without
stripping the clutch. The inventor has gone to Europe
to stagger motoring humanity with the new idea, and
deserves to do well.
* * * *
“J.”: Saw a startling sight in Sydney’s Cen-
tennial Park the other night. Was cycling along with a
companion, doing about 10 miles an hour, when a man
suddenly shot past us. He seemed to be walking, but
loomed up about 7ft. 6in. in the starlight. We started off
in pursuit to investigate. The giant looked over his
shoulder, and immediately put on pace ; and it was not
till half-a-mile had been cut out close up to record that
we ranged up, one on either side. “ Are you a jim-jam ?”
I asked as pleasantly as I could in my puffed state. “ No,”
he replied cheerfully, “ not yet 1 ” i‘ Well, what are you ? ”
“ I’m a pneumatic skater ! ” he replied, and then went on
to inquire if we had anything cool about us. Afterwards,
under the lee of a rook, we extracted the cork with a
pocket knife, and he showed us his feet. Fastened to his
boot was a light steel framework, from which protruded
a pair of forks, bicycle-like, back and front.
Each pair of forks was fitted with a pneu-
matic-tyred wheel of about eight inches diameter.
1 asked him was it a new invention, but he said it was a
very old one, dating back some eight or nine years, but
which had failed to catch-on owing to the frequency of
punctures. He claims that a man travelled on the
apparatus from Bulawayo to Capetown in half the time
a horseman could have done, and made a great and scare-
ful impression on the natives and Boers as he fled
through. About 15 miles an hour seems to be the limit
of safe travelling on an ordinary road. On asphalted
tracks, like those of the Centennial Park, he claims to be
able to work up to 20 or 22 miles an hour.
* » * *
The Northern Union Rugby game is described
by A. H. Baskerville, who is running the professional
“ All Blacks ” in the Ma Land, as a faster and better game
than the amateur. There is more skill and less bullock -
inginit; also a great deal of “passing,” and not much
kicking into touch. The Northerners do still kick into
touch, but the ball must fall into the field of play ; it
can then roll or bounce over the touch line. This reduces
line-finding to a fine art. The wing-forward finds no place
under Northern Union rules. He is half-back, in the
true sense of the term, and he isn’t allowed to advance in
front of his back-row forwards until the ball is quite clear
of the scrum. There are no ridiculous formalities about a
free kick, as there are in the amateur game. When the
latter has a “ free,” a placer lies dourn, often in the mud,
aud wastes time while the kicker places the ball in posi-
tion. Then there is more fooling round until the kick is
delivered. The Northern Union man places the ball him-
self, and has a “ free ” kick in every sense of the word,
his opponents not even being allowed to charge his
attempt.
“Soccer” football, as played in England:
Chelsea (London) Club, being at the top of the Second
division of the League last season, was promoted to the
First. Apparently it found the company very different
there, for, by the middle of October, it had only won one
match and lost six. This meant sinking to the Second
division again at the end of the season. So the directors
(the club is a registered company, like most of its rivals)
got to work and “searched the country from end to end
for capable players,” who would be willing to move, and
whose clubs would let. them move, for in England you
mustn’t poach, but have to buy. At latest advices, the
Londoners had strengthened their eleven with two of the
best men in Lancashire, and were beginning to win
matches. One of the two, a goal-kicker, “is said to have
cost the southerners very nearly four figures,” all of
which would go to the funds of bis old club. The induce-
ment offered to the player himself is a secret; there is a
pious fiction that £4 a week is the maximum wage, and
must never be exceeded. But, in that case, why do
about a third of all the crack performers change owners
every season ?
* * * *
The Vic. Football Association, which kicks
itself at times during the summer to make sure it isn’t
dead, lately cut off another piece of its nose to spite its
face by wiping out West Melbourne and North Melbourne
as clubs, because they had applied for admission to the
Football League. “ North ” wasn’t much loss, but
“ West,” which was runner-up last winter, and Richmond,
which departed on its own, were payable propositions;
and as “South Oss ” is now playing speaks with the
League once more, and wants to drop the Association, it
looks as if that feeble-minded body will be amateur all
right—amateur because it won’t be able to be anything
else.

“Snell” : I was present at that championship
“ ’rastle ” in Melbourne, the other night, after which the
public was much more tired than the competitors. As
explained, Buttan Singed, or Singh, or something, was to
hoist a “pairitcb” expert “fra Glaskie awa,” but
champion Willie Withers Bain declined at the 11th hour,
in consequence of some difficulty about the bawbees, and
a lesser light named locally Peter Burris —which is the
Australian for Peter Barmon apparently—was put up to
take a wind out of the Indian. The public howled for its
ducats, but it was practically told to go hang, and when
the one man who stood up for restitution of public beans
was torn down by several plug-uglies, and fired into the
cold, wet night, the audiencebecame abjectly decorous, and
the wrestle proceeded. Sometimes it was a wrestle, at
other times it was a prize fight with the “ raws,” and
again it was a realistic representation of two fierce he-
sheep trying to butt each other’s brains out. Then, fora
term, it was pure wrangle and barney. The last half-hour
was devoted to mere pawing; and when it was all over
there was another frantic wrangle. The promoters
wrangled with the umpire; private managers, cornermen,
and casual acquaintances wrangled with the promoters;
and theaudieuceclimbedon the chairs and yelled aimlessly.
The promoters, wrestlers, manager, hangers-on, rela-
tions, and bottle-holders retired to the dressing-room in
a clump, still wrangling, and at midnight their frenzied
disputation was filling Exhibition-street with vehement
echoes. At the time of writing no news of a decision has
been made public. Possibly the wrangle is still on. If
10 people get talked to death it will be a splendid thing
for wrestling in Australia.
• * * *
The Moodie-Sullivan scrap, at Sydney Gaiety,
on Saturday night, was a series of curious reversals. At
first Moodie, the Victorian, got so thoroughly pasted
that it took the combined cursing of all his seconds to
keep him going. He was receiver-general till, in the
fourth round, he got a fearful bash that woodened him
for about five seconds. Then he arose all wrath
and baresark, and waded in to Sullivan in a way
that staggered the Sydneyite, and quickly wiped out
the margin of marks, bruises and contusions. This cheer-
ful rally lasted to the eighth round, when his baresark
fury suddenly petered out, and he quitted. The most
surprised man in the crowd was the victor, who was just
beginning to realise that he was getting a devil of a
hiding. By the way, why the authorities allow this
building to be crammed and jammed to bursting point by
fight patrons when it is condemned as a theatre is beyond
human understanding. *
“ There are only four punches after all that are
any real good to a fighting-man,” said “ Billy ” Jordan,
of Chicago, t’other day. And therein William, who is now
73 years old, and who has followed boxing since the days
of Jem Mace, uttered a great truth. The punches he
nominates are (i) a right to the jaw, viz., to the chin.
Here the fighter punches W'ith a downward slant, and no
man on earth can take a hit thus administered and re-
cover quickly enough to avoid a knock-out. Then
there are (2) a left to the stomach, and (3) the
right under the heart. Latter is the hit that
is w’orth all the others. It takes the life out of the
stricken battler, and sickens him and bends him up with
pain. Who hasn’t seen the little man in the street-fight,
who knew T what he was about, settle a dangerous-looking
adversary with this blow ? Number 4 in Jordan’s list is
the cross-counter —effective, but difficult to bring off.
These are the hits that the big fighter, careful as he is of
his hands, puts all his beef into. If any one of them comes
off it means the registering of a knock-out.

After 14 years’ absence from Australia, Tom
Burrows, champion club-swinger of the world, comes
home again with quite a heap of records, the best (or
w’orst) of which is that of swinging continuously a pair
of 41b. clubs for 61 hours 45 minutes— a mighty piece of
useless endurance.
By no manner of means do all the
“nearly-drowned” cases on the Sydney surf
beaches get to a knowledge of the public.
Most often the unfortunate is just yanked
out by a casual rescuer, spread out on
the coast to dry, and nothing more
is said. An instance : Colonel Wells, A.F. A., on
Sunday, single-handed, brought a man to shore
who had got into difficulties and was crying out
for help. It is only the organised rescue as a rule
that gets into the papers ; the often far more
plucky individual save gets no limelight. By the
way, a new' danger is added to the beaches—
mischievous horse-play by larrikins, who try to
help the hungry waves to victims by “ducking”
weaklings.
WOEMS:
INFALLIBLE SYMPTOMS.
Intestinal worms afflict both young and old,
but are more frequently found in children than
adults. They are generally the result of defective
digestion, depraved appetite, or unwholesome
food. The long thread worm and the pin worm
are the most common, and their presence in the
bowels is accountable for the peevishness and
irritability of children. A child who is constantly
hungry is invariably suffering from worms, as the
worms cause a gnawing and distressing sensation
when the bowels are partly empty. A depraved
appetite and a disposition to pick the nose are
infallible symptoms of the complaint, which
should not be neglected. Comstock’s “ Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets are perfectly harmless to
the most delicate constitution, but are a safe and
sure remedy for the eradication of worms. Ob-
tainable at all chemists and stores, or from The
W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd., 58 Pitt Street,
Sydney. Price, Is. 6d. per box (six boxes, Bs.).

SQUARE-TREAD TYRES
Here and there some other make of tyre secures a brief tenancy of
local popularity, but in every country in the world where Motoring
is indulged in to any practical extent Continental Square Tread
Tyres are used and appreciated by experienced Motorists. Here
at Home in Australia —-in England, France, Germany, the United
States, South Africa, India—go where you will—the great
majority of cars are fitted with Continental Tyres. This world-
wide choice of Continental Tyres is due to their never'-failing
durability and reliability.
MELBOURNE DEPOT: 46-48 MARKET STREET.
SYDNEY DEPOT: CLARENCE STREET.
“Rhode Master” : Be the often-discussed
state of the Vic. railway tracks, please allow me
to affirm, on my word of honor as a Bulletin
contributor who avoids the Aboliars’ page, that
said tracks are all right. Not once, for the last
eight years, has a requisition for funds for re-
newals, made by the engineers’ branch, been
reduced. To the regular railway traveller the
most familiar sights along the tracks are the
camps of special gangs. These men are engaged
strengthening the tracks. Two splendid socialistic
quarries—one at Broadford, near the line, the other
at Penshurst—are sending out ballast as fast as
trains can handle it. Enormous stacks of sleepers
of good quality lie at every few stations ready for
loading or distribution. The re-ballasting and re-
sleepering have been going on without cessation
for the last 10 years. The main lines have in
the same period been re-laid with 80-lb. steel rails,
and the less important lines with lighter steel
rails, and very few miles of iron rails now remain.
The system of inspection is not, as stated by an
outside witness at the Moranding accident in-
quiry, hurried and inadequate. If that witness
had charge of a hundred-mile length for twelve
months he would be surprised at the require-
ments of his supervising officers. The tracks will
carry the new engine, and at 60 miles an hour.
On the main Sydney-Melbourne line, the south-
bound express daily cuts out the 25 miles from
Wallan to Essendon in 25 to 30 minutes, usually
the former. I’ve timed it any number of times,
and the running is so smooth that the jaundiced
N.S.W. traveller will hardly believe the evidence
of his own imported stop-watch. I’m rather
proud of the Vic. railway equipment, and no evil-
minded paper is going to pour obloquy on it with-
out hearing from me.
The crow’s-foot elm of North Queensland,
mentioned in Bulletin (31/10/’O7) as being a suit-
able timber for chair-making, is also an excellent
wood for rifle stocks. Queensland Director of
Forests McMahon has been trying—with some
success, it is said—to persuade the Commonwealth
authorities to use it in connection with the con-
templated manufacture of small arms. The tim-
ber is widely distributed and easily obtainable
throughout N.Q.
“ Curlew ”: Probably there is no town in
Australia so entirely foreign as Tanunda (S. A.).
It is the centre of a German settlement. The
sign-boards above the various stores and hotels
and the brass-plate of the doctor, all point to this.
The only thing carrying an Anglo-Saxon label is
the branch bank (there is no German bank at all
in Australia), and this is barely needed, as the
Germans with money lend it to their needy
neighbors. The famous Seppeltsfield, the second
largest winery in the world, is in this district.
Here 60 men are permanently employed, and, of
course, numbers of extra hands during the busy
season. All are Germans. The one Englishman
on the place is the Government Excise officer.
Curious what man will do to make his moustache
—proud banner of manhood- grow. Some have used lard
to advantage, others recommend kerosene, others have
whims and fancies. But almost every man desires a
“ mo.” As to how to get one : Writer knew of a man
who, the other day, went to meet his brother, whom he
had not seen for some little time, on the railway station.
After a while, he began to speculate whether his brother
had come by the train that had just arrived, when a man
resembling his brother came up to him. Then he
recognised him to be his brother ; but what an altered
man to what he had been several months previously !
“ Where did you get that fine moustache from, old man ? ”
he asked. He admitted having used a course of Capilla-
rine from Oraig and Aitken, of 654 George-street, Sydney,
who are the sole agents of this well-known moustache
producer. AH the “ mo.” had cost him was 10s. 6d., the
price of a ]x>ttle Of Capillarine.*

IN the time of the Arabian Nights the chief form of amusement was
telling stories. They had no books. Professional story-tellers went
around and amused everybody who was able to pay. This idea
of being amused is as old as the human race. “Give us something
to entertain us,” has been the constant cry. The answer in this day
and age is the Edison Phonograph.
Everyone can have his own story-teller, his own songster, his own
band-master, right in his own home. The man who “ kept a poet
is not in it with the man who keeps an Edison Phonograph.
Instal a genuine Edison Phonograph in your home this Christmas.
No other gift gives such lasting pleasure. Everybody likes it,
from baby to grandmother.
No skill is required to reproduce perfectly brilliant band music,
the finest vocal solos, duets and quartettes, the funniest character
songs and vaudeville skits.
THE EDISON PHONOGRAPH
(Buy one for Christmas)
provides more fun and music than any other automatic entertainer—
yet is within the reach of all. Hear it at your nearest dealers before
deciding on any Christmas present.

“Alb.”: Rev. J. Conraee, S.J., Jesuit
Provincial (that is to say, Inspector-General) for
Ireland and Australia, is on this continent. He
has ended in South Australia and
Victoria* and is in N.S. W. as I write. It is inter-
esting to speculate on what he is about. There is
an impression that the Jesuits may be removed as
pastors, but will remain as teachers. There is as
much animosity against the Jesuits in the R. 0.
Church as outside. Archbishop Goold gave them
an opening in Melbourne somewhere about 1800,
after the diocese had been in existence 13 years.
They were astonishingly successful, in the persons
of Father Dalton and Father Moore. Dalton
built the great church of St. Ignatius in Rich-
mond, while Moore built the fine one of
the Immaculate Conception, at Mike Lynch’s
Grace Park,. Hawthorn. Such works shone
with lustre in the feeble time of Goold.
But it was otherwise when the commanding
Archbishop Carr came along in 1887. He could
beat any Jesuit for church-building with his
opportunities. The Jesuits only accomplished
one other great enterprise, St. Xavier’s College,
Hawthorn. Father Dalton went to Sydney,
where he tuned affairs up by building Riverview
College. The Jesuits don t work with other
priests. It will be observed that they have their
establishments to themselves. Consequently,
they are virtually outside the jurisdiction of
prelates like Cardinal Moran and Archbishop
Carr, and only accountable to their General in
Rome (now General Wernz). This is a continual
thorn to a masterful man like Archbishop Carr.
Two of his principal churches, St. Ignatius and
the Immaculate, are beyond his control. He is
very Skilful in shifting priests about, round pegs
for round holes, but nob a Jesuit can he touch.
They stick together like wax. It stands to
reason that there is more or less incompetence,
especially as men grow older. Father Conmee
is splitting differences somehow.
“Edy”: With me, necessity alone is the
mother of intervention. The other day, I added to
my long list of striking examples of the folly of
taking part in other people’s troubles. There was
a stolid circle of tired men and phlegmatic women
who looked as if they had been hastily summoned
from sordid, domestic duties, standing on the
suburban roadside. Inside the circle, one man
was holding another down. The top man had a
black eye, and his shirt was in strips; his nose
bled freely on to the man below. The under man
was being held down without ceremony,
his nose was pushed violently into the
dust, he was 'biting the public estate.
Obviously, the top man’s one ambition in life was
to keep the other fellow down. Obviously, too,
it was a worthy cause. But presently a large man
off a fish cart pushed through the ring and assumed
command. “Ga-a-art, le’ ther man up,” he said.
Fish-o was a fine fellow, with a splendid sense of
English justice. “Be ’anged fi do,” replied the
man on top. “Let ’im up ! ” snorted Fish-o, and
he spanked the other off his victim. Then the
under man got to work again. He was on
his feet in half-a-second, and there was
something doing. Fish-o represented the dis-
turbed centre. The mischief revolved round
him like a pack of hounds round a hapless
cat. The ungracious wretch he had just rescued
was dealing with Fish-o, biting, kicking, clawing,
gouging, and tearing hair. Before the blessed
peacemaker could summon up his resources, he
was reduced to the condition of a blasted and
blighted object. After a terrible fight, very trying
to a fat man, Fish-o got the disturbing element
down again, and sat on him, and now it was his
turn to push the fanatic’s nose into the dust, and
hold it there with the determination of an anxious
man convinced that dear life depended on his
maintaining existing conditions. The driver of a
brewer’s lorry was the next to push in. He insisted
on British fair play, and pulled Fish-o off. Fish-o
fled to his cart while the lorry driver fought his
hand, and five minutes later the lorryman was
holding the nose of the insurrectionist hard against
the macadam, and praying for brighter days.
For half-an-hour the lorry driver held the zealot
firmly to the highway, and then five policemen
and a van removed the violent maniac to an
adjacent cell. The lorry driver retired hurt.
Possibly he got the sack for dilatory conduct in
the pursuit of his duty. Moral: Man was born
to trouble, but he needn’t get down to pick up the
other fellow’s inheritance.
The old idea of the superiority of imported foot-
ware is now proved to be a fallacy, and it is recognised
that the locally-made boot or shoe, manufactured from
the best of Australian leather by skilled labor, is in every
way equal, if not superior, to the imported in both wear
and appearance. His Excellency Lord Northcote has
recognised this, and has appointed Mr. VV. Leeming, pro-
prietor of Leeming’s Boot Stores, 109 Swanston-street,
Melbourne, as his bootmaker.*
Dear B.—Kindly hurry up your trans-
continental railway, and bring your south-eastern
seaboard a week or two nearer white civilisation.
After three trips to and from Australia I am
satisfied that your alleged mail service is a
proposition in scrap heaps. The fourteen-knot
crawl —the present travelling rate of the sub-
sidised steamers —is worthy of the old South Sea
whalers of fifty years ago. In 1885, as a boy, I
made my first trip round the (Jape of Good Hope
to Australia, in the s.s. Aberdeen, Captain Barclay
in command. Time of journey, about seven weeks.
Twenty-two years later, on an alleged modern liner,
geared and installed with “racing’’ engines, I
performed the journey to London, via the Red
Sea—i n s ix weeks. The need for frantic haste in
the matter of sea-travel may not be apparent
to the Australian, removed as he is from the whirl
and fight of modern speed competition. But once
in Europe, the man from the South discovers
that his country is situated somewhere near
Wilkes Land, in the Antarctic regions. And
the European farmer grasps this fact the
moment he enters one of our emigration offices.
“ Six weeks to get there! ” says he. “ Six hells ! ”
I have spoken to dozens of well-to-do English
farmers on the question of emigration ; I have
attended several lectures given by the representa-
tives of the N.S.W. Agricultural Department,
and while many of the listeners were enthusiastic
about Australia as a wool and wheat growing
country, they take their money and their large
families to the shipping office that will put them
on an iceberg or a frost-bound forest within seven
or eight days. The big Liverpool shipping com-
panies are teaching the agriculturist the art of
hurrying up. And he hurries—over ice-hum-
mocks and Indian reservations, where the scalp still
dries in the wigwam, and where frost bites harder
than our Gulf mosquitoes. Writer is unacquainted
with the Commonwealth’s proposed new time-
service. He is certain, however, that efforts will
be made to improve on the old system by a few
hours only. With a transcontinental railway and
a Lusitania speed-rate, Australia could be reached
comfortably within three weeks. The Canadian
and U. 3. liners that fail to put up an 18-knot
service are soon relegated to the cattle-carrying
trade. Australia is content with a 14-knot slouch,
and Australia has Jar more need to worry about
time and space than the U. 9. or even Canada.—
Albert Dorrington.

healthy with a sluggish liver or inactive kidneys; sometimes these organs get throw i
out of action on account of improper food or drink, sometimes from a fau y
metabolism. Nature then requires assistance, and it is of paramount importan
that the right aid should be employed in dealing with these delicate pieces of
mechanism. It has been known from time immemorial that certain European spas

REV. E. R. GIBBENS
WRITES :
“Wesleyan Methodist Church, Tonbridge,

The Inky Way.
“F.H.R.”: Recently a writer on the “Red Page”
advised the newchuni story-writer to, above all things,
“light his pipe.” Now, I’m a man and a non-smoker,
but I’m no story-writer—worse luck. I’d buy a pipe and
a plug. though, if I thought it would make me write ;
but, being of Scotch descent, I’d like to know before
going to such expense. At present I have my doubts.
“G. B. Lancaster,” I believe, is a woman. Does she smoke ?
If so, does she find any ideas that way ? I’d like to have
Roderic Quinn’s, Alf Brasch’s and Dyson’s ideas about it.
The only time I really feel like writing is when it’s raining
heavily ; and sub-editors can thank their stars that I live
in a droughty part.
* * * *
The linotypes of Maoriland Dominion, the squattah’s
new organ, do something to brighten up a dull sheet. For
instance:—“ Mr. Maxwell, the caretaker of the forest,
possesses hens which lay all the year round bacon of the
most nourishing description and milk of the richest
quality.” If the caretaker of the forest could only cross
them with something that would drop into the nest an
occasional million to reduce the national debt, Maori-
land’s future would be assured.
• » * *
Australia’s most knowledgable taxidermist is an inky
wayfarer— G. A. Heartland, of Melbourne Age publishing
staff. A little while ago he sold his famous collection of
Australian birds’ eggs, numbering over 600 varieties, to
Malcolm M’Eaeharn. At his home near Coburg he has a
duplicate collection, and an almost equally complete one of
stuffed indigenous birds and reptiles. Heartland puts in
his spare time lecturing to literary clubs and instructing
the younger members of the Vic. Naturalists’ Association
in the art of taxidermy.
» » * *
The Australian Star has its own ideas about things.
For instance (U/11/’07):
LATEST SPORTING
TERRIBLE TRAM ACCIDENT
EXPLOSION OF A BOILER
TWO MEN KILLED AND SEVERAL INJURED
A standing heading, “ Murder and Other Amusements,”
will probably follow.
• * * *
Adelaide Register had to do some waiting; but the
chance came at last. Years ago the rival Advertiser
printed a Register leader alongside one from a bygone
English paper, and there wasn’t as much difference
betvveen the two as you could spread out on a butter-
man’s conscience. Now the Register smacks its withered
old lips as it prints, in the same sort of parallel columns,
the Advertiser’s latest “dramatic criticisms ” along with
extracts from Paunch, and Table Talk and other papers.
* * * *
“E.D.” : Noting Mr. Bent’s outburst about the cussed-
ness of papers that take Government advertisements and
abuse the Government, reminds me that 1 onceran an intem-
perate weekly. In pursuance of the sacred duty I owed
the public, I attacked a stout councillor for his attitude
in supporting a reduction of a penny an hour from the
stipends of street sweepers, or something of the kind.
The great man called on me the day after, with fire in his
eye and a loaded walking-stick in his hand. He stormed
for ten minutes, and almost beat the publishing counter
to splinters in his virtuous wrath. “ Attack me 1” he
yelled in conclusion. “ Attack me 1 I’ll show you where
your living comes from 1 Take my son’s advertisement
out of your wretched rag ! Out with it 1 Out with it 1 ”
He repeated “ Out with it 1 ” five times as if he expected
me to set to work and root the advt. out of every copy.
When I looked up the advt. in question it was a one-line
item about hair-wash, worth 6d. a week, and was given
on the express condition that the office “ took it out” in
hair-cuts or shaves.
*<• * •
This month an Inky Wayite—Henri Rochefort, editor
of L’lntransigeant —who has fought more duels than any
other member of the profession, retires for good. Roche-
fort virulently attacked in his newspaper Gambetta,
Oonstans, Jules Ferry, and pretty well every other pro-
minent French politician of the last 30 years, and ex-
changed shots or sword-thrusts with most of them.
* « * *
“Castro” (London): Presented myself to editor of
Strand Magazine t’other day, and allowed him to read a
couple of Australian yarns. He liked them, and offered
me £3 for the pair, 8000 words in all. And this
after a little booming of my work by several big London
papers. At the Press Club, Wine Office Court, Fleet-street,
I was introduced to half-a-dozen bull-necked Australian
writers, full to the lips with eahon-ranche matter, “and
our denied lariat-swinging whelps who whoop along the
valley of the Diamantina on their bronchos.” The literary
agents practically run the magazines. Pett Ridge, Barry
Pain, Hewlett, Percival Gibbon, “Napoleon” Bailey,
and scores of others are all in their hands. And the up-to-
date London editor won’t edit your screed ; it has to be
ready for print. No matter how dull the article or story,
it must have a finished appearance. If you are as bright
as Voltaire, original as Heine, your MS. is returned if it
lacks polish. There’s no market for rough diamonds ;
but there’s an unlimited field for well-faced blue metal.
* * * *
“ 10.2.4.”: Re Bulletin pars (“ F.M.” 5/8/’O7 et alia),
dealing with the troubles of the backblocks editor. I
ran a daily in a rather important town ofN.S.W. once,
and got my share. What got me down and in-
duced nerve crises o’ nights was the casual caller.
The lights of a newspaper office draw the country
drunk like magnets, and the same applies to the
peripatetic village idiot. Was reading proofs one Friday
night: time, 11.30. There came a thundering on the
rickety stairs outside, and presently a large aggressive
man entered. He advanced trailing in his wake an
aroma as of some mighty and recently-deserted brewery.
In his hand he held a small bound volume. “Ami
addressing the Editor ” ? he asked with inebriate courtesy.
I said “Yes.” “I have yere,” he explained, “a book of
my poems for review.” I tried to wave him away, but
he was insistent, and drew a chair near mine. “ These
poems,” he said impressively, “ will create a stir.
Pipe the moniker on the front page.” I piped, but it
conveyed nothing to me. He seemed - disappointed. “Con
over this little sonnet kindly, and give me your judg-
ment on it.” It was headed, “Lines Written on Com-
pleting my Fifth Year of Penal Servitude ’’—which was
quite the most notable thing about it. Before I could
say anything by way of criticism, he had turned over
three or four pages, and spread the book open before me
again at some verses entitled “ My Tenth Year’s Hard, or
A Sufferer’s Plaint.” I started violently, but thought it
better to humor him, and so read the stuff, which struck
me as being plainly written with an inner knowledge,
whatever its literary demerits. I murmured something
of the sort, and was immediately referred to what my
visitor held to be-bus masterpiece. I only read the title,
to wit, “Emotions experienced by me on being
sentenced to Death,” and then rose alertly and
sped downstairs. One of the comps got him into
the street. He was quite sane, as it turned
out, and unpleasantly genuine—a reprieved murderer,
in fact, who had been let out after serving 12 years.
About half-an-hour later, the advance agent of a travel-
ling theatrical co. arrived, and negotiated a violent fit of
delirium tremens in an empty room on the other side of
the passage. It took 20 minutes to dislodge tjiis reveller,
and then the undertaker hurried in, fresh from a “ case ”
at the hospital, sizzling over with his interminable and
irremediable grievance about the printing of his ad., and
reeking hideously of oil of lavender and other death-
suggestive medicaments. And when he had gone I fell-to
once more on my proofs.
* * * *
One of the best inky billets outside America is the
editorship of Allahabad Pioneer (India), which carries, or
used to carry, a screw of £2OOO a year with quarters. A
military man generally fills the chair. The Pioneer is
unlike any other daily this writer knows of, being in the
form of a weekly, say like The Bulletin. It varies in bulk
from 26 to 32 pages, and no military man is seen early at
his club without his Pioneer, which, by the way, is a gold
mine to the proprietary.
* * * *
The proprietor of a Gippsland rag took on himself
the reporting of the local races. When he returned
he passed over his book so that the editor might write
up the meeting, and went out. His notes on the first
four races were capable of being translated by an editor
who was able to bind up the wounds in the King’s Eng-
lish, but the notes on the pony race, as far as decipher-
able, were these simple but eloquent remarks : “ Damned
awful good lot of people here, but course faces wrong
way, too much sun on me head.”

BRONCHITIS AND PLEURISY.
A SEVERE CASE CURED BY TWO BOTTLES OF HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE.
AFTER OTHER TREATMENT HAD FAILED
Mr. Hearne, Chemist, Geelong.
Dear Sir,—Some months ago in Sydney, I suffered from a severe attack of influenza, and was confined to my
room for about a week, at the end of which time, feeling somewhat better, I got up and tried to transact my busi-
ness as usual. But I got up too soon, for the very next day I had a relapse, and suffered tortures from what the
doctor told me was pleurisy and bronchitis. The pain from the former in my chest and shoulders was frightful, and
for four long weeks I was confined to my bed under the care of a well-known Sydney doctor, and all the time his
medicine gave me but temporary relief. The landlady of the hotel (the Cleveland), where I resided, told me of a
medicine—Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure—from Victoria, which had cured her of a bad attack of bronchitis and pains in
the chest, and begged of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks and gratitude to you, tell you that, after the second
bottle, my cough had ceased ; but what is more astonishing, the pains from pleurisy entirely left me, and in about
a week I was able to attend to my duties as usual.—Yours faithfully,
J. BRAHAM, Melbourne Punch Office, Melbourne.

A SEVEN TEARS’ CASE.
EXPECTORATING BLOOD AND MATTER.—COMPLETELY CURED.
Mr. W. G. Hearne. Dear Sir, —Your medicine has cured me of bronchitis and asthma from which I had
suffered for upwards of seven years, during which period I was scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the
difficulty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in succession I had to sit up. I write to you this acknow-
ledgment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of the
medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily improved as I continued the treatment, until I became as I am now,
quite well. —Yours sincerely,
H. WALKER, Balmain, Sydney.

ing your advertisement in some Tasmanian papers brought it to my memory. I told my husband, and he got your
cure for me, which I have taken with completely satisfactory result.—Yours respectfully,
W. McCO.VlßE,*Mosgiel, New Zealand,

BEWARE OF IMITATIONS! The great success of HEABNE’S BRONCHITIS CURB has induced a number of
unprincipled persons to make imitations, each calling his medicine “ Bronchitis Cure,” with the object of deceiving the simple-
minded, and So getting a sale for an imitation which has none of the beneficial effects that BEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURB
lias. Consequently it has become necessary to draw your attention to this fact, and to request you in your own interests to be
particular to ask for HE ARNE’S and see that you get it.

“ J G ” • Crows are the bane of the
, . i
cocky s wife out on tho edge of the plain coun y.
They watch and listen for the hens to cackle, after
laying an egg in some foolish public place; then
the sooty thief swoops down and has a cheap egg.
On my farm the egg-stealing crows used to come
in scores Tried to poison them by shoving
arsenic in a lump of meat, but it only resulted in
slaying about a dozen fowls and two or three cats,
Tried shooting the crows; spent about £1 in car-
tridges, and shot six birds. Then got two rabbit
traps, fastened lumps of meat on the plates, and
set the traps on top of two posts. Half-an-hour
afterwards heard an infernal row outside. Went
out, and discovered the Missus’s prize Leghorn
rooster caught m one of the traps. Let him go, but
ga j ( j nu ffi n> Fixed up higher posts to support the
tra p St and-soon had a couple of crows caught,
Dealt it out to them, and re-set the traps, and
caught two more. Before the day was over, had
caught 13. The remaining crows then saw that
things were not as they ought to be and cleared
out, and I never saw a crow about the place for
about three months. After that, they started to
come again. Got traps to work once more, and
caught a crow by the claws. Took him out alive,
and dipped him in a solution of red ochre that
happened to be handy, and let him go. When he
made for the nearest tree every living crow that
was within sight took after him. The last I saw
of him he was about five miles away, and chased
by about 500 crows. Not a blessed crow came
near that farm all the rest of the time I
remained there, and that was about 12 months,
Don’t know what happened to the red crow, but
probably he is going yet.
“S.T.S.” : As toMurwillumbah, deceased
-.nr, - • ,
(B. 10/10/07). Why is it so difficult to guess
where the accent falls in Australian names ?
(Critics who drag in English anomalies like
“ Wooster ” and “ Berriek ” are warned off; those
things have nothing to do with this matter.) I
have a brother-in-law who lived there for years,
and the family spoke of his postal address, always
and undoubtingly, as Murwillwmbah, vid Tum-
bulgum. When he at last came along to pro-

A mother’s uppermost thought and care is for the health, safety and well-being of her babe. It is but

to various ailments, principally affections of the skin and scalp,, such as chafing, irritation, rash, milk crust, scald
head, etc. The risk of the child being affected with these troubles is ever present, hence special care must be
shown to prevent them. A most important factor in the health of the child is its bath, and every care should
be taken to secure a soap suitable for its delicate immatured skin. The use of common toilet soaps tend to set up skin
irritation, which, in many cases, have resulted in skin affections of various painful forms. It is in the nursery Zam-Buk
Medicinal Toilet Soap fills a long-felt want.
ZAM-BUK MEDICINAL TOILET SOAP

skin of the adult. It has the same result on each, viz., it soothes, softens, and gives the

natural oil in the pores that, through ignorance in the use of common soaps containing
injurious acids and fats, has been allowed to drain away. For toilet purposes, Zam-Buk

healthy. That is just what you want. To keep the skin as nature intended, and assist in
the prevention of the many skin affections that occur, Zam-Buk Soap should be in regular use.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The N.S.W. orchardist, more especially the
orange-grower, whose ground is littered with fallen fruit,
owing to the ravages of the fruit fly, wears a worried
look nowadays. The Act decrees the burning of infected
fruit, and as oranges are about as inflammable as a
cataract or one of Neild’s speeches, their destruction
takes some thinking over. One Pymble (N.S.W.) man
has enlisted the domestic duck to carry on the campaign.
In pursuit of the early worm, the strenuous waddler
attacks the infected spot, and makes a clean sweep of the
pulp, leaving only the shell, which soon gets dry enough
to burn.
* * * *
Sussex-street, Sydney, the lurk of the whole-
sale produce dealers who raised a howl “ in the interests
of the poor producer” against the Federal forage and
fodder duties, thought it saw a chance during the coal
strike. The shortage of .coal threatened a disorganisation
of the Interstate 1 shipping trade, so the big men in the
street started to corner the market in all the leading
lines of produce, with the view of unloading on the
consumer at exorbitant rates. Happily, the strike did
not last long enough, and it is good to know that some of
the crowd burnt their fingers.
Butterine is now being made in Sydney to the
value of £500,000 per annum. So perfect" has its manu-
facture become that, as far as appearance goes, it cannot
be distinguished from good butter, and in taste is said to
be superior to many cheap butters. Its chief market
is found in shipping circles. It keeps better than
cheap butter, and is considered quite good enough for the
forecastle. A lot of it is sold in the cheap grocer’s shop
as butter. It is labelled “ Factory Made.” Butterine is
mainly beef suet and is wholesome enough, and if it is sold
openly as butterine, and does not masquerade as butter,
there is no valid reason to object to it. But its success
has seriously alarmed that section of the butter trade
which deals largely with inferior grade butter, and
some people are threatening to ask for legislation.
* * * ' »
The Melbourne shark finds lots of ways of earn-
ing a dishonest “ quid ” on Cup day. One smarty spotted
a buggy standing outside a city hotel. Simultaneously, a
couple of simple sailor men rolled casually along. His
brain worked swiftly. Jumping into the buggy, he sang
out, offering to drive the two round the city for the after-
noon for “ half-a-quid a head.” They closed with the
offer, and the three had a pleasant excursion. The police
had been informed, and various Johns, recognising the
turnout, tried to capture it, but the horse was fleet, and
its driver was cunning. Towards evening the smarty,
having collected his fares, strolled casually into a hotel
and left by the back way ; and the police seized the two
mariners and deposited them in the lock-up. They got
off next day, but they had a hard job to make the Bench
believe their simple tale.
* * * *
One of the best assets the Victorian landholder
Up North has just now is the dead timber on his holding.
Firewood is becoming so scarce that 7s. 6d. a ton, for sft.
lengths, is charged the water trust pumping stations
to-day, as against 3s. a few years ago.
“ Wyben ” : The alleged working-out of the
Torres Straits pearling beds is mostly myth. October
witnessed a catch of shell which established a record in
the history of the industry. One boat brought in
5 tons 6cwt. of mother-of-pearl for twelve weeks’ work.
The previous best catch was under 4 tons. The real
trouble at Thursday Island is that the Japs practically
monopolise the industry, and do not buy goods from
white traders, but send all the profits from the industry
to Nippon.
* * * *
“Wyben”: Said that James Clark and Co.,
whose pearling fleets were “driven ” out of Torres Straits
by the White Australia policy, are now dissatisfied with
working at the Aroe Islands under the Dutch flag, and
have been looking longingly at Philippine waters. But
that scheme has gone wrong, for the American authori-
ties have passed an order-in-council, directing that pearl-
ing luggers in Philippine waters must be” owned by
Americans and built in Manila.
* * * *
A Washington (IT.S.) consular report states
with alarm that Australian meat-packers are cornering
the enormous Jewish demand in England, by having
whole consignments “koshered” in due form before
shipping, the seal only being broken by a Jewish official
in London. Satisfactory to hear of Australia out-
hustling America in one direction.
* * dt 0
The exceeding regularity of the supply of rats
begins to give Melbourne City Council a suspicion that
somebody is breeding the beasts for the bonus of 2d. a
head. There is no drought or flood about this crop ; it
keeps steady at from 1100 to 1200 per fortnight, so, if
anybody has started a new primary industry, he is doing
fairly well at it.
* * * *
One of the latest unions registered in N.S.W.
is the —ahem—Sausage Case-Makers’ Union.
In promising his assistance to a deputation of
municipal officers, Premier Bent assured them they need
adduce no arguments to back up their requests. “ I
know a gentleman,” he said, “ who acts as shire secretary,
valuer, rate collector, dog registrar, gasworks secretary,
and cemetery secretary. He is a highly-qualified man,
and he is paid £lBO a year after 30 years’ service.” If
these landowners and their parasites sweat like this when
dealing in public with public money, what sort of slavery
will they practise behind their own private fences when
their own money is at stake ?
Queensland lately sent off an experimental lot
of frozen turtles, and got buyers for them in London at
prices said to range up to £6 each. One of the French
municipal visitors was so taken by them that he bought
one, and had it sent to Paris. About 35 years ago the
manager of the Lake’s Creek meat works tried tinning
turtle, and offered £1 per cwt. for all he could get over
lcwt. All the little coasting craft about the Fitzroy and
Port Curtis had a shot at the trade, and some sensational
cheques were made while it lasted. One little 25-ton
ketch, with three hands on hoard, made £9O in a fort-
night, and though she knocked off her false keel among
the reefs of the Bunker Group, it paid handsomely. The
turtle in those days were caught at night when they
went ashore to bury their eggs in the sand.
* * * *
Adelaide is suffering from an overdose of lamb.
The recent hot, dr.v spell burnt up the northern grass,
consequently graziers fired Mary’s little animal into
Adelaide as quickly as they could arrange for transport.
The Produce Dep6t accommodation, which is barely up
to normal requirements, was insufficient for the sudden
rush, so Adelaide has been fed up on the surplus until it
begins to bleat piteously, and tbe bleat of the boarder is
mingled with the bleat of the exporters, who beg the
Government to provide a larger depot. These exporters
are mostly Fat gentlemen, but, for the moment, they
have forgotten that State Socialism is a horrible thing,
with a special “set” on marriage ties. Meanwhile, the
restaurants are crowded with pale young men asking
firmly for pork chops.
* * * *
“Sr”: Why will Australian people persist in
using iron for roofing houses? We are ridiculous in our
dress, and we dress up our houses just as ridiculously by
putting on an iron hat to keep off the heat of an Aus-
tralian sun. A few Melbourne suburban houses are using
shingles, and they make the most satisfactory and sensible
roof I know. They have a far better appearance than
tiles and are more waterproof, and they are cheaper than
iron. Yet we live in ovens, or under flaring red roofs,
instead of under a characteristic Australian roof of
shingles. Objection may be made to shingles on account
of fire, but so little do the insurance cos. think of this
that the rate is but 6d. per hundred extra.
“Hamer” : When pure foods are insisted on,
the Cheap Jack will want a lot of attention. He is bad
enough when he deals in superfluous oddments, but when
he sells groceries he is a horror. One Cheap John, to my
knowledge, bought up the sweepings of flour mills and
disposed of the mixture as flour. There is nothing that
an economical person like that will not handle. When he
can’t sell his horrors separately he makes them up into
“ lucky bundles ” —say, a tin of condensed milk, a dozen
matches, a pound of flour, and three lead pencils, for six-
pence. The condensed milk sold under such conditions
shrieks for analysis.
« * * *
In some respects even the Foreign-trade
Britisher is more patriotic than the Australian. A con-
signment of Kameruka (N.S.W.) cheese, lately sent to
England, is reported to be the best ever received there
from any “ colony,” Canada included. It brought from
635. to 655. per cwt., while Canadian and Maoriland goods
brought 58s. to 60s. But, “the agents had great diffi-
culty in selling the cheese to the retailers as Australian
Cheddar ; if they could have called it English Cheddar the
very highest price would have been realised.” So, in
England, the people prefer their own goods ; but with
many Australians any old thing will do, as long as it is
not Australian.
* * * *
“X.”: A clothing manufacturer has told me
his experience of the run-’em-up trade, which he
has only recently entered. No matter how carefully he
worked it, he found that he was invariably under-cut on
contracts by his trade rivals. His plant and methods
were equal to theirs. He paid his girls as little as
they did, and as little as he dared. Yet he was
always 3d. to Is. outside the rest of the trade.
After some cogitation he “dropped.” The next time a
big contract came his way he specified the number of
skirts or other clo’ he would undertake to return out of
so much cloth, and this proved to be half-a-dozen more
than previous contractors had supplied for the same
quantity of material. Their profit had consisted in this
half-dozen finished clo’ which they retained out of the
material. Now business is booming with that run-’em-up
man, and he wears a smile the size of a railway arch.
The talk of the chance group turner! on the
chagrin of missing the ’bus during business hours. It
was the auctioneer’s turn. He was, he explained, at
Maitland (N.S.W.), once offering a lucerne farm under
instructions to sell with a reserve, and the reserve was
high enough to fill him with doubt. So he lavished his
finest arts on the property, and told of the vast things it
would do if it were better improved and more thoroughly
supervised. And as he finished and waited for the bids he
felt rather proud of his efforts. He felt better still when
the bidding began. There were bids in all directions.
The reserve price had been well passed when the
owner came up to him, with the craving for wealth in his
eye, and whispered hoarsely : “ I won’t sell at all. I never
properly saw the value of the farm before. You put it
grand, man. I can make all those improvements. I’ll
keep it ! ” So the vehicle drifted round the corner, as the
auctioneer turned away with coarse language in his
mouth.
■* * » *
“ Quan ” : Queensland pineapple canning fac-
tories either throw away the refuse from the fruit or feed
pigs with it. Now, however, somebody suggests cider.
Interesting experiments have already been carried out,
under the direction of the Agricultural Department,
at Toowoomba, with 3cwt. of pineapples. Being some-
what dry, they yielded only three gallons of juice, out of
which about 90 gallons of “ grade 2 ” cider were made.
It is claimed that, with a pulping machine of a-ton-an-
hour capacity (which could be made for about £8), six
grades of cider can be manufactured. John Cashin, a
fruit-grower of the Tweed River (N.S.W.), used to make
pineapple cider, and a Tumbulgum publican used to sell
it. I was persuaded to try it one hot day instead of beer.
Liked it so well that I had some more, and I went on
having some more until I had to leave for a district
which knew not John Cashin or his juice.
**■*-*
“Stony” : If a man isn’t a thinker, his politics
can mostly be told by the time he strikes the city in the
morning. The fat-souled person who gets the train at
10 a.m., and only sees other fat-souled people lazing into
town, falls into a bloated idea that there isn’t any suffer-
ing and poor clothing and scanty feeding in the world,
and that the hard-faced, starvtd people simply don’t
exist, except in quantities that are Scripturally reason-
able. But any time between 7and 8 o'clock of the morning
is the time to see the poor. Then they come out of their
burrows in droves, Girls in millions ! Trains full of
them ! Lean and lank, and mottle-faced, with a ha’pprth
of lunch in a scrap of paper or a tin, all bent on getting to
work in time, for fear of a fine or a brutal sneer front the
petty boss. Most of them are dressed in tuppenny
replicas of last year’s fashions, and (more power to their
game spirit!) flaunting some feeble attempts at gaiety in
their costumes. But hard-up—thousands of them ! Then
at 9 o’clock and later, the more prosperous office-people
rush in (lean enough, too, a good proportion of these);
and, still later, the person who bellows loud untruths
about Guv’ment interference with Private Enterprise—
the person with the greasy soul who hardly knows there’s
a skimpily-clad shop-girl or a poorly-fed factory-hand in
the city. Nor cares—except when someone sticks Up for
the under dog ; and then he knows the last word on the
subject.
* * * *
The “backbone of the country,” despite its
horror of Socialism, likes a good, strong, Socialistic post
to lean up against, and when it finds one to its liking it
wants a heap of persuading thUEt the post isn’t its own
private property. During the drought, in 1902, the
Queensland Government purchased a large quantity of
seed wheat in S. Australia, and passed it on to the
farmers, on condition that payment for it should be made
when the crops were reaped. That’s five years ago, and
though there have been several good seasons since, the
amount owing a few weeks ago was £ll,OOO. A recent
circular to the debtors brought in only £lOOO.
* * * *
The new commercial morality has spread to
Ceylon. From one of the newspapers of that balmy
island the London Daily News takes the following
rYH. A. HORSE—rising 7, fine mouth and paces,
about 15 hands; fine Lady’s Hack; shows a lot
of breeding. Reason for selling: bad with motors;
won’t go in harness; jibs when leaving stables;
catches rider by seat of breeches when mounting ; but
a darling pet.—Apply, etc.
THE SECOND CHRIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As I go to my work of a morning
I think of God’s ruined plan,
How the iron heel of Progress
Has deadened the soul of man.
Men crowd to the city with me,
All smileless and stern and cold.
The shrine of their hearts —an office ;
The God of their seeking—gold.
I would rather have been a rover
By some grey ocean’s shore,
Mating with men and women
In the red Cave Days "of Yore,
Than a son of the bloodless present,
A child of these pulseless years,
When gold and silver and copper
Are more than laughter and tears.
We hustle and cheat and barter,
Not caring for wrong or right,
And hunt all day in the Market,
And count our gains by night.
We heed no light of sunset,
No song of field or wood.
The swine are housed within us,
The wolf is in our blood.
The maiden buys a lover,
For love is but a jest,
With book and ring and candle
The sale of hearts is blessed.
I hate the human market,
Where lust is lord of love,
And Soul is trodden under,
And Self is throned above.
I long to see a rider,
Foam on his horse’s flanks,
Charge through the hungry hucksters,
And break their sordid ranks,
And rise upon his stirrups,
And cry, while they stand dumb,
“ A Second Christ is needed,
And a Second Christ has come.”
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.


MICK SIMMONS
HaymarKet, Sydney, N.vS.W.

The Song of the Axe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS is the song of the driving axe to the
woods ere the bright-browed inorn.
Sweet from the laver of sunrise, stept,
light-foot, o’er the mountain horn ;
Sung ere the sullen mists climb up to lie in the
heart of the Sun :
“Make way, make way, on the hills to-day, your
pride is past and done.”
There is a cry in the mighty masts that have dared
the hate of years,
The thunder-flame, the whirling storm, the winds
of the hemispheres ;
Ah, bitter the doom when the axe bites deep, and
the lean tops waver slow,
Once more, once more, till the legions roar head-
down to the depths below.
They bowed the head to the still, small voice of
the slayer come up to kill,
Who mocked the thresh of the frozen gales and
stood in glory still ;
(Majesty born of the sun was theirs, of the mist,
and the golden days ;
But who may stand when the gods command and
the wild men work their ways !
Shoulder to shoulder, and rank on rank, the
green-plumed people tost
Their crests to the breeze of the yellow dawn
that glittered across the frost;
Midnight had brewed them heartful draughts,
and fearless and fair they hung,
And challenged the little men murm’rously, and
laughed till the axes swung.
The invaders came in the dewy dawn, and spoke
to the listening wood,
And fear crept into the towering trunks, and
shook them as they stood.
They whispered the tale, each unto each, till
blown of the wind it crept,
A last long sigh, to the shining sky, and the
jewelled bowers wept.
The brown birds woke to the clang below, and
fled in wild despite
To drink the breath of the honeyed morn, and
wheel in the glory-light;
And the axes chimed on the earth below, their
song to the shoulder stroke,
Till a green lane yawned tumultuously, and the
mist went by like smoke.
(So the loved of the gods, the human great, who
have hardily fought and won—
Their feet in the furrows of earth, their brows in
the blaze of a giddy sun :
The glamor of fame is theirs, when lo ! a grim
iconoclast;
Time, blind and bent, with fierce intent, smites
home, and they fall at last.)
This is the song of the driving axe ere the new
day’s hot desire
Swims up the chaste, pearl Orient in a soft, smooth
flood of fire :
“Make way, make way, ye little trees, ye arro-
gant woods, make way !
We are the heralds before the host, the Slayers
come up to slay ! ”
Axe, and fire, and smoking nights—such is the
sorrowful tale,
When the flocks, and herds, and caravans turn
over the sunlight trail,
And brave-browed women of quiet step, and
speech, and toil-worn hands—
Make way ; to-morrow the heirs of men are here
for the mountain lands.
The axes sang to the creaking woods, and slew
them as they went,
The green-plumed legions reared and roared ere
yet their pride was spent.
No more the winds in their arms shall dream—
man works his subtle plans,
And rocking slow on the plains below steal out
his caravans.
MAORI LAND.
C. ALLAN MARRIS.
By Rail and Semaphore.
THE SPECIAL RATES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LIKE most other manufacturing centres of
Maoriland, Rangihape was blessed with 3
variety of special designed tQ foster
industries. Raw material for the factories
was carried at a ridiculously low tariff, and the
freight on manufactured articles to Romahapa was
less than half that on the same class of goods
from Romahapa to Rangihape. Also, coal from
the Rangihape mines was allowed a concession in
freight to enable it to compete in the Romahapa
factories with the Newcastle article with which
the potato ships used to fill up for the return
journey.
All these things encouraged local industry, and
increased the output of blasphemy whenever a
new sub-stationmaster arrived at Rangihape. The
usual rule was for the old man to leave by the first
train after the arrival of his successor, but at
Rangihape and Burning Plains he usually stayed
for a couple of days to guide the newcomer
through the wilderness of local rates. But when
Jones went down to replace McGregor, the man
with the Highland name was in a sick bed, and,
as usual, the man who knew least about the local
rates was the local stationmaster.
Jones wa§ famous for his flow of language. He
Wely Vised objectionable words, and his
ings took the form of loud questions, addressed to
the Almighty, as to what He meant by it, and
statements to the effect that he had always given
the Almighty his moral support and expected
better treatment in return.
But he was a man who had the knack of grasp-
ing intricate things, and by the time he had been
at Rangihape 24 hours he was master of the
system. It had been designed hy old Hambling,
the great bald-faced Scotchman who held the
position of Assistant-General Manager, and who
was logical in all things. So, once [ones had
found the key, the mystery was simplicity itself.
Then Macphairson happened along.
• * * «
Macphairson was member for the district, and
maintained his position by wearing kilts and care-
fully spreading the impression that he was the
titular head of the clan. No Scotsman would
stand against him —it would have been treason ;
and when an Englishman from Canterbury did
oppose him, that misguided individual lost his
• deposit. All around Rangihape Macphairson was
very solid.
But as the years wore on and coal mines
opened up at Stirling, a few miles from Rangi-
hape and in the same electorate, there came an in-
flux of Welsh, Cornish, and Durham miners, who
gibed at kilts. Some of these men eventually went
on the land, others drifted into other industries,
and their children were numerous, and taught the
Highland bairns that kilts were ridiculous. Stir-
ling became a manufacturing centre, too, on
account of the close proximity of the coal, and the
local people howled for the benefit of the special
rates from Rangihape till they eventually got them.
Then the new settlement started to overhaul the
older centre rapidly, and as Rangihape had
always voted for Macphairson, Stirling went
against him on general principles. But the
Scotch proprietors around Rangihape held the
balance of power, and they stuck to kilts.
Macphairson was never really anxious about his
seat till Duncan Mackinnon announced his candi-
dature. Mackinnon was a formidable rival. He
was a Scotsman. He announced that Macphair-
son’s claim to be titular head of the clan was
bunkum. Also, he hurled dirt at the clan gener-
ally, and talked about being a Maorilander.
The younger generation was enthusiastically for
Mackinnon, though the old men stuck to Mac-
phairson ; and in that bracing climate the
old fools lived long. Realising . that he
was safe in the older districts, Macphairson
determined to carry the war into the new ones,
and he raged up and down in a startling manner.
Everywhere he was listened to with attention, and
when he had finished speaking the meetings carried
resolutions declaring that he had thrown no light
on the political situation and had given no evidence
of having done anything for the district that it
should re-elect him. At one meeting a resolution
disapproving of kilts was lost by a single vote.
This sort of warfare broke the Macphairson up.
It was necessary that he should claim credit for
something. The question was what the Devil
there was to claim credit for. Then he suddenly
thought of the local rates. Here was an argument
why he should be re-elected. He rushed down
to stationmaster Creedon, borrowed a copy of the
railway tariff, and took it home to study it.
It was after dinner when he opened it, and he
was feeling good. When he had glared at it for
two solid hours without understanding anything,
he felt rather bad. It was all Greek, and his
head ached ; he felt exasperated and disappointed.
He went to Creedon’s house, and asked him to
explain. Creedon couldn’t.
He went over to the station-office next morning,
about 11 a.m. He was wearing a kilt, a sporran,
plaid, and all the rest of it, and his journey to the
office was a sort of triumphal progress, punctu-
ated by whiskies at the pubs along the line of
route. By the time he arrived at the railway
office he was in a state of exaltation and about as
aggressive as a hungry bulldog. He strode into
the office after kicking open the door, and Jones
sprang up from the telegraph key with an oath.
“ Dinna swear, or she’ll catch nae feesh ! ”
said Macphairson. “ See, laddie, ye’ll gang
hame tae your supper early th’ nicht, an’ then
gang doon tae ma place. Get there aboot a
quarter tae eight. Ah hae worruk for ye. ”
He strode out without waiting for a reply.
Jones was a public servant, and the Macphairson
was the public. A few minutes before he went
to tea, old Creedon reminded Jones that he had to
go to Mr. Macphairson’s. Jones rose angrily.
J‘ Do you mean to say that it is your instruction
I should go to this fellow’s house to-night ? ” he
inquired with a snort.
<l I do ; an’ please be veera respectfu’ to him.
He is member for the district and one of our
employers. ”
Jones was too full for speech ; but after the
S. M. had departed, Jones made severe remarks to
his Creator, and finally demanded an explanation
as to why he was placed in such a world wdthout
being consulted.
Receiving no reply, he began to wonder what
he was wanted for. He picked up the paper and
glanced down the announcements of the Mac-
phairson’s speeches, and found he was to hold
forth at Stirling the following evening on the
subject of local rates and the great railway con-
cessions he had secured for the district. And
Jones smiled a dreadful smile.
He arrived at the Macphairson mansion a few
minutes after eight. The Macphairson abused
him for being late, and then explained that he
required a proper analysis of the local rates, so
that the public could be made to understand what
he had done for the district. Jones mentioned
that he had a slight cold, and that whisky and
honey was a specific, Macphairson said he had
heard that before, and suggested first of all that
Jones should make out a complete list of the local
rates, and then write out a short review of the
effect of each and how it operated to the benefit
of the district.
Jones drew a flask from his pocket and asked
the Macphairson if he had any glasses. He inci-
dentally expressed a hope that Mr. Macphairson
had a mouth, and the tone in which he said it
suggested that the lack of evidence made him
doubt it.

KIDNEY TROUBLE affects PEOPLE DIFFERENTLY
Though no two persons alike are
affected in precisely the same way by
kidney disorder, the danger is always
equally great. One man may suffer
from pains in the back and loins, or
from rheumatism —another may have
urinary troubles, and his eyes may be
puffy and swollen; one woman may
notice heart irregularity, she may be
sleepless, nervous, and despondent —
her neighbour may have all the
symptoms of dropsy or Bright’s disease.
It depends on the sufferer’s constitution
and the nature of the impurities kept
back in the body by the blood filters—
the kidneys.

inflammation; they drive out the uric
acid that irritates the kidney mem-
branes and clogs the passages. Doan’s
Pills help the kidneys in their great
blood-filtering work, and give tone
to the whole lddney organism.
