Macphairson brought glasses with alacrity, and
drank heartily.
Then Jones took off his coat, and wrote steadily
for two hours. After that he handed the sheets
to Macphairson to read. Mac. read, and became
excited. He said it was “veera plain, veera
simple ! ” Jones advised him to read the state-
ment to the public and not to try too much
explanation, as the subject was a difficult one.
Macphairson said he would, drank what was left
of Jones’s whisky, and then saw him off the
premises.
This conduct was not due to any meanness on
the part of Macphairson, who, to his peers and
electors, was hospitality itself. But Jones was a
public servant, and being a stranger on a relieving
trip only, had no vote. That was all.
• * * *
The meeting at Stirling was a crowded one.
Macphairson held forth for an hour and a half.
Then he produced his summary, and read it out.
A titter ran through the building, and dazed-
looking men were busy taking notes. There was
some interruption, which the Mackinnonites
crushed with great suddenness. They wanted
the Macphairson to have plenty of rope, and he
got it. He also hung himself.
When he had finished his speech, the manager
of the Stirling Enterprise Coal Mining Co. arose and
made a few remarks. He produced a copy of the
tariff, and pointed out that the Macphairson’s rates
were all wrong, and his deductions about what he
had done for the district consequently imaginary.
The rates he had quoted for coal really applied to
hay ; and the pig-iron rate, which he had said was
a reduction secured by him with great difficulty,
was actually the freight charge on corned beef
when sent in large quantities. Also the alleged
concession on steel rails in quantities above eight
tons didn’t apply to steel rails at all ; it was the
concession allowed on passenger rates when eight
preachers were travelling together to attend a
church conference.
It was quite evident that a man who did not
even know what the local rates were had nothing
to do with the obtaining of them. He added that
he didn’t believe Macphairson was the head of his
clan at all, and he said he regarded kilts as a relic
of barbarism.
Others followed in the same strain. In the
middle of it all, the candidate suddenly sprang
from the platform and rushed after the retiring
reporters to ask them to suppress all accounts of
the meeting. Then he re-entered the hall by the
front door and made a wild spring at the throat of
a tall young man in a knickerbocker suit, and was
met by a straight left, that laid him out for five
seconds. Foaming with fury, he arose and once
more attacked the stranger, and this time suc-
ceeded in getting a grip of his throat.
When the excited audience hauled him off,
Jones was nearly throttled. But the crowd chased
Macphairson to the railway station, and threw
mud on his kilts, and Macphairson, when he
arrived at the station, threw coal at the crowd.
And when the train came in, he got on the
carriage platform and challenged all and sundry.
Mackinnon was elected.
C. A. JEFFRIES.
The Soul-Feeders.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE may be married and she may be single,
or she may be married and he may be
single, or they both may be married ; but
they are never, never married to each other.
She may begin the soul-feeding by saying he
is too nice or too young to be married, or he may
begin it by saying : “ Are you really married ? I
shouldn’t have thought so.” “Why?” “Oh,
well, you look too young and untroubled —er, any-
way, I’m sorry.” Or again : “ Are you really mar-
ried, and have you been able to decide yourself as to
the question, ‘ls Marriage a P'ailure?”’ If she
is all on for a nourished soul, she looks sadly or
dreamily, or whichever expression most suits her
style of beauty, and, dropping her voice to the
right pitch, says : “ Does any woman ever find
marriage anything else but a failure ? ” or some-
thing equally pensively effective. Or again, he
may tell her that his wife doesn’t “ understand ”
him ; she starves his soul, and so he comes to her
to have it fed.
There is plenty for them to talk about, poetry
and the arts, sunsets, moonrisings, cloud effects,
motors, and “ shoes and ships and sealing-wax ” ;
but not necessarily Love. This is what makes
soul-feeding so intensely alluring. It is treading
on dangerous ground, and then steering off again.
Even old men grow quite skittish and romantic
over the business. When they couldn’t walk into
the next room to get something for their wives,
because of a twinge of gout or a rheumatic toe,
they can trot along miles and miles of country
lanes and sandy beaches with their soulful She.
They are so many, and they all look so happy.
Is it the knowledge that they will not marry each
other that gives them the happy security in feeling
that they cannot be disillusionised ?
The Soul-feederess is generally more in earnest
than He is ; she it is who arranges the outings,
and brings the basket —for the soul-feeders do not
exist entirely on soul. She does not wait to be
wooed. If she did, she probably wouldn’t be at
all. She has to keep him interested by a variety
of novel inventions, or he would soon “fade”
away through an insufficiency of soul (or other)
nourishment. She works hard to gain his in-
terest ; it means so much more to the woman ; the
man thinks he always has her interest.
If she mentions her feeder to you, she says they
are “ only chums,” and they have “ lots of things
in common,” and “ it is such rubbish to say a man
and a woman cannot be chums without anything
else”; besides “he is my husband’s oldest
friend”; or he says she is his wife’s “dearest
friend,” etc., etc. Sometimes “ we are cousins.”
Looks are not of importance to the soul-feeders ;
only souls “ rule the court, the camp, the grove.”
What matters it if his legs are crooked, or that his
ears stand out or down ? Or if her figure is bad
or her face indifferent ? They see beyond the out-
ward semblance to the bright, glowing soul within,
pining for its affinity soul.
If you take a tram to La Perouse, and strike
out along lonely fields and dales, you come across
them ; if you go to Manly and walk miles over
the cliffs, you find them, taken up with their
pretty, high-souled talk, or bending over the
beloved poetry —Browning for choice. Husband
or wife at home has no place in their holy
thoughts. But they do not go beyond the “ soul ”
stage ; they don’t wish for anything stirring and
tragic and public.
Near our selection there lives a lady of
mature years who has a soul-feeder. Pie has a
wife who, in the first case, was a “very dear
friend of mine.” Well, this lady is charming,
and although her hair a few years back was fairly
silvery, it is now a beautiful and successful bur-
nished golden brown. Her whole appearance is
dainty and fascinating—that is after noon ; nobody
ever sees her before that hour. If I am passing
her sliprails and see the pretty creature in her
garden, arrayed in virgin white, with roses
“ tucked in her belt,” a shady garden hat with
more roses tucked thereon crowning her golden
gleamy locks, trilling a catching air, and giving a
little start of fear w'hen she hears the gate click,
I say to myself, “What ho, the soul-feeder
cometh,” and sure enough ’tis he. This has gone
on, according to the neighbor’s knowledge, for the
last six years, and still he seems attracted, so here
is something in favor of soul-feeding as a staying
game. This wise lady also combines her little
affair with dainty dinners, at which they eat a decent
amount. She works hard to keep youthful look-
ing, half-killing herself with Turkish baths and
other things, and all for what ? —so that she can
hug herself with the thought that she has robbed
another woman of her husband !
The wife of the man who soul-feeds tells her
friends that it is a shame the amount of “ working
back” her husband has to do. She is full of
pity for him. But he is not working back, he is
soul-feeding, though one can hardly say there is
no work in struggling to live up to the ideals of
an hysterical female, and climbing up cliffs, and
trampling over green fields, and gasping through
verses that he doesn’t understand or care two-
pence about. Then there is great sacrifice as well
for the man soul-feeder, for while he is with the
soulful She he has to deny himself many things
dear to the stomach of the ordinary man, for it is
against all the cult of Soulfeedingdom to eat
steak and onions and things like that..
The husband of the woman who soul-feeds wishes
his wife would not lead such a strenuous life ; for,
besides her home duties, she attends daily classes
for cookery, and dressmaking, and millinery, and
anything that will make her a good housewife and
save his pocket. And then she is a great ‘ ‘ reader ”
too, going over every day to the Library to change
the books. A man likes his wife to be a “reader” ;
and then it is so cheap. She is fond of having
lunch in town too, a simple little hobby which he
encourages ; and besides, she can’t get decent
meat in her suburb —another journey to town.
Women who do not desire to soul-feed (believe
me it is an unprofitable and risky game), beware of
the married man who hints at being “ Not under-
stood.” Tell him if you like that you think his
wife is far above him, and you pity her ; he won t
throw out any more feelers. Men, beware of the
woman who hints “ things” against her husband
and his want of soul. Your own wife might be
trying the same game !
M.M.
Over the River.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I RODE beside the river
On a summer afternoon,
Where the sunbeams throb and quiver,
And the river-lilies swoon.
And you dwelt over the river, love,
Where the poplars showed so green
Over the silent river, love,
That seemed so deep between,
Loving a man whose hands were tied,
And who deemed it better that he should ride
Lonely there by the river.
When I rode by the river
Did you hear the bit-bars ring,
Where the river-grasses shiver,
And the lazy rushes swing ?
Was it only the bells in the river, love,
That called as I rode away ?
My heart said, “Over the river, love,
She waits where the poplars sway,
Singing ever a golden song.”
And I heard you, dear, as I rode along
Lonely there by the river.
When I rode by the river
You were so near to me
(Only a shallow river,
Not deep, as it seemed to be).
I heard you over the river, love,
When I was riding past;
And over and through the river, love,
I rode like a thunder-blast.
As the strong hoofs made the water fly
I heard the chattering ripples cry,
“ Love finds a ford through a river.”
When we rode by the river
The miles went flashing past—
The horses sobbed, a-quiver—
For we were riding fast.
And the wedding-bells in the river, love,
Were ringing our bridal tune,
And the willows talked to the river, love,
On that summer afternoon.
And the dragons knelt, who held the ford,
When a queen and a knight with a trusty sword
Rode clattering over the river.
M.L.
